Australian Poet when a volume of verse qoes into its
third edition within six weeks of its first publication, and
zreat encouragement is to be extracted from the fact
that tlie success of the book is entirely justified by the
quality of its contents.”

book published in Australia for very many years.”

“ Degeneration. ”
Nordau’s newest work lias hitherto had no salts
in Australia, for at sight of a publisher demand-
ing a guinea for a volume democratic pockets shut
like an oyster. As the first volume in Heine-
mann’s new Empire Library (ss. Od. posted),
“Degeneration” will get more attention. Two
others of Nordau’s works, “Conventional Lies”
and “Paradoxes,” are to follow it in the same
series ; but these have already become familiar in
the cheap American edition. Not so well known
are Masson's “ Napoleon and the Fair Sex,”
or Walisze.wski’s account of that marvel among
marvellous women—Catherine IT. of Russia.
These spicy redactions of old memoirs have a tang
and gusto which one rarely meets in contemporary
“ cheap literature,” and the success of the reprints
should be signal.
‘' Degeneration ” is in many ways a remarkable
work remarkable for its vigor, its knowledge,
the interest of its theories and the ingenuity with
which they are advocated. Nordau dedicates his
book to Lombroso, who traced the relations
between civilisation’s neurosis and civilisation’s
Crime and insanity. It is Nordau’s own aim to
collect arid classify the effects of nervous degenera-
tion in modem art and literature.
Ho considers the leading schools —mystic, egois-
tic realistic---argues for their degeneracy, and
ill nitrates his arguments by a detailed criticism of
their chief apostles. This survey takes up the
greater part of the-well-packed book. It is per-
formed with great skill, but the author’s fecundity
frequently embarrasses, and he loses lucidity in
the whirl of uncontrollable language. Hence an
apt and well-sustained accusation that he himself
is a graphomaniac—one of the very degenerates
'whom he denounces !
There is a prologue of causes, and 'epilogue of
consequences—a discussion of the manner in which
“ degeneration ” has arrived and the way in
which it will depart—for of its departure through
the gate of the “survival of the fittest” Nordau
is assured.
The text for the author’s whole sermon is Guerin-
sen’s apothegm ; “Genius is a disease of the
nerves” (though, says Nordau, “ science does not
assert that every genius is a lunatic”). The
sermon itself is an encyclopaedia of dissertations
upon physiology, criminology, insanity, hypnot-
ism, literary, musical, and artistic expression—its
modes and significations, and a hundred branches
of science which Nordau cleverly connects with
his radical idea. Wagner, Zola, Ibsen, Tolstoi,
the Pre-Raphaelites, are exhaustively treated.
Nietzsche, Maeterlinck, Verlaine, Walt Whit-
man, Oscar Wilde, and a score more are
less elaborately discussed. Gleanings from
a thousand books are added to the mass—
which, it is not wonderful, leaves an impression
of amorphous crudeness. The whole is more
admirable than convincing'—it has enormous
weight and comparatively small penetrative
power. And Nordan continually strains the
interpretation of his facts. Doubtless, modern
degeneracy being given, it must and does exhibit
itself in modern art and literature. That is
almost an a priori certainty ; but, of Nordau’s
® posteriori proofs, almost as many weaken the
conclusion as enforce it. _ His judgment seems to
drift on the current of his prodigious fertility.
A passage from the explanation of the “ why-
ness ’ of degeneration
[Modern] activities, even the simplest, involve an effort
of the nervous system and a wearing of tissue. Every
line we read or write, every human face we see, every
conversation we carry on, every scene we perceive
through the window of the flying - express, sets in activity
our sensory nerves and our brain centres. Even the
lit tle shocks of railway travelling, not perceived by con-
sciousness, the perpetual noises, and the various sights in
the streets of a large town, our suspense pending the
sequel of progressing events [e.ff, the Dean case !], cost
our brains wear and tear. In the last 50 years the popu-
lation of Europe has not doubled, whereas the sum of its
labors has increased tenfold, in part even fiftyfold. Every
civilised man furnishes, at the present time, from five to
25 times as much work as v. as demanded of him half a
century ag'o.
And this, according to Nordau, will be deg’enera-
tion s outcome :
The hysteria of the present day will not last. People
will recover from their present fatigue. The feeble, the
degenerate, will perish ; the strong will adapt themselves
to the acquisitions of civilisation, or will subordinate
them to their own organic capacity. The aberrations of
art have no future. They will disappear when civilised
humanity shall have triumphed over its exhausted con-
chtion. The art of the twentieth century will connect
itself at every point with the past, but it will have anew
task to accomplish—that of introducing* a stimulatin<>'
i anetj into the uniformity of civilised life, an influence
which probably science alone will be in a position to exert,
many centuries later, over the great majority of mankind.
Few books published have held such a wealth of
ideas and facts for alert minds on the literary
scientific plane : there is dynamic suggestion and
stimulus on every page.

R.R.: “S.M. Herald, in its “Literary Gossip ”
quotes the first stanza of Rudyard Kipling’s
limes poem with the comment: “It consists of
14 verses, all in Mr. Kipling’s most characteristic
vein. This is the stanza quoted :
We’ve drunk to the Queen, God bless her,
We’ve chunk to our mothers’ land ;
We’ve drunk to our English brother
(But he does not understand) ;
We’ve drunk to the whole creation,
And the cross swings low to the dawn
Last toast, and of obligation :
We’ve drunk to the native-born !
What can be said of the acumen of a critic who
declares a stanza like this to be in the “most
characteristic vein ” of a verse-maker who, as a
rule, is an exceptionally dexterous craftsman as
regards rhythm and rhyme. The first quatrain
is distinctly poor, while the second contains
the atrocious cockney rhyme of “dawn” and
born, and a line “ Last toast, arid of obliga-
tion," so limping and unmusical that it barely
scans. The truth is that the verse is about as
feeble a one as Kipling ever wrote, and the whole
poem, rhe Native Rom, is below his record. ”
[All the same, Kipling “gets thar.” He isn’t
as academically correct as he anight be ; but look
at the swing and fire of the verse, the wonderfully
vivid epithets (despite errors), the glamor of the
imagery. Isn't this good for a man who never
saw Australia?
To the hush of the breathless morning
On the thin, tin, crackling roofs,
To the haze of the burned back-ranges
And the drum of the shoeless hoofs—
To the risk of a death by drowning,
To the risk of a death by drouth—
To the men of a million acres,
To the sons of the Golden South.
That stirs and sticks. But your side is forcibly
put by a National Observer "parodist :
We’ve drunk, too, to Rudyard Kipling,
e’ve drunk at our Kip.’s command,
We’ve drunk to this, that, and the other, ’
To more t han we understand !
We’ve drunk to the things he bids us,
To the moon and the stars and the dawn—
Last toast, and a rhyme that’s cockney—
A health to the Native-bawn !
He charged us charge our glasses
(For this pun hie charge is free !)
To some Four Mysterious Peoples,
To the islands in some sea
To the last least hr up of mud-flat,
Where none may stand or slide ; :
We did it—wc toasted each sand-bank,
For vie had no darned false pride.
Three “ hies ” to the Native-born (shtan’ up !)
We’re six tight men arow ;
We don’ wanter fi’ for the lil thinnsh we shing about—
We don’ care a cuss for the lil thingsh we shing about—
We’re more comfble here below 1
Goo ni’, good ol’ Kipple (shake hands !)

G. H. Davies, of M.L. Native Department,
points out that the Maori name for the snn, “ Ra,”
is identical with the ancient Egyptian. Has any-
body ever broached the theory that the Harvard
University “ Rah I ” English “Hurrah!” are
relics .of times when our smi-worshipping
ancestors used to shout unanimously in praise of
the great God of Life and Light ?
Angus and Robertson rightly say that “Banjo’s”
book makes an admirable Christmas gift: it is the
characteristic book of Australian verse for friends
at a distance. Brunton Stephens’ praise is all
deserved.
Recent Publications.
“ Phil May’s Winter Annual ” (Is 3d) maintains
a good average quality of art and literature. This
year the titeraturo is the more interesting—Phil
is always Phil, but one can’t easily find humorous
scones for half-a-hundred sketches!
The first number of Chapman’s Story Series (2s
lOd, 3s lid) collects the prize detective stories
which recently appeared in Chapman’s Magazine.
The yarns are good, but they came decidedly
cheaper in the M'agazme.
W. Pett Ridge’s “ Minor Dialogues ” (3s, 4s)
shine with real humor. They are better taken in
nstahnents, maybe ; but not one of the dialoguo-
Agwigs—Anstey, Anthony Hope, Violet Hunt—
is more entertaining for a space. And the char-
acters are real, too —common objects of the London
shop-rows—flies classified _ and fixed with keen
entomological pins. This is a book worth buying.
Yol. VII, of “The Yellow Book” (6s. 6d.) con-
tains a great many pretty things and no parti-
cularly strong thing. The verse is cultured and
graceful and empty of inspiration; the tales are
mostly good without being very good; and the
art grows more conventional and certainly less
ugly- “The Yellow Book, ”in losing its note of
heresy and ‘ ‘ decadence, ’’ is, however, losing all
that made it remarkable. There is one strik-
ing article in Yol. Vll.—a note on books and
critics, by “ The Yellow Dwarf,” written in
the old slashing Saturday style. The essence
of it is an assault on current English methods of
book-reviewing—with an indication of the writer’s
“ better way.” He saith :
In order to criticise you must have certain endowments
—you must have a certain equipment. You must have
eyes and ears, you must have taste; you must have the
analytic faculty and the knack of nice expression ; you
must have the habit of getting at close quarters with
your thought and your emotion-you must be able to
explain wuy, for what qualities, for what defects, you
cherish Mr. Henry James (for instance), regard Mr.
Marriott Watson with expectant pleasure, dread Mr.
Anthony Hope, and flee from Miss Marie Corelli as from
the German measles. You must have knowledge—a Uni-
versity education, indeed, would do you no harm, nor an
acquaintance with the literatures of France and Russia.
You must have a tradition of culture. And, above all,
you must have leisure—for any sort of oonsklered writing
you must have leisure. Well, how many of these endow-
ments, how much of this equipment,'is vour Pressman,
your Saturday Reviewer, likely to have V

To Correspondents. — W.M.W. : Lean.
French from a teacher if possible. If not, get an
Ollendorff (os. fid.), a dictionary (2s. 8d.), and any
easy annotated classic (Is. 3d.) ; and peg away.
When you’re through Ollendorff take up another
book of exercises, and a novel like “ Les Trois
Mousquetaires,” and then go through Ollendorff
again. He’s absurd and antiquated, but he
hammers the language in.

THE Vl f AL SPARK I i
The near approach of Xmas Day is bringing
tens of thousands to our stores from far and near,
people who have been there before, and folk who
for the first time have boon vouchsafed that wis-
dom which impels so many to steer for the Hay-
market. Our arrange cents for the special wants
of the festive season have this year been on an extra
liberal scale, and the comfort of our customers has
been ministered to, in no small degree, by recent j
improvements. Thus, as a consequence, ample ]
space, so essentially necessary to successful shop-
ping operations, is at command, while that leisurely j
inspection of purchases, only to be accomplished
in the absence of undue noise and bustle, is
afforded to all on business bent. And yet there .
are not wanting signs that before our doors close
on XMAS EVE, the capacity of
OUB IMMENSE WAREHOUSES
As well as our desire, and that of our willing
assistants, to do their level best, will be taxed to
the utmost. The position may well be likened to
that of a great national gathering upon the occa-
sion of some popular demonstration. A great
man mounts the platform to speak, and as he )
proceeds the crowd warm to his utterances. <
Cheer after cheer follows his glowing words, when
as he nears the end, and, in fervid language, sets
the nation’s course and proclaims the people’s
destiny, with one accord the masses rise and cheer
the speaker to the echo, till there is danger of
some precious words escaping ears whose owners
long to profit by them.
THE VITAL SPARK
The life-giving fire of Low Prices for the best of
everything, has set the town aflame and stirred
each Xmas buyer to come to us. Since the Xmas
trade opened in the first week of the month there
has been, day by day, a steady increase in our
trade. And so it will be. Customers will multiply,
till when Xmas Eve comes, from far and m ar,
from suburbs and city and township, from village
and mountainside, from every point of the compass
and every part of the colony, everybody, impelled
by a mighty enthusiasm and urged by ihe solemn
conviction that it’s the proper thing to do, will
have done their Xmas shopping, or be going to do
it, at our store.

General Bo.otli : “If a sheep walks over
an acre of land once a month they call it cultiva-
tion out here.” And in England, if a hook-nosed
evangelist walks over a county once a year, they
call it religion.
To a Southerner, the havoc committed
among cattle, up the Gulf way, by ticks is
incredible. Out of a mob of 250, recently travel-
ling South, 150 died in crossing a tick-infested
district. They suffer intense agony, and the hide,
in parts, is as full of holes as a sieve. If the tick
could only be induced to browse on the rabbit 1
Inspector-General Fosbery, as Chairman
of N.S.W. Aborigines Protection Board, expressed
himself at a recent meeting in terms of enthusi-
astic satisfaction with the results obtained by the
work of his organisation. “At one time,” he
said, “ the aborigines were to a certain extent
beyond control, but now every individual family
is under the cognisance and control of the Board.”
And then the census returns of the aboriginal
population for ’95, compiled from police informa-
tion, were read, and showed that, in Oct. last,
there were 96 full-bloods less than in the same
month of the preceding year, and 125 half-castes
more. “Now, every individual family is under
the cognisance and control of the Board.”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic ; 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. •
Hori Grey once enlisted in the ranks.
During the West Coast war the Govt, steamer on
which he was a passenger anchored early one
morning in New Plymouth roadstead, and he was
conveyed ashore in the ship’s boat. There was no
one astir on the beach except the provost-sergeant.
“Nice morning,” he remarked in his most suave
style. “Beautiful,” responded the stranger.
“ Going up town ? ” inquired the sergeant. “I
want to find the Marsland Hotel,” was the reply,
“Let me have the pleasure of showing you the
way,” said the extremely civil non-com. —who
beguiled the walk with sweeping criticisms on the
conduct of the war, and fierce denunciations of
the philo-Maori sympathies of Governor Grey.
Suddenly halting and eyeing the new-comer
from head to foot, he remarked : “ Well, you’ve
seen your best days, but you look a likely chap to
carry a rifle. Ever been drilled?” “Well, I
think I know my facings,” replied the visitor.
“Thought so,” said the sergeant, “ Well, I sup-
pose you don’t know that anybody who lands here
during martial law is liable to be compulsorily
enlisted?” “Oh, yes, I’ve heard something of
the kind,” replied Grey. “Well, then, my
friend, here’s Her Majesty’s shilling, and you
may consider yourself enlisted in the Taranaki
Militia. I’ll get the doctor to pass you by and
bye, have you sworn in, and rig you out in uni-
form.” By this time they had reached the hotel.
“Thanks, sergeant,” said the new recruit, pocket-
ing the shilling. “If you’ll just allow me to
deliver a message here I’ll be at your service.” He
whispered a message to the barman, who flew
upstairs. “Have a drink to wet your enlist-
ment,” said the sergeant. “Thank you, I never
drink before breakfast.” “I do,” said the non-
com. Two minutes later the Defence Minister,
hastily attired in a dressing-gown and smoking-
oap, rushed downstairs, and, making a low bow,
said “Good morning, your Excellency. Pray
excuse my dress ; I did not expect you so early.”
The sergeant almost choked in the act of drinking
a long beer, and when he had recovered from his
confusion he stammered out an apology. Grey
complimented him on his zeal, and afterwards
recommended him for promotion. But he kept
that shilling.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 21, 1895.
The New Foreign-trade Fra.
On the first day of 1896 New South Wales is
to commence another epoch of partial Free-
trade, and the alleged curse of Protection—
such Protection as it was—is to begin to dis-
appear by instalments out of the land. By
that time New South Wales will have had
about four years of a more or less Protective
tariff, and before it enters upon the great,
glad era of freedom and prosperity that is to
come, it is worth while to look back on the
short-lived experiment inaugurated by the
Dibbs Government and consider its results.
The charge most persistently advanced by
the Foreign-trade party and the Foreign-
trade press is that Protection raises prices,
.and the burden of its most frequent wail is
that Protective duties are a heart-breaking
load upon the miserable “consumer.”
Therefore, the Foreign-trader’s crusade has
been carried on in the name of this allegedly
down-trodden person, and he has been called
upon incessantly to vote for the importing
interest in order to save himself from des-
truction. In most cases, too, he has done it,
for the average man in course of time
believes almost anything that is told him
often enough, and with sufficient energy,
and by dint of much iteration he really came
to imagine that the Protective period in New
South Wales had been one of high prices and
scarcity. Yet, in reality, it has been, so far
as concerns the necessaries of life, a period
of such cheapness as the province never saw
before, even in the best days of the Foreign-
trade regime, and the doleful lie that Protec-
tion always raises prices is confronted by the
fact that prices have fallen everywhere.
• • • ♦
Comparing the retail prices of 1891, the
last year of the old Foreign-trade period,
with those of 1894, the last year of Protec-
tion for which complete returns are avail-
able, Government statistician Coghlan
shows the following results : Bread fell
from 3|d. to 2|d. per loaf ; beef from 4d. to
3d. per lb. ; butter from Is. Id. to Is. ;
cheese from 9d. to Bd. ; sugar from 3|d. to
; potatoes from ss. to 4s. 6d. per cwt. ;
bacon from lOd. to 7d. ; eggs from Is. 6d. to
Is. 3d, per dozen ; oatmeal from 2|d. to 2|d.
per lb. ; salt from Id. to |d. ; soap from
B|d. to 3d. ; and starch from sd. to 4|d.
Almost all o r hese are articles on which
duties were either imposed or increased
under the Dibbs tariff, and according to all
Foreign-trade logic the prices should have
had a steady upward tendency. There was
also a noticeable decrease in the prices of
hoots, clothing and furniture, and in
house-rents, and there was, during the
period covered by Coghlan’s figures, not
one noticeable increase in any article of
common consumption—in fact, the Pro-
tective period in New South Wales has been
par excellence the period of all-round cheap-
ness. The stereotyped arguments in favor
of Foreign-trade demonstrate, with appar-
ent clearness, that this state of things can’t
be, but, logically or illogically, the fact re-
mains that it is. Why it is' the Foreign-
trade logician is left to find out at his
leisure.
* • • *
During the same four years agriculture
progressed as it never progressed before in
New South Wales. The total area under
crops, which had remained almost stationary
during the ten years of Freetrade from 1881
to 1891, increased about 60 per cent, under
Protection between 1891 and 1895. The area
under wheat, which had barely doubled in
the last 13 years of the great importing
policy, now almost doubled itself in four
years, and the actual increase in the four
years between 1891 and 1895 was 50 per
cent, more than that in the 25 years between
1866 and 1891. In almost all other kinds of
grain-crops, as well as in potatoes, fruit, honey,
and other miscellaneous products, the in-
crease during the short period of Protection
was the biggest recorded in the history of
the province. And as the natural result
New South Wales, for the first time in its
history, came somewhere near to being self-
supporting in the matter of agricultural pro-
duce. All through the many years of Free-
trade, that province, despite its great area,
its small population and fertile soil, and the
distances from which imported supplies
have to be fetched, was helplessly dependent
on foreign growers for a large part of its sup-
plies of wheat, oats, barley, flour, eggs, fruit,
vegetables, butter, bacon, and many other
like articles. Also in most cases, right up to
the beginning of the Protective era, instead
of the local supplies getting nearer to an
equality with the demand they fell steadily
farther behind it. The brief period of the
Dibbs tariff reduced the imports of wheat,
oats, barley, and other grains by nearly one-
half, and of maize by three-fourths, thereby
saving £470,000 of hard cash per annum; and
made New South Wales a great exporter of
butter and cheese instead of an importer as
formerly, thereby bringing much money
into the country as well as preventing
a lot going out. In a very few years
more, at the same rate of progress, it would
have made New South Wales wholly in-
dependent of foreign supplies, and thereby
furnished much-needed employment to some
thousands of agriculturists and stopped a
ruinous drain on the national resources.
The Dibbs tariff, inefficient as it was, was
the only real atttempt to “ settle the people
on the land ” in the whole dismal course of
Botany Bay legislation.
* ♦ * *
In manufactures the brief period of Protec-
tion, despite the insufficiency and haphazard
characterof the tariff and various other things,
was one of reasonable prosperity. In 1892,
the first complete year of the national policy,
there was a large increase in the number of
persons employed in the factories and work-
shops of New South Wales. In 1893 —the
year of bank-smashes, panic, and paralysis—
there was a very large decrease—the largest
ever recorded in one year in the province.
In 1894, when the slow recovery began to
set in, there was the largest increase in New
South Wales history, though the recovery
was only partial at the best, and by no
means compensated for the previous year’s
downfall. But the net decrease was mostly
in the industries which were not protected
under the Dibbs tariff, and was as much a
proof of the evils of Freetrade as of those of
Protection. Comparing 1891 with 1894, the
total decline in the number of hands em-
ployed was 3384, and the falling-off in the
building trades owing to the dying away of
the borrowing boom, the economies prac-
tised in the railway workshops, and the
fluctuations of the sugar and tobacco indus-
tries, neither of which was interfered with
under the Dibbs tariff, accounted for more
than all of these. Most of the industries
which the tariff was specially designed to
encourage showed some slight increase of
employment, despite the banking crash, and
the great building and investment society
collapse, and all the other disasters of the
most disastrous period in the province’s
history. Altogether the Dibbs tariff, though
it had but a brief trial under very tem-
pestuous conditions, produced reasonably
good results even in manufacturing in-
dustries, although that was the department
in which it was least successful.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
During the Protective period New South
Wales, for the first occasion in more than 20
years, became for the time a solvent country
and paid the interest on its foreign debts,
public and private, out of its own resources
instead of paying them out of the proceeds
of new borrowings. A country which is
cumbered with a huge burden of foreign
debt must export as much more than it im-
ports as is necessary to cover interest on its
liabilities—or else it must incur more and
still more liabilities. The temporary
prosperity of the Foreign-trade epoch
was arrived at by the latter process,
and under the old regime which is
being so joyfully restored New South
Wales went to the had to the extent of
six or eight or even twelve millions per
annum and took no thought for the morrow.
The temporary adversity of the Protective
epoch was the result of an attempt to face
the hard realities of the situation and make
the country pay its liabilities out of its earn-
ings—a thing which had hardly been
previously attempted within the memory of
this generation. During this same period
the re-export trade of. New South Wales rose
to the largest figure ever recorded—a conclu-
sive answer to the theorists who allege that
Protection drives away commerce. The
shipping trade of the province attained its
high-water mark during the Protective era,
and the savings-bank deposits reached a
higher point than ever before. The public
debt increased at a slower rate than it had
done at any time during the last 16 years of
the old Foreign-trade era. And, despite the
oft-told lie about the “burden” which the
Protective tariff laid upon the people, the
amount taken from the people as Customs
duties in 1894, the last complete year of the
Dibbs tariff, was less than the amount ex-
tracted eight years previously from a smaller
population under the alleged Freetrade
policy, and the total amount of tariff taxation
per inhabitant was smaller than at any time
since 1880. Altogether, the alleged burden
of the Protective tariff' was about the most
hollow imposture that could well be ima-
gined.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
The chief reason of the downfall of the
Protectionist Government in New South
Wales was its ghastly ill-luck. The average
unreasoning inhabitant sets down to the
policy of the party in power at the moment
all the disasters that befall the country—
drought and flood and fire, bad crops, bank
failures, boom collapses, rust, weevil, large
blue flies, low prices for exports in foreign
markets, battle, murder, and sudden death,
all alike. And the Protectionist era was a
period of more unavoidable disaster than the
country had ever compressed into so short a
space of time before. The banks, building
societies, and investment institutions came
down with one hideous run, through many
years’ accumulated folly and mismanagement;
and though New South Wales came off better
than its neighbours, it came off badly enough.
The prices of New South Wales exports in
London, which had remained almost station-
ary from 1885 to 1890, dropped fully one-
fifth between 1892 and 1894, which involved
a loss of fully £4,'000,000 a year. Silver
declined so heavily that the value of the
province’s silver output fell between ’9l and
’94 to the extent of £1,300,000 per annum.
There was an almost equally heavy drop in
tin and copper. The opening-up of coal-
fields in Victoria and Japan so injured the
New South Wales coal-trade that the value
of the output declined between 1891 and
1894 to the extent of £600,000 a year, or
rather over one-third. The sheep-breeding in-
dustry, the main prop and stay of New South
Wales, had enjoyed seven years of splendid
good fortune and good wool prices from 1885
to 1891, and in that time the number of
sheep in the province increased from
37,000,000 to 61,000,000. Then with the
advent of the luckless Dibbs Government
came a sudden change ; in 1892-3-4 over
5,400,000 sheep perished through western
droughts, and, instead of an increase of 3g
millions per annum as formerly, there was a
decrease, at the time when wool prices were
lowest, of 2,000,000 a year. Yet, in the face
of this unparalleled combination of disasters,
the brief period during which New South
Wales followed the Protectionist faith was
the most prosperous in its history for many
years past, and the reckless abandonment of
the policy which achieved so much out of
such very had materials is one of the most
remarkable instances of popular ignorance,
ingratitude, and credulity imaginable.
The Great Calvert Myth.
That truly monotonous person, young Albert
F. Calvert, the “great Australian explorer,”
turns up again in the London papers, this Lime
in Jerome K. Jerome’s To-Day, wherein a
foolish nonentity named Gilbert D. Suther-
land grovels over him in a truly awful fashion.
The spectacle of Gilbert D. Sutherland slob-
bering Albert F. Calvert in Jerome K.
Jerome’s paper is a solemn essay on the evils
of having a gaunt initial slicking up in the
middle of your name. Sutherland’s painful
shriek is adorned with a portrait of Calvert,
in which he appears as a nice-looking, but
rather vacuous youth, with a waxed moustache
and a beautiful carriage-drive down the middle
of his head, and a large, ordinary-looking ear,
and silk facings on his coat, and a shirt-stud
about the size of a dinner-plate. ■ Nobody in
Australia that The Bulletin is aware of
ever heard of young Calvert exploring
anywhere in particular ; it is believed in
some quarters that another man of the same
name did a little in that way many years ago,
but even his doings didn’t amount to much,
and the mild, young person with the dinner-
plale on his abdomen is almost unknown in
these parts. Yet Gilbert D. Sutherland not
only grovels about him, but about all bis
ancestors, and particularly he goes on in a
dreadful fashion about bis grandfather. That
hair-raising progenitor seems to have been a
mixture of Bishop Pontoppidan, Munchausen,
the great sea-serpent and Deadwood Dick. In
his early days he “ went to Attaoama, in Peru”
(Sutherland doesn’t know how to spell
Atucuna, but that doesn’t matter) to study the
silver mines of Potosi, which are in Bolivia,
many miles away. How lie lived in Atacama,
which is a horrible land of death and flies and
alkali, is not stated, but while there he in-
vented the chlorination process. This was
about 1835, and the chlorination process was
invented by a quite different man some2o years
later, but that doesn’t matter much either.
• • •
Then the hair-waxed Calvert’s dreadful old
progenitor discovered gold in large quantities
in Wales, but somehow he seems to have done
nothing with it. He was also the first dis-
coverer of gold in New South Wales and
Maoriland—the fact that gold wasn’t heard of
in Maoriland for a good many years after his
alleged visit really doesn’t signify. Where-
abouts in Maoriland he made his gold-discovery
isn’t mentioned. He was likewise the first man
to find the Kapunga and Burra-Burra mines in
South Australia. It is hardly worth mention-
ing here that the Burra-Burra was dis-
covered by Pickett, and that Kapunga
is apparently a misprint for Kapunda,
and the Kapunda mine was found by
Francis Dutton. After this the ancient
mariner seems to have broken loose
from all semblance of fact or probability, and
raged promiscuously over a great sea of loose
fiction and gory adventure. He was tl(e first
man to find gold, silver, diamonds, and extinct
craters in S.A. Also, he was the first man to
find silver and gold in Tasmania. After that
he landed at Exmouth Gulf (W.A.), and was
the first man to find gold in that province also.
Likewise, he was the first man to discover coal
there ; in fact, speaking generally, he seems to
have been the first man to find anything any-
where. Then he founded Port Lyttelton (which
Gilbert Sutherland doesn’t know bow to
spell properly), and Christchurch (M.L.), and
did many other awful things which no one in
Australasia seems to remember ; and at last
he returned to England in a great glare and
howl of magnificence with a ton of pure gold.
Also, he owned an immense gold-mine on the
Amazon River, in South America. There is no
mention by any authority that The Bulletin
ever came across, or that anyone The Bulletin
ever heard of came across, of Calvert’s ton of
gold, and his famous coal-discovery was really
made by the Gregorys, but these small trifles
really don’t signify.
• # #
Old Calvert finished his career with a final
burst of magnificence. Says Gilbert D.
Sutherland
He was asked by Messrs. York and Company, the great
railway contractors, who had invested thousands in a
copper-mine without any result, to go to Devon to inspect
their location. On arriving at Okehampton he found the
shaft being put down in the middle of the town. At this
he demurred, telling them not only that there was no
copper there, hut that they were two miles out of the
way. To this Mr. Bird, a man of law, objected. How-
ever, Mr. Calvert, not to be beaten, ordered a trap to take
him southward, and, when he had proceeded a little over
two miles, indicated a position to sink a shaft, which hap-
pened to he in the centre of a highly-cultivated turnip-
field. Capt. James Richards, of the Devon Great Consols,
did all in Ids power to persuade Mr. York that no copper
could be found on the spot declaring that it was blas-
phemous in the eyes of God for anyone to say that he
could see what was under the ground without, first ex-
ploiting the same, and he offered to eat. all the copper
that should he found there. To cut a lonsr story short,
Mr. Calvert set to work. The shaft was commenced, and
called the Calvert, shaft. In six months’ time the driving
had cut into ricli copper-ore. A lump of about 3cwt. was
placed on the table at the White Hart Hotel, in Oke-
hampton, and a cover was made for it. Mr. York invited
a few friends to dine with him, not forgetting Capt. Jann s
Richards. At the right moment the cover was removed,
and Mr. York invited Capt. Richards to swallow his pill.
“Oh!” said Mr. James, “Mr. Calvert is either a very
clever man or a very lucky one."
Let them make a magic-lantern slide of that.
* * •
Then the biographer gets on to the present
Calvert. He was born in 1869, and was a
dreadfully remarkable infant. In 1890, when
he was only 21, be arrived in Victoria, and his
fame had preceded him to sucli an extent that
he was at once rushed by the directors of the
“National Bank of Australia” to report on
the Hustler’s Extended and Great Extended
mines, at Sandhurst. “He was unable to
endorse their valuation of the property.” It is
quite a trifling matter that there is no “ National
Bank of Australia,” and that the two mines in
question belong to private companies, so that
the bank, it it existed, would have no par-
ticular interest in having them valued. Also,
they were so well-known to everybody that
the idea of any bank, even if it existed, hiring
a small youth fresh from England to value
them is unique. After that, the story tails off
into vague and delirious whoops about Cal-
vert’s great exploring-expeditions in Westralia.
The details given are interesting in their mild
and harmless simplicity. Sutherland doesn’t
even state that Calvert went any distance or
was away any remarkable length of time.
The “ Westralian Paragon ” started from Roe-
bourne, which was then a decent-sized town,
and he got as far as Mallina, which was then
also an actual town just two days’ journey
from Roebourne by a moderately good road,
and then apparently he made for the telegraph-
line and seems to have followed it carefully,
presumably in order that the line-repairer
could find him if he got lost. This heroic ex-
pedition carried three months’ provisions in a
buggy (think of exploring with a buggy !) and
was badly bitten by ants. The biographer is
particular about the ants. Evidently it never
strikes him that the Australian gets ant-bitten
almost every Sunday. In the course of the
journey Calvert found vast deposits of gold
and didn’t wait to annex them ; crossed a
river-bed five miles wide; found more rich
gold, silver, and other metals ; found the richest
gold and antimony mine in the world and left
it there; found rubies, sapphires, and tour-
malines ; found another rich gold-reef; also
a very rich tin lode; also plains “literally
covered with oxide of manganese ” ; like-
wise a hill of gold, more tin-mines ; more
gold-mines ; and finally he gallantly sailed from
Port Darwin to Melbourne in the China mail
steamer, and made the acquaintance of Robert
Louis Stevenson. It is further mentioned
that the hero was considerably “ lacerated by
spinifex.” And now it is reported that Cal-
vert returns to Australia to explore some more,
and find a lot more things for the first time, and
again have his biographer sitting like a solemn
nightmare on the chest of this much-afflicted
land, where the memory of his wonderful grand-
father has so completely dropped out of sight
that nobody remembers that lie ever did any-
thing surprising. As a specimen of frantic


literature (price two-pence with coloured cover),
Gilbert D. Sutherland’s history of the
Calvert family is unique.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Victorian Railway Reform.
The new Victorian Railway Bill, which is to
put the Victorian railway service on a per-
manent and thoroughly efficient basis, is about
the weakest thing yet devised by the Turner
Government. The trouble in the Victorian
Railway Department is that the lines don’t pay
working expenses and interest on cost of con-
struction, and that they never have done so to
any great extent. The reasons for this state of
things are various. Victoria has too much rail-
way in proportion to population to make it
possible to cover expenses under existing condi-
tions. The money was borrowed at too high a
rate of interest by the mud-headed financiers of
the past, and far too much money was fooled
away on land-resumption swindles, therefore
the railways have to pay interest on perhaps
£3,000,000 more than they should do; and the
loans were fixed for too long a term, so that
they can’t be converted when a favorable oppor-
tunity presents itself. No railway system
situated as badly as Victoria’s is in all these
matters has ever paid working expenses and
interest at the rate Victoria has to pay.
The Turner Government, however, has found
two notable remedies for the present slate
of things. One is to place the railways in
charge of a Trust, the members of which
will receive £750 a year each, and the
Trust is to appoint a General Manager, subject
to the approval of the Governor-in-Couuci 1
which is about as interesting as if the appoint-
ment was subject to the approval of the Gover-
nor in his shower-bath. In old days the
Minister was practically the General Manager ;
this system was a distinct failure. Then the
responsibility was shifted one step further on
by the Ministry appointing a Commissioner to
do the management. Now it is moved on two
steps by the Ministry appointing the Trustees
and the Trustees appointing the Manager ; and
if this fails it will presumably be moved along
three steps by the Ministry appointing a Board,
which will appoint the Trustees, who will
appoint the Manager. The other remedy is
still more remarkable and is expressed as
follows :—■
In the following oases, that is to say : —(a) Where Par-
liament makes any alteration in the law which occasions
any increase of expenditure by the trust or any decrease
of the railways revenue ; or (b) where Parliament directs
the trust to carry out any system or matter of policy
which occasions or results in any increase of expenditure
by the trust or any decrease of the railways revenue ; or
(c) where Parliament authorises the construction of any
new line of railway which, when vested in the trustees,
does not produce sufficient revenue to cover the interest
on its cost of construction, and the expenses of its main-
tenance the annual amount of the increase of expendi-
ture or decrease of revenue or of the loss resulting from
such new line of railway shall be from time to time
notified in writing by the trust to the Commissioners of
Audit, and, if certified by them, shall be provided by
Parliament in the annual Appropriation Act and paid to
the trust.
This simply means that the general revenue is
to makegood the railway deficit, and as it does
so now, and always has done so, and must keep
on doing so in any case, this is not a very
bright or original idea. Anyhow, it leaves the
deficit just where it was ; the “ notification in
writing ” doesn’t alter the case in the least.
The only way to reduce the shortage on the old
lines is to diminish expenses, or to cut down
interest on borrowed capital, or to increase
revenue, or to do two or all three of these
things at once, and if they can’t be done just
now, the only way is to let the shortage stand.
And the best way to prevent a loss on new
lines is to build them cheaply on the better-
ment principle, and to construct them wholly
or in great part out of revenue instead of with
borrowed money. But no amount of mere
stationery can alter the situation. The fact that
the Trust is to notify the Audit Commissioner
about the deficit, which he knows of already,
and he is to certify to Parliament, which is
fully aware of the matter in any case, and
Parliament is to argue at great length about
voting the amount, which is already spent
whether Parliament votes it or not, and finally
tell the Ministry to tell the Trust that it will
be paid when it has to be paid anyhow, is
merely so much wasted ink and circumlocution,
and won’t alter the situation one copper.
The “ First Offender.”
Item from Queensland paper
The criminal sittings of the Supreme Court were con-
tinued yesterday before S. W. Griffith, Chief Justice. A
young man, named Marks, charged with loitering in the
public streets with intent to commit a felony, was sen-
tenced to six months’ hard labor. Alphonse L. Pene,
late agent for the Queensland National Bank at Mitchell
(on the Maranoa River), was sentenced to two years’ hard
labor for embezzlement. The total defalcations amounted
to t'l2B. The prisoner was released under the Offenders’
Probation Act upon entering into the necessary sureties.
The old story : the man of no social account
who does six months’ worth of alleged crime
gets his six months, and the high-toned person
who does two years’ worth gets nothing. Yet
it is just possible that Griffith, C.J., acted up
to what he thought were his genuine convic-
tions, though they were only his hazy eye-
impressions. The man who is shabbily dressed,
and who has been in gaol awaiting trial, so that
his beard has grown coarse and scrubby, and
his hair hasn’t been done by a barber, nor his
boots cleaned, nor his linen newly got up at the
Chinese laundry, and who has enjoyed few
bathing facilities in the lock-up, always looks
an irreclaimable ruffian on whom mercy would
be wholly wasted. And the man whose well-
to-do friends have bailed him out, and
who has bought a new suit of clothes
out of his stealings, and who turns up in
the dock clean-shaven and nicely hair-parted
and with a dazzling dickey, and rests a white
band on the railing, always looks like a saint
who, even if he has given way to sudden
temptation, deserves a chance to reform.
How would justice go, The Bulletin wonders,
if the accused was concealed behind a thick
mosquito-curtain during his trial, so that his
appearance couldn’t affect the jury or the J udge
one way or another? Then, if he was found
guilty, he could be removed, still unseen, and
when the sittings were over the accumulated
prisoners could be brought up for sentence, all
fixed up alike by the gaol barber, and dressed
alike in grey slop suits and Crimean shirts, so
that no man would look more villainous than
another unless the villainy was inherent in his
countenance. Then a conscientious Judge
would have a fair chance of considering each
offender on his merits. Many a Judge who has
consistently refused to give the shabby criminal
a chance under the First Offenders’ Act if he
could be gaoled for 14 days awaiting trial, and
then photographed in the dock with a straggly
undergrowth on his face, and a dirty collar,
and disordered hair, and long nails, and the
whitewash of the cell on his clothes, would find
that he also looked about the last person that
he would dream of letting loose upon society.
And if this picture was always kept before him
on the judicial desk, then John Smith, accused
on the hazy charge of being “a suspected
person,” might meet with a lot more justice
than he does. Some day The Bulletin will
perhaps print Griffith’s portrait as he would
look under Mark’s conditions, and ask him
if he could feel, under such conditions, like
a person who deserved any consideration what-
ever.
Sneaking-in Protection.
Premier Reid lately struck the truly brilliant
idea of paying <£50,000 a year out of revenue as
a subsidy to the N.S.W. Railway Department,
so that the Department might pay it back to the
revenue later on as part of the regular receipts,
and, as a natural consequence, the railway
earnings would profess to be so much more, and
the deficit so much less than the real figure.
There are at least four decided objections to
this device, altogether apart from its humorous
imbecility. It is done to enable the rates on
agricultural and dairy produce to be re-
duced, therefore it is professedly a system
of Protection for the agricultural interest,
proposed to be “sneaked in” by a Govern-
ment which lives by denouncing Protection
and gets its salary under false pretences
by professing to abolish it. It is also a
foolish and reckless outlay by a Government
which lives upon vain promises to reduce
expenditure. It is further a foolish and clumsy
“ faking ” of the public accounts. If the rates
on produce are so reduced as to bring in
<£50,000 a year less than they now do, then the
railway deficit will be .£50,000 a year more than
it is at present. If the nation reckons that it
is worth while having that amount added to
the existing deficit to secure certain advantages
for a certain class it is its own affair, but at all
events it should know exactly how it stands,
and this method of making the railway receipts
look £50,000 larger and the shortage that much
smaller than reality seems expressly devised to
keep the country from gaining this knowledge.
And, as a fourth objection, this new departure
makes an end of the boasted commercial
system, and is the beginning of more political
interference. If the principle is once estab-
lished that the Government may demand that
coal, or carrots, or gin, or galvanised iron, or
“ colonial ” beer, or any product in which it
takes an interest for any reason, honest or
otherwise, shall be carried below cost, provided
it pays the loss out of the public Treasury so as to
make the railway accountsfoo& square when they
are not, then the railways are no longer being
run on straight business principles, and the era
of political dodgery has started again. The one
great purpose of the Commissioner system was
to put the railways beyond Government inter-
ference, so that no Ministry could use them as
a means wherewith to secure the support of
any person or class at the expense of the
community at large, and that is just what
the Reid Government avowedly wants to
do. And while the Ministry thus bids
for the agricultural and pastoral vote by
falling away into the paths of Protection, it
is not even dead certain whether the Protection
is all for the native, or partly for him and
partly for the foreigner. The reduced rates
for which the community at large will be
taxed if the scheme ever gets through will pre-
sumably apply all round, and in that case the
nation will pay to enable the Sydney importer
to send foreign grain into the nearer country
towns to compete against the N.S.W. pro-
ducer, as well as to enable the latter to send
his produce into the cities. This is a fifth
aspect of the case which calls for some slight
inquiry. Protection of this variety was hardly
expected, even from the most rampant of
Foreign-trade Governments.
The Law and the Pugilist.
In Sydney, last week, John Barker, amateur
practitioner in the art of manly disfigurement,
went through the formality of being tried for
“ inflicting grievous bodily harm ” on the bat-
tered Dutchman, Placke, and was duly ac-
quitted. Therefore, the law either decided
that Placke wasn’t grievously harmed, which
is impossible ; or that it wasn’t Barker, but
some other person, who harmed him, which
looks wholly improbable ; or that the mashing
of a man in a pugilistic encounter isn’t recog-
nised as an offence at law, in which case the
matter should have been left alone from the
beginning ; or else it decided that no blame
attached because the injury wasn’t intentional,
in which case Barker would of necessity have
been held equally guiltless if Placke had died
from his injuries. Amid all these muddled
alternatives it seems about time the law
seriously defined its position in regard to
aggravated pugilism. The statutes of New
South Wales were fully cognisant of the fact
that Barker and Placke intended meeting for
the express purpose of assault and battery.
Certain legal agents were there and saw
them assault and batter in pursuance of this
purpose. They met, in short, to do bodily
harm to each other, and both they and the
law were fully aware that they might do a lot
more harm than they intended. As the law,
being fully forewarned, allowed them to take
the chance of whatever might happen, its
futile attempt to kick afterwards was wholly
absurd and illogical. If it was illegal for
Barker and Placke to meet for the express
purpose of obliterating each other’s features at
the risk of skull-fracturc and accidental death,
then the agents of the law, who didn’t stop
them when they had the opportunity, were
accomplices in the act and should have been
tried along with Barker. If the meeting
wasn’t illegal, but only the accidental skull-
fracture, still the fact remains that the law
permitted the circumstances that led up to the
said fracture, well knowing that such circum-
stances were liable to lead up to things of the
kind. Moreover, it was wholly absurd to
arrest Barker for hitting Placke unless the
law also arrested Placke for hitting Barker.
These two unscienced men swiped wildly
at each other with the same purpose
in view, and the difference between
them lay chiefly, if not solely, in the
fact that an inscrutable Providence made
Barker the taller and longer-armed man
of the pair. Therefore, the law should either
have charged all the parties concerned, includ-
ing the police and the Government, with the
same offence, or else it should have let them all
go and arrested Providence alone. The law is
hereby invited to seriously consider these dis-
crepancies, and if it has any definite attitude
in the pugilistic business, then it would do well
to explain it at once. The failure of the
Barker prosecution merely suggests that it
has been hiring an expensive lawyer to appear
before itself an 1 find out if it has an attitude,
and the verdict has been that it doesn’t know
whether it has one or not.
A Unique Dinner.
The dinner given the other day, in Melbourne,
to celebrate Syme’s defeat of Speight, was
unique in many ways. It was the first time on
record that a grateful counsel feted his client,
with a view to reminding the outside world
that “absolutely nothing has been done
latterly ” towards taking up a collection for the
victim. Then the guest, and the oratory !
The company couldn’t separate without drink-
ing the health of Parliament. Parliament was
the corrupt railway-making institution that, on
David Syme’s showing, had “ debauched ”
Speight. So they drank its health. Deakin
returned thanks, both as an M.L.A. and a
junior counsel for Syme in the legal victory
under discussion. Deakin was first lieu-
tenant to Premier Gillies, and, therefore,
a prime mover in the ruin of Victoria
(vide Age) through the agency of Speight.
Suitable man to return thanks on this occasion.
Deakin blandly stated that “ the first insight
he gained into the condition of railway affairs
he could truly say was acquired by reading the
Age articles. The Ministry to which he
belonged also first awoke to the threatening
prospects of the future through what was
said in those articles.” He might have men-
tioned that the Ministry to which he belonged
was tottering into oblivion before the articles
appeared. He might have admitted that he
was included, years afterwards, in the list of
politicians who brought pressure to bear upon
Speight. But he didn’t. Ex-Chief Secretary
Deakin, the man who really represented
David Syme in the Gillies Ministry, was con :
tent to plead sinful ignorance re the Gillies-
Deakin Railway expenditure previous tp read-
ing something about it in the Age. Therefore,
he blessed the Age for having pointed out the
error of his ways at the eleventh hour, when
his capacity for bringing the country to ruin
was exhausted. And his former colleague Dow,
who sat somewhere in the vicinity, backed him
up with several cheerful “ Hear, hears.” A
unique dinner.
Picked Marks.
The case of Gail, the alleged bigamist, is
another remarkable side-issue of the Dean trial.
Gail was a Crown witness against Dean, and
just before the most interesting part of the case
came on he was arrested on a charge of bigamy.
So far as appears, he was technically more or
less guilty ; his offence seems to have arisen,
however, through a mere misconception of the
law on his part, and he was presently dis-
charged. The surprising part of the story is
that the Crown didn’t prosecute Gail according
to its usual custom, or send a big legal authority
to enthusiastically demand his conviction ;
instead it paid the costs of his defence, which
was quite a novel character for the Crown to
appear in. And, seeing that the same Crown
lately stopped the miserable allowance formerly
paid for defending penniless prisoners arraigned
on capital charges, and while prosecuting them
all it knows leaves their defence practically
to take care of itself, and that it took this
course on the ground that there were no
funds available for defence purposes, it
would be interesting to know how funds
became so suddenly available in this par-
ticular instance. If it could find the money
to defend Gail, who was in no serious
danger, and who was not penniless, then it
presumably can find money from the same
source to pay for the defence of other men who
are penniless, whose lives are at stake, and who
are much more in want of defence, inasmuch as
the whole legal machinery of the State is at
work for the prosecution. There is no earthly
reason why the Crown shouldn’t have defended
Gail, providing it did the same in the case of
other accused persons —such as the perse-
cuted Mrs. Butler —who require it a lot more;
but it doesn’t—it prosecutes them instead, and
if they have to hang or go to gaol for many
years for want of adequate defence it simply
lets them hang or go to gaol. If there is no
money for the defence of accused persons then
was the money for Gail’s defence misappro-
priated from some other fund ? And if it was,
will the legal authorities of Botany Bay, in the
name of common decency and humanity, mis-
appropriate some more from the same source,
whatever it may be, to defend the next man
who can’t hire a lawyer to show cause why he
shouldn’t go to prison or the gallows ?
A Victorian gunner who sued the State, the
other day, for £6 17s. 3d., part of his retiring
allowance, evoked some interesting information
about the status of the gaudy military person
in uniform. The authorities refused to pay
this amount, which Chief Justice Madden
decided was legally due and ought to be paid,
but all the same the C.J. found against the
plaintiff because, according to law, “a soldier
cannot sue the Queen,” however just his claim
may be. Any other adult person may sue the
Queen if he thinks he has a grievance against
her, but the miserable object in a glaring coat
and a helmet isn’t allowed to. A further hard-
ship was that this particular plaintiff didn’t
want to sue the Queen ; he simply wanted to
sue the person who paid his wages —the Vic.
Treasurer. But, legally, it seems, the Vic.
Treasurer is the Queen, though he presents no
visible resemblance to her, and the State
generally is the same Gracious personage, so a
soldier can get no satisfaction from anybody.
If the Queen doesn’t pay his wages, or if she
runs a train over him, or if he is interfered with
in any capacity by her policeman, or her flun-
key, or is bitten by her dog, or kicked by her
horse, or maltreated by her in any way through
any of her agents, he has no redress. And,
considering that the Queen, in a technical sense,
is spread all over the earth, and a soldier is
liable to be injured by her with impunity in
almost any part of the globe, this decision takes
a good deal of the glory out of the military pro-
fession. Apparently, if a military man is
aggrieved by the Queen anywhere the only
satisfaction lie can get is to promptly “stoush”
his sovereign lady through her responsible
representative, and then take the consequences.
Judge Hodges, of Victoria, believing himself
to be inspired to some extent, takes no stock in
juries. A Gippsland man named Ruggles,
charged with wounding a fellow selector’s
animals, was brought in not guilty by two
juries. Before sending the accused about his
business his Honor relieved his feelings in the
following “ piece ” :
You have been tried by two juries and found not guilty
You are fortunate in that you have been tried by a jury
instead of by a Judge. If you had you would have been
convicted. Now, let me caution you, lest next time you
are brought here on a hanging matter.
Ruggi.es was a man who bore a good character
as a quiet, hardworking reclaimer of the wilds ;
the evidence against him was mainly circum-
stantial, and failed to satisfy 24 good men and
true ; the 25th was satisfied, and being a Judge
was, unfortunately, in a position to jump on
the accused with all his feet, and give it out to
the world that the latter at the best is only a par-
ticularly lucky ruffian, and at the worst a possible
murderer. The Bui.i.etin holds that the jury
should have had the power to incontinently send
Hodges to gaol for contempt of court after that
speech. Also, it holds that it should becompetent
for Ruggles, or somebody else, to sue Hodges
for libel and recover heavy damages. Hodges
asserted as distinctly as possible, after the
accused’s character had been legally cleared by
the verdict of acquittal, either that he was
guilty of the mean and cruel offence charged
against him and that if he had been tried
before a Judge only he would have been con-
victed because he was guilty, in which case he
misused the privileges of his position to out-
rageously insult a legally innocent man—or
else that if Ruggles had been tried before a
Judge only he would have been convicted des-
pite his innocence, in which case it was dis-
tinctly implied that Hodges himself and his
brother Judges were quite unfit for their posi-
tions and were howling for the blood of innocent
men. Either way, Hodges should be called
upon to explain himself.
Hear Bulletin, —Your correspondent,
“J.5.R.,” has missed the kernel of the agita-
tion foi evening lectures at Melbourne Univer-
sity. The main objections to that proposal
were really that it would deprive the professors
and the sons of gilt Toorak or Boomville of
their social pleasures. The agitation was not
for additional lectures by a special staff in the
evening, but that all lectures at the University
should, where practicable, be delivered at night
by the ordinary staff.. And, as the University
was granted its charter in order that sound
learning might be given to all classes of the
community without any distinction whatever,
and as the vast bulk of the community cannot
possibly attend in the daytime, the persistence
of the University authorities in arranging the
lectures for unsuitable times comes perilously
near a deliberate breach of the charter. In any
case, the Legislature can and ought to make it
a condition of any further grant of money that
the majority of the lectures should be given at
night. The good of universities is, perhaps,
problematical; but when the people have got
to pay for one, they might as well have the use
of it.—D.
Dear Bulletin, —Collective Man is a blood-
thirsty animal. When any particular offence has
become unusually prevalent, the community
always wants to punish the next person con-
victed thereof, not upon the merits of his own
case, but collectively on behalf of all the other
offenders whom it has allowed to escape.
Which is why while it has agitated
vehemently on many a murder-in-fact—it has
remained so apathetic about Sheridan, who is
only a murderer-in-law, and whose hanging
would be another ghastly Botany Bayism.—

If you want a pleasant evening in W.A.,
writes a Bulletin correspondent, live next dooi
to an Afghan camp. Soon after tea every night a
draped and ghost-like figure steps forward into
the firelight and begins in a low monotone, gradu-
ally increasing in volume and untunefulness, to
enumerate the things he has done, is doing, could
do, and is going to be about to not-do, until naturt
refuses further utterance; whereupon up springs
another child of the desert anxious to “ cap ” tin
deeds of wonder enacted by the other, and repeats
da capo. The howls are accompanied by a banjo-
like instrument of torture, on which an untiring
accompanist tries to guess at and follow the prob-
able refrain of the “singer,” and fails dismally.
About 11.30 p.m. things get exciting, everyone
joining in, in different keys, husky with much
shouting, ending in one long fell screech that puts
even the pibroch of a pack of dingoes in the shade;
Then the suffering neighbors curse for the last
time and go to sleep.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Theatre Royal “ Pat, or the Bells of
Rathbeal.”
Her Majesty’s.... “ Robinson Crusoe.”
Tivoli Rickards’Variety Co.
Sydney Music Hall Variety Co.
MELB. SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s Theatre “ La Belle Therese.”
Opera House Rickards’Variety Co.
A new Australian opera, “ Club Life,”
try A. B. Paterson (the “ Banjo ”) and Ernest
Truman, was executed, or rather lynched, by an
amateur co. at Sydney Lyceum last Thursday.
This was its first stage appearance, and the com-
pany, in a perfectly well-meaning spirit, knocked
it down and jumped on it right from the beginning.
What it would have looked like under better
auspices The Bulletin doesn’t pretend to know, for
it was inaudible to the present writer all through.
The chorus was painted in a more reckless and un-
real fashion than even the amateur chorus gene-
rally attains to, and seemed to feel its position
keenly. There were long, anxious pauses, during
which nobody spoke, and moments of dreadful
anxiety, when the orchestra didn’t know whether
to start out for an encore or not. This hopeless
uncertainty on the part of the orchestra kept
things in an unsettled state all night. After
a decisive burst of applause, the singer
would advance to the footlights and open
her mouth preparatory to starting again, and, as
the orchestra was always too late in making up
its mind, she would close her mouth again, after a
deplorable interval during which the audience
had seriously considered her back tooth, and pro-
ceed to retire. And then, when she was just
vanishing, the music would suddenly make up its
disjointed intellect and commence, and she would
have to scuttle to the front again. After one
decided encore, the large, heavy man of the co.
opened his mouth three times to sing another
verse, and finally, in despair, he started on his
own without any music. Even this broad hint,
however, didn’t get the melody into motion, and
it wasn’t till after he had stopped in disgust that
the choir began in a flurried sort of way. Most of
the performers were nervous and much grieved,
and wore the fixed expression of one who is trying
to look happy in tight boots. Misses Celia Marks,
Alison Rose and Austin, and Mr. Geo. Clibborn
are omitted from the general execration, but, so
far as concerns the rest, it would have been better
if the opera had been struck by lightning on a
Friday and then arrested for being in a public
place with intent to commit a felony. Under all
these painful conditions, the composer’s music
didn’t get much more of a show than the libretto ;
in fact, nothing got much of a show except the
back tooth of the lady who received an encore and
was waiting for the orchestra to start.
** * *
“ His Natural Life ” is announced, at time
of writing, to be succeeded at Her Majesty’s by
three days (Thursday to Saturday)
of “ Robbery Under Arms,” and
after that the pantomime, which
is being built on a scale of great
magnificence, falls due on Tuesday.
The late convict production de-
veloped no new horrors since last
reported on, but all the old horrors
continued in good order till the very
end. Gabbett’s pursuit of the small,
funny convict with the axe, with a
view to having that stunted person
for lunch, was even a more satisfy-
ing performance towards the finish
than it was at first, and the agonised
flight of the small, funny convict
was a sporting event of some in-
terest. By way of furnishing a
highly condensed epitome of the
drama, The Bulletin presents on
this page a sketch of Gabbett’s teeth,
which suggest the spikes on the
gates of hell in a state of disrepair.
Gabbett and his bristles, and his teeth, and
his gory howl are the story of the System
put into the smallest possible space, and that
raucous, death’s-head convict will shriek and
gnash through the long years of history as a
record of the good old Australian Past.
** * *
The co. at Sydney Royal apparently
decided after all to treat the co. at Her Majesty’s
with silent contempt, or something to that effect,
therefore it didn’t put up its rival version of
“ His Natural Life ”as expected. If it had done
so it might possibly have ruined the other show so
utterly that the management would have been re-
duced to keeping a coffee-stall, so it doubtless for-
bore from motives of Christian charity. Also
certain reports that the Royal was going to get an
injunction, or a decree nisi, or an egg-beater, or
some other legal instrument against the other
house, seem to have dropped out of sight for the
present. The “ Silence of Dean Maitland ” filled
m the balance of the season with reasonable suc-
cess, and Johnson Weir’s Dean improved mate-
rially in places as time went on. This week the
co. has moved on to Brisbane, and the Royal is
temporarily deserted.
♦ * * ♦
On Saturday Mr. C. B. Westmacott will
commence a season at the Royal with “ Pat, or the
Bells of Rathbeal,” a musical comedy never before
seen in Australia. Westmacott, Wilfred Shine,
Sweeny, and Misses Harrie Ireland and Florence
Forde are in the cast, so that Patrick commences
with good chances in his favor.
* * *
Sydney Lyceum closed up on Saturday
after a week of that good old play “ Blow for
Blow,” and two nights of “One of Our Girls.”
The theatre now remains empty until the return
of those old reliable favorites, the Brough and
Boucicault Co., on the 26th, and in the interim the
wall-pauer in the gallery will probably be renewed
where the hair-oil mark of the local hoodlum is
painfully visible from the d.c.
* * -* *
The luckless and much-deserted Criterion,
which has had no continuous good fortune since
the departure of the B. and B. combination, is to
be fe-opened on Boxing Night by Pollard’s
Juvenile Opera Co. in “Olivette,” a piece which
hasn’t been seen in Sydney since the days of the
Israelitish captivity, or thereabouts.
** * *
Alice Leamar has been absent a good
deal from the Tivoli owing to indisposition,
and that establishment is shorn of much of
its glory when this wild and jumpy artiste
is not in the bill. Meanwhile, the Ivanoff
Brothers, alleged to be from Russia, have
thrown themselves into the gap, and the Lenton
Brothers are due on Saturday. Rickards is
running a good deal to brothers just now ; possibly
he considers them appropriate to the Xmas season
when brotherly love continues, or might continue
if it had ever got started.
** * *
The Sydney Music Hall put up a really
joyous novelty this week in the person of Walter
Rivers, whose imitation of a pious social festivity
in the school-room, under the eye of the mild new
curate, and assisted by the local shoemaker and
others, proved to be something quite out of the
ordinary rut. There is nothing in all this world
quite so serious as a social evening in an agricul-
tural English schoolroom, under the supervision
of the curate and the “squiah,” and helped on
apologetically by the local bootmaking lout, while
the humble aged inhabitant named Crump, who
carts mud fora living, sleeps with his mouth open
on the back seat. Australia never produced any-
thing even distantly resembling that festivity, and
no music-hall man that The Bulletin knows of
lias attempted to put it on the stage before.
* * *
Mark Twain bas returned to Sydney after
a tour in Maoriland, where he did well nearly
everywhere save at Gisborne. It was too rough to
land in that unreliable roadstead, so the agonised
Smythe saw the profits of the Gisborne visit
vanish over the horizon. Twain will lecture in
Sydney School of Arts on Friday and Saturday
this week. It is to be a new lecture, and will
embody the Australian poem which was unfinished
on his previous visit, and which has since been
completed with much greater difficulty than the
results seem to be worth.
** * *
“La Belle Tberese,” the new comedy-
opera at Melbourne Princess’s, is a one-man show,
having a tangled back-ground of gaudy soldiery
and school-girls in flaxen wigs. The prevailing
character is a village notary, twin brother to the
music-master in “ Nitouche,” and first-cousin to
Mayor Polydore of “The Old Guard.” Circum-
GA BBETT'S TEETH, i n " His Nalural Life” at Her
Majesty's.
stances place the notary in petticoats for awhile
and then he becomes a rampageous caricature of’
Charley’s Aunt. Take him altogether, this lead-
ing personage is a very cheerful joke, very well
interpreted by George Lauri. But “La Belle
Therese” needs to develope its natural re-
sources and profit by experience. Up to
now the story seems' to have been trans-
lated from the French by somebody whose name
wasn’tworth mentioning on the bill, and some of the
lyrics are rocky roads for a warbler to travel. One
naughty song, which probably delighted Paris,
appeals to Melbourne in this sort of form :
Francois, now colonel and commander,
Thought “The discovery of my wife’s infidelity is at
hand ; ”
Soon he saw her wander
Off to keep an appointment she had planned. &c., &c.
The musical numbers are weak in a general way.
The best of them is a concerted piece that may
have been shaken from an opera. The worst is a
music-hallish solo with a jodel refrain. A young
lady sings it in an uncanny voice, regardless
any meaning in the words, and the result is a
double encore. Nothing fetches the Melbourne
gods like a jodel, clever or otherwise. This is a
very second-rate jodel, but no matter. Give them
a little bit of “ tolloiete ! tolloietee !” and the boys
go mad.
* • • •
Miss Florence Young plays the name-part
in “La Belle Therese.” She romps in an easy
second, about twenty yards behind Lauri. If the
part were stronger Miss Young would get much
nearer to the winner, for this is about her most
vivacious effort thus far. Miss Juliet Wray fills
a fairly prominent position in a distinctly foreign
style, Ilhe is an ex-actress of uncertain age, and
she looks the character to the life. Miss Wray’s
style and accent are not exactly French, but the
effect is fur Tin and satisfactory. The other people
don’t go for very much. Little Miss Nash
runs an incidental Purity Society with
success, and Courtice Pounds tries to take
himself seriously as a romantic lover in a prepos-
terous uniform. He doesn’t appear to try hard,
however, though he makes a point of singing a
few lines sweetly at the last moment, by way of
surprising the audience. The chorus is admirable,
as usual.

The Princess’s will be closed on Monday
and Tuesday next, leaving the Opera House to
revel in a bloated monopoly pro tern. The Oross-
ley Sisters, described as Hingland’s “leading
soubrettes and society girls,” make their first Aus-
tralian appearance at the Opera House on Satur-
day. Meantime, Coleman is preparing to transfer
his legs to the Firm’s theatre for Xmas., as per
contract.
** * *
A new High Art Society, constructed by
the indomitable Miss Frances Tyrrell Gill in place
of a similar society which lately died of Philistinic
neglect, gave an inaugural “At Home” at Mel-
bourne Masonic Hall the other night. The
function took the form of a musical evening,
relieved by light refreshment, and an ancient
German botanist threw in a few remarks gratis.
The remarks were unintelligible, but sooth-
ing. A string quartette party, of the first
water, achieved an artistic success with a classic
thingummy in F major. On the other hand, the
vocalism proved to be of a kind which no society
can hold up to acute admiration for long. Never-
theless this society, known as the Illuminated (in
plain Australian language) has The Bulletin’s
best wishes. Melbourne requires all the artistic
light it can get. If the Illuminators really take
an interest in the things they chatter about, and
are anxious to do something definite, this journal
craves permission to say “ Amen.”
*- * * *
Coleman, the leg humorist, suffers from a
perennial fit of depression. He acquired the habit
of melancholy long ago—when he was a racing
stable-boy, sworn to secrecy concerning the ’osses
and their doings on the training-track, at the cold,
grey hour of daybreak. He is never observed to
smile ; his horsey, care-worn face won’t run to it.
In a general way, Coleman’s expression threatens
to burst into tears, and his favorite antidote to
grief is a toothpick, which he chews, jockey-like,
whilst discussing stage business, as though it were
the beloved straw of the horse-profession.
At Poverty Point.
Elton joins the Firm’s comic opera company for the
forthcoming tour. Also, it seems probable that Tapley,
who lingers on in Melbourne, will re-engage himself.
Courtioe Pounds objects to being specified as a “tenor,”
when he is “ an actor and a dancer, with a tenor voice.”
There’s a good deal in this protest. There is also a good
deal in Pounds : all the same, his visit to Australia doesn’t
promise to he a very happy circumstance.
John Lawler has shut down on the deadhead industry
at his Sydney Music Hall. After a brief experience of the
business, he has .come to the conclusion that there are
2200 alleged press representatives doing the variety shows
in Sydney alone, and their hurt expression when they
don't get in on the nod is larger than the front of the
General Post Office.
Polly Emery’s smudged slavey in “ Our Boys ” was a
fine example of mingled coal-dust and vacancy, and one of
the best things in facial expression seen on the Australian
stage. The London cheap slavey is pale through confine-
ment and an unnatural desire to eat the flabby pikelet for
tea: and wild-eyed through reading in the London
Journal about the beautiful Muriel, who was persecuted
by a h’earl; and has the drawn, bony expression of one
who carries coals up six flights of stairs for 16 hours a
day, and Polly Emery’s Belinda represented her to a hair
—the same hair that she drops in the gravy so regularly.
Mark Twain had his grisly hair cut at Napier, and now
relics of the great humorist are being sold at fancy prices.
Nearly lewt. of Mark’s hair has already been disposed of.
Tenor Pounds much feels his fatness and tries to reduce
it by cycling. He is already a conspicuous figure about
Melb. in his knickers, golf socks and the rest of it.
Dacre inherited a goodly fortune from his father, said
fortune being made mostly out of a certain brand of
court-plaster. Dacre, who was both an M.D. and Sc. 8.,
only gained his medical degree because it was the chief
condition of his inheriting under the old man’s will. He
never really oared tor medicine, and probably only re-
tained about enough of his surgical knowledge to make
his suicide effective.
E. J. Lonnen, the comedian whose skin acted so well,
has had his eyeball damaged by a careless cabby’s whip-
lash. While driving in the average hansom it is much
safer to wear goggles.
Apropos the Dacres, thus Francis Bacon : “ There is no
passion in the mind of man so weak but it mates and
masters the fear of death. Revenge triumphs over death,
love slights it, honor aspireth to it, and grief fleeth to it.”
Maggie Moore’s burlesque co. will play Ballarat and
Bendigo for the Xmas-New Year season. “ Little Jack
Sheppard” and Steele Robertson’s “Aladdin Junior” will
be the menu, possibly with a dramatic season later.
George Rignqld is said, on no authority worth men-
tioning, to be contemplating another scriptural drama,

John Coleman, now on the boards at Melb. Opera
House, hut who has been retained for the Princess’s panto.,
is a comedian of experience. He took to the stage at the
age of four, but on reaching the maturer years of eight he
.abandoned it for the racing stables of the celebrated
Matthew Dawson. After 10 years he returned again to
the footlin as, r id has been there more or less ever since.
