George Rignold on a bicycle 1 Henry V. on a rocking-
horse ! Marc Antony in bloomers ! Fancy the oratory of
Caesar’s avenger being hurled at the clumsy cab-driver on
the wood-blocked street by a portly rider capsized along-
side a fruit-barrow! The cycle, however, may take off
some of that too too solid flesh under which G.R. now
groans.
Wallace Brownlow is “resting” on an up-country
station, rounding up cattle in bush-whacking attire.
After fifteen months’ continuous singing with the Royal
Comic Opera Co., Brownlow’s vocal works want oiling,
and he will not be in the bill again until the co. goes on
tour at Xmas.
People who know the circumstances are remarking
that hi a week for an actor who is playing a leading
part in a big Australian theatre is a remarkably mean
wage, anyhow.
A beery person from way back slept out a recent Mel-
bourne performance of the “ Mikado” until the Japanese
potentate made his entrance. Then he woke up, and
asked in a large, husky voice : “ Who’s’im?” His mate
replied in a whisper: “The Emperor of Japan.” Then
the drinky bushman got up on two unsteady legs : “ Wot?
The Emp’rer of Japan ? The bloke as licked the bloomin’
Chows. Hooray ! Three bloomin’ cheers for the F.tnp t., ;
Hooray! Let’s drink his bloomin’ health, l.uo —”
Just here a policeman helped him out.
A certain very peaceful-looking actor, rather well-known
in Australia and now located, Tub Bulletin believes, in
Melbourne, is reputed among his intimates to be a can-
nibal. The unreliable legend goes that he was ship-
wrecked in boyhood, and floated about in a boat with six
others until they grew mad with hunger. Then they
killed the fattest man of the party, and lived on him
till they were picked up. And now, it is said on bad
authority, that actor-man feels a desire for human flesh
every full moon, and sometimes when he is worse than
usual he will leave his dinner and rush out because he
wants man, and the sight of any other meat drives him
frantic.

Wicked Old Man : “ The Flegeltaub has more expression
in her shoulder-blades that there is in Katisha’s whole
elbow. Her liquid eyes are the faintest degree askew, the
kind that glance round a corner, with an expression
always destructive amid the men. Her palish face, with
sharp, peaky nose, may not be expressive till viewed
through a lorgnette, and then it is tantalising, especially
from the limpidity of the eyes, the rather largish mouth,
moist lips, and a general suffusion of not too pronounced
animalism. Juliet’s face makes everybody drivel just like
this.”
Miss Flo. Wilton, the imported lady for Coppin’s next
panto., reached Melbourne last week. She was recom-
mended in glowing terms to Coppin by Billee Barlow, of
the never-to-be-forgotten tights.
Fifty years ago an Irishman was charged at Melbourne
police-court with heaving a large, unripe apple at George
Coppin whilst that popular young comedian was singing
“ Billy Barlow.” The culprit put in a very good defence.
He was tipsy at the time, he said, and, seeing other people
fling things on the stage, he thought a fruit-offering
would be acceptable. Pined five shillings. The law is
conservative in its scale of fines. An Irishman commit-
ting the same indiscretion to-day would be sentenced to
the same old five “ bob.”
The Xmas show at Melb. Princess’s seems to be develop-
ing further comic-opera tendencies as time goes on.
Flora Graupner is in the cast along With Lauri and Flo.
Young, and Leonore Orelli’s part is mainly musical.
Martin Simonsen is storming the Vic. provinces with a
jagged opera co., and way back in the wheat districts,
where “ Maritana ” and “ Girofle-Girofla ” count as latest
novelties from Europe, the dread invader sets ’em up
again at prices to suit the times—two shillings and one,
especially the latter. Son Jules, “the greatest Aus-
tralian tenor,” heads the bill.
Poor old Charles Hall 6, now gone the way of all con-
ductors, was a real good fellow, and many stories are told
of his kindness, writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent. In Salford one Christmas time he came across a
postman on his rounds who had been shouted for by the
people on his beat not wisely but too well, and was in a
profound state of obfuscation. Hallii took the man to his
house, told him to lie down and sleep for an hour or two,
and then set out and, in the fullness of his benevolent
heart, delivered the letters himself.
There was a scene at a Southern railway-station re-
cently in connection with the departure of the remnants
of a variety co. Several of the performers were left be-
hind owing to money troubles, and the “ proprietor,” who
was trying to get on the train, was the centre of an angry
throng. The bass violin man was uttering fearful
language, and the light tenor grabbed the manager’s
luggage and threatened to hit him on the head with it
(and it contained all the jokes, too) unless his £2 ss. was
paid at once. Finally the luggage, consisting of one pair
of tights, a new burlesque, one pair of pyjamas and a
bottle of beer, was left behind to be apportioned among
the crowd. The trombone man grabbed the beer, the
bass violin man received the tights, and a lady skirt-
dancer had to be content with the pyjamas. No one went
within a hundred yards of the burlesque.
The bouncing Lilian Tree got unkind notices for her
London debut in Wagnerian opera. One heartless wretch
said that she sang indifferently, and was not physically
qualified for her part. Lilian is running to flesh, The
Bulletin fears. She was somewhat of a full-blown budlet
before she left Australia.

Imperious Fashion
Ordains that the summer season of 1895 is ended.
Social life, no less than climatological phenomena,
marks the changes of the seasons, .The winter
season is Fashion’s Carnival; the summer its
period of comparative respite. The autumn and
winter seasons are the periods for indoor merry-
makings, festivities and functions, all of which
necessitate much more “ dressing (speaking
technically) than the out-of-door recreations of the
hot months. Howbe.it, each change of season
brings its monster “clearing sales.” Surplus
stocks must be quitted to make room for the new
importations. This is the occasion of the startling
bargains now obtainable at Manning and Co. s,
Oollins-street, Melbourne. The cream of this
fashionable firm’s stock of Millinery Costumes,
Fancy Dresses, Silks, &c., &0., is now being posi-
tively sacrificed to make room for new goods.
Ladies who cannot call in person are invited to
forward orders by post with a full description of
the goods they require. All favors by post, from
any part of Australasia, meet with the promptest
attention of Manning & Co., on the Block,
Ooliins-street, Melbourne. *
Australian Native Association.
Wb come across this heading very often when
perusing the leading dailies, and understood that
it was an association of the aboriginals of Aus-
tralia to get-um more beer and ’bacca from the
various Governments ; but on enquiry find that it
is an association of native-born white people,
formed with an object of forwarding their interests.
We can give all native as well as imported Aus-
tralians an object to gain and congratulate them-
selves over when we tell them that the famous
B. L. Corto Rothschild Cigar is now obtainable
at all first-class tobacconists for - fid. If you have
never tried them do so at once ; they are the same
as supplied to the f a'uous baron. Ask for the
B. 1 1. Corto Rothschild, fid. each.*

Sydney,
December 17, 1895.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—•
The society item of the week was the production
at Sydney Criterion, last Thursday, of a comic
operetta, “ Club Life,” words by “ Banjo ” Pater-
son, music by Ernest Truman, A.C.O. There was
a crowded society house, and the presence of so
much Pure Merino made the dress-circle very hot.
Gov. Hampden was railed off in the_ centre, and
wore a tolerant expression and a white gardenia
in his coat, and some of the suite were similarly
beflowered, which rather looked as if they had all
patronised the same tree. Lady Hampden was
not present, but Miss Brand was on view in mauve
silk with old lace bertha, and her two sisters in
white silk. The V.R. enclosure was made festive
with a huge basket of flowers tied with Australian
blue ribbons, set there for His Excellency to yawn
behind, I suppose. All Darling and Potts Points
were on top in purple and fine linen—with
emphasis on the linen, so far as the men were con-
cerned. One scion of a legal family, deposited
prominently in the front row, had a most buxom
shirt-front which billowed out over his chest in a
manner that gave him a better bust than any dis-
played by the ladies in his party. He was indeed
a sort of object-lesson as to how a fat _ woman
would be likely to look above the belt in male
evening-dress.
From what I could hear of the operetta it
seemed rather promising. Some of the music
was lively, especially “ Oh, ’tis merry when lovers
meet,” “ The Butler’s Song,” “ The Saveloy ” duet
and dance, and the “Society Manners” finale.
The same kind remark applies to the song, “ All
things come to the man who waits ; ” nearly all
the choruses, and especially “ If you don’t love the
lasses then you’re no son of mine,” the “ Alas, for
poor humanity,” duet, and one or two more.
There were a good many, not exactly plums, so
let us say currants, in the operetta, which only
wants a little professional straightening up, the
“story” more accentuated, and a larger supply
of dialogue to be an amusing show. At least, that
is my foggy impression ; but it is difficult to size
up a play so indifferently presented as “ Club
Life ” was. Hardly a line of the book could be
heard in the circle. One gathered the argument
from the programme. The members of the Casso-
wary Club decide to have waitresses in place of
waiters, and society girls disguise themselves and
apply for billets in the hope of getting husbands
out of the deal. One maiden (the heroine, Mabel)
is engaged to a member (Freddy), but parted by a
proud papa, and her amourette supplies what
story there is. In the end a Govt, billet is heaped
on Freddy, who marries Mabel; the waitresses
reveal their true lineage ; the club admits them as
members, and the curtain drops.
There isn’t much the matter with the yarn, but
words fail to express what it looked like from the
front. All the actors and actresses (with the
exception of Mr. Whir) were society amateurs of
the aggravated variety, and it was quite instruc-
tive to see how many damsels who occupy the
front row in society are only fitted to fill the back
row of the chorus when the footlights are in ques-
tion. They certainly looked very smart and
stylish in white linen skirts and Eton jackets
with scarlet vests, white linen collars, hair poudre
and big-buckle shoes. As a parade of local young
ladyhood they were simply immense ; as an
operatic chorus they lacked many things, and
when one of them tottered out in obviously tight
shoes, with an expression of corn-fed agony on her
face, and began in a terrified “ I-shall-cry-m-a-
minute-girls ” sort of voice to chant what might
have been a rattling sort of song, and then
tottered back again, the audience knew what lay
before it.
“ Mabel Valentine ” (heroine) was the brightest
vocalist of the lot, but she over-acted and dressed
badly, and her dancing was a series of elementary
bumps. “Clara Valentine” looked winsome m
white satin brocade and large white plumed hat
(I must say the dressing was excellent on the
whole), and her ballad, “ 0 ’tis merry when lovers
meet,” was artistically sung in a small, sweet
voice, and her encore was the most genuine of all,
though it was a friendly gathering that encored
everything. Clever Miss Winnie Austin sang in
a small voice that required superhuman ears to
catch, but her acting was piquant, and she looked
very pretty, , , , ,
Mr. W. F. Hinchcliffe, R.N., as the butler, had
the best male voice, and bore up admirably against
many trials ; his singing was capita,!, and ms
dancing, a la Gaiety, was very bright. Mr.
Gibson was vocally inaudible as Sir Frederick
Pottspoint. Mr. Geo. Clibborn gave a crisp
sketch of a waiter who gets into Parliament on
the Labor-ticket, and Captain Dangar, as the
secretary, looked like a cash-fielder in a huge
black moustache, and had a walking part. Judged
as a performance the whole thmg might have got
off under the First Offenders Act if it hadn t been
for the orchestra led by the composer himself. A
good composer is not necessarily a »rood cooduc or.
and the way that orchestra wobbled in a weak,
apologetic manner two bars ahead or behind the
people on the stage, occasionally leaving off alto-
gether to wipe the perspiration off its brow while
the hapless singers were left totally unsupported
on the wild high C, was a novelty in musical
experiences, anyhow. My opinion of the whole
thing is that I want to see “ Club Life ” inter-
preted by a smart, tuneful cast and a competent
orchestra with its fiddlers in tune, before sitting
in judgment upon it.
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society gave its
last concert of the season ’95 the other Wednesday
at Sydney Town Hall. Little pamphlets—wisely
distributed after the show so as not to spoil
people’s appetites, for there’s nothing so depressing
as being conscious that one’s entertainers are work-
ing a dead horse —drew attention to the society’s
impecuniosity, and to the fact that unless the
“ subscribers ” paid up the society would have to
melt its badges into old iron and retire into private
life. It is not good biz., however, to make these
dreadful announcements by circular. A long,
thin president or something standing up in a
death-like pause and remarking in hollow tones
that : “Ladies and gentlemen, ruin stares us in
the face ” —pointing meanwhile a meaning fore-
finger at the most unreceipted subscriber of the
lot, is much more effective in a young country.
However, I hope people will pay up, for the
Orchestra is only now budding into something like
proficiency, and it always plays good music. Per-
haps if there was more “ Ta-ra-ra ” and less
“Tannhauser” in its methods, Hazon wouldn’t
now be waving his baton on board wages as it
were.
The last concert was not very, thickly attended
and the dressing was depressing—mainly consist-
ing of fat women in light colors holding round,
red, hard-boiled-looking fans tight up against their
billowing chests, like targets in a shooting-gallery,
and there was no gubernatorial interest in the
front row. There were no ferns on the platform
for the last concert of the series—which rather
looks as if S.A.O.S. stands for “Small Ankles
Only Staged.” Verdure is usually called in to
hide big feet. But, taking a line from the ten-
tacles of some fiddleresses, there is no fear of
seeing any of ’em Bloomering on bikes if what a
wicked man once told me —that you can judge of
a girl’s lower limbs by her wrist and arm —is to be
relied upon. But the music was good. Apart
from the orchestral numbers, Edith King, in pink
and blue, fought a brave fight with the Town Hall
echo ; Rivers Allpress played a dreamy solo with
great effect, and Strauss got in a ’cello solo—an
old Jewish tune, unabridged, that sounded like
the wail of Ikey Mo watching the favorite winning
all the way, so that it must have been an artistic
rendering. “ Ruy Bias ” ( not by Meyer Lutz) and
part of the Eroica symphony, were the main
orchestral dishes, but the most seasonable thing
not mentioned on the programme was Hazon’s
bow, which suggested a timid man putting his
head under a pump.
W. H. Paling and Co.’s Xmas Annual ’95
(13th number), comes forth with a good photo, of
Maud Hobson as Lady Virginia on its gay and
festive cover, and Maggie Moore’s rollicking song,
“ Molly Riley 0,” as chief attraction inside.
There are 16 numbers in the annual which, though
it doesn’t contain any classics, gives you “ Sweet
Marie,” “Good Rhine Wine,” “Ben Bolt” (as
sung by Little Billee), and a host of other popular
things for the insignificant amount of one shilling.
From the same firm comes a gavotte, “ T’Amo
Assai,” by G. Podenzana, and “The Daffodil
Waltz,” by Philip H- Mohr. The gavotte hasn’t
sufficient motive, and the whole thing impresses
you as being so anxious not to be like something
of Sullivan’s that it quite forgets that a tune is
necessary. “ The Daffodil ”is badly named —the
title is too namby-pamby for this dashing, sparkle-
some dance-tune, which takes strong wrists to do
full justice to its exhilarating chords.
Mdlle. Trebelli’s second concert at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, last Friday, was V.R. Lady Hamp-
den in black vestments, with a black bow and a
flame-color aigrette as her inevitable head-dress,
had the worst seat in the house, as usual, right
under the singers’ noses. She was accompanied
by two daughters, dressed in water-ice colors, and
the regular basket of flowers (managerial) com-
pleted the V.R. staging. There was a good
attendance, but the dressing in the reserved places
set my teeth on edge. First, a diamond dagger
and a bare neck, and then a bonnet and a linen
collar cheek by jowl alongside of it, positively
jolted my sense of fitness.
If Mdlle. Trebelli (good name for a soprano,
isn’t it ?) had warbled with her hands behind her
back she might have posed for Trilby. She wore
her dark hair parted in the middle, with a tricky
little row of diamonds on either side. Only the
young and blooming can afford to part their hair
like that. What looks like a mere bridle-track
across a young woman’s head turns to a regular
stock-route on the middle-aged. Her gown was
pale-blue satin with lines of gold passementerie,
and she blazed with Paris diamonds. Mdlle. T.
has a free, high, large soprano voice, under perfect
control, and the penalty she paid for her tuneful-
ness was that her four solos, including the jewel
song (Gounod) and “Oh ! Quand je dors ” (Liszt),
were lengthened into eight. Besides being a cul-
tivated vocalist, Mdlle. has the virtue of intro-
ducing songs new to Australian concert-goers,
especially in her encores ; and she is well supported
and diversified by Mr. Pianissimo Weir (tenor),
W. T. Barker, the clever Melbourne harpist, and
pianist Beaupuis, of the tossing mane. J. Edward
Sykes manages the Sydney season.
The members of the St. John’s Ambulance
League (grafted on to Australia by the first Lady
Brassey, and foster-mothered in Sydney by little
Lady Carington) held their annual war-dance at
Sydney Town Hall last Saturday afternoon, with
Lady Hampden to give the medals and stimcates
and a loud brass band kicking up its heels in the
eastern gallery. There was not a huge attendance,
and the occasion had no sartorial significance.
Lady Hampden wore a simple steel-grey crepqn,
with white belt, collar, and cuffs, black-and - pniß
bonnet, fawn suede gloves, and a pink rose in her
buttonhole. Her Excellency is neither a speech-
maker nor a speech-taker, for while the chairman
mumbled some statistics, she carried on an
animated chat with Bishop S. Smith (no relation
of mine, remember), whose well-filled gaiters were
quite a feature of the platform.
Lady Hampden is not a potentatess of the
Universal Pumphandling variety. She gave the
medals and things to both men and women with
a few “ well-chosen,” inaudible remarks and a
honeyed smile, but the usual spasmodic hand-
shake was left out of the bill—to the recipients
great relief, I have no doubt. Even as it was,
some of them seemed to have great difficulty in
getting off the platform alive, and one of the
amateur first-aiders (a man), who got a medal,
kicked Miss Brand’s white parasol half-a-yard in
his struggles to get away, and never thought of
apologising, but left the lady to grab her property
for herself. The prettiest part of the ceremony
was when Lady H. pinned the order of St. John
of Jerusalem on to the collar of the athletic Dr.
Reuter Roth, while the audience whacked the
floor with its umbrella. The private secretary, in
white spats, accompanied the vice-regal party, and
the usual committee basket of flowers did its best
to trip everybody up. The giving of that basket
was amusing. A committee-lady poked it under
Lady tf.Vuo.se at the wrong moment, just as she
was talking to the League’s pocket-Providence,
Dr. Vandaleur Kelly, and when, at length, her
Excellency turned to dutifully gush over the
flowers (what a lot of floral superlatives a
Governor’s wife must use in a busy week !) the
committee lady had fled, leaving her Ex. to pour
out her delight to empty air !
The most-stared-at individual on the platform
was the third bride of a hoary politician who was
there too, leaning heavily and picturesquely on a
stick, though I always understood that a young
wife did away with the necessity for a staff. The
latest addition to the ranks of local nobility wore
grey and pink like a rosy cockatoo, and her hat
was wonderfully trimmed with feathers.
At Sydney; Lyceum Miss Dauvray closed her
so-so season last Saturday with “One of Our
Girls ” and the second act of “ Our Boys.” “ One
of Our Girls ” is a charming play, and the dressing
on Saturday was quite en suite. Miss Dauvray,
as Kitty, wore a cool-looking creamy silk dress
with lace insertion for the first act, and Miss
Nobte, as Julie, was innocence itself in white
muslin with weak little flounces. I have often
noticed that stage-innocence never knows exactly
how to dress. Kitty’s pink satin and chiffon with
gold sequins and her handsome wrap had a good
show in the 2nd act, and Julie wore yellow brocade.
Indeed, the only discordant note in the whole en-
joyable play was the bald spot on top of the
gallant “ cap ting.” Surely he could have trained
a bit of crape hair over it ? In “ Our Boys ” the
most amusing thing was King Hedley’s get-up
d la Chief Justice Darley. Among the Lyceum
audience was Miss Elsie Cameron in a wonderful
toque and a pink-silk blouse, accompanied by her
husband, Mr. J. S. Cowell. They are en route for
Bananaland.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH
A record. The pure merino flock at
Booborowie, S.A., shore 51,703 grown sheep for an
average of 91b. wool per head, and 15,509
lambs for 31b. lloz. per head; or a general average
on the total mixed sheep and lambs of 81 b.
Moral —careful scientific breeding.
A Maoriland labourer, whose humpy had
been robbed on three occasions while he was away
at work, fixed up an elaborate man-trap with a
gun attached and waited results. Four days
later, when he came home, he found the result
in the shape of a highly respectable neighbour
lying dead on the stair. The ingenious person
arrested on a charge of murder, and got two
years for exercising the right to set man-traps on
his own premises.
The other day a Mrs. Trenchard, of Guild-
ford (N.S.W.), gave herself up to the police for
killing her husband in self-defence with an axe,
the husband being a fiend who bit and kicked and
otherwise ill-used her and horribly ill-treated the
children. Then Sydney Telegraph described
her as “a heart-broken, wild-looking creature,
who seemed hardly to understand her position ...
she seemed listless and almost unconcerned.” The
Herald also said that she was “ a quiet, heart-
broken-looking creature.” Next day it was
alleged at the inquest that Trenchard was a mild,
hard-working man, that his wife sometimes
drank heavily, and that instead of being
killed in self-defence the old man had been
butchered in his sleep. The Herald wisely
dropped the matter there, but the Telegraph
repented of its former description, and stated that
Mrs. Trenchard was “ a spare woman, with a hard
mouth, large eyes, and emotionless face.” The
point of view makes all the difference. When the
average man hears that somebody has committed
atl awful axe-butchery he immediately finds that
the accused looks just like the person who would
have done that sort of thing. And if the same
individual is alleged to be a martyred saint he, or
she, always looks that way also. Anyhow, the
second description could have been condensed a
lot. “ She looks like a woman who habitually
kept a boarding-house” would have covered every-
thing.
The Dean “Conspiracy.”
Now that VV. P. Chick has been acquitted of
all share in the Dean “ conspiracy,” almost
everybody in the community is anxious to
know on what possible grounds he was ever
dragged into the case. The Crown did not
produce one shred of really direct evidence
against him, and hardly any that could be
called even moderately indirect, and, consider-
ing the vigor with which the prosecution was
conducted, the public naturally takes this to
mean that no evidence of any value was avail-
able. Not one among the multitude of Crown
witnesses alleged that Crick had spoken
anything, or written anything, or done
anything which bore any real semblance of
conspiracy, and even the police who searched
his office and ransacked his papers failed to
find one incriminatory document. The jury
distinctly expressed its opinion as to the utler
futility of the charge against Crick by
acquitting him, apart from ihe other prisoners,
without waiting for the Judge’s summing up,
which evidently implied that it regarded his
case as beyond all argument. Also, the Judge
fully concurred in the acquittal, though the
Crown’s representative opposed it most vigor-
ously. Yet the Crown tlid not simply serve
Crick with a summons, as is generally done in
trivial cases, when the accused is a person
whom no one could reasonably suspect of an
intention to run away, though considering the
case it produced against him, or rather didn t
produce against him, even a summons would
have seemed hardly justifiable. Instead, it
arrested him, and at the Police Court the
Crown, or someone who purported to act
on its behalf and whose act was never repudi-
ated, made a great effort to prevent the grant-
ing of bail. If it had been successful in this
respect Crick would have lain in gaol for
weeks awaiting his trial, arid the consequent
loss of practice would probably have ruined
him. Also, he was put to great trouble,
anxiety and expense ; his practice of necessity
suffered severely ; and he was confined for two
weeks during the progress of the trial. The
fact that the Government which instituted this
extraordinary case, and pursued it in such a
remarkable fashion, was one to which Crick
has been always politically opposed, and con-
cerning which he has used much aggressive
language, makes the circumstances all the more
unfortunate. Botaiiy Bay justice has had in-
numerable slurs cast upon it of late, and it
can’t very well afford to give even a semblance
of excuse for further obloquy.
Richard Speight has resolved to carry his
endless case against Syme to the Privy Council.
Speight has more fight in him than a bull-ant,
and if his funds only permit, his case will last
Syme’s lifetime and be handed down to his
descendants.
Having simultaneously reached its first
anniversary and its first libel-action, Sydney
Licensing Guardian celebrates both events by
appearing in an improved form with a colored
cover, and is now a neat and rather interesting
little publication.
No doubt people have lately noticed a
reflection in the western sky (writes “ Blaok-
swanalian ”to The Bulletin). Albert F. Calvert
has come. His “fourth tour” of Westralia has
begun, and the objects thereof are announced in
flaring headlines and a three-col. interview in a
local paper. The young man is about to splash on
a scale never before attempted. Bursting with
mighty schemes for the booming of W.A., and
conscious that the man who arrives like a comet
has bigger chances than he who enters unobtru-
sively by the back-door, Mr. Calvert called out a
special train from Albany to Perth, although he
might, by waiting a little while, have travelled
almost as quickly by the ordinary express. He
explained to the interviewer, who was curious why
so great a man should travel by ordinary P. & O.
steamer, that he had no time to wait for the com-
pletion of his steam-yacht. Then as to his
schemes. The Govt, is to be asked to establish a
kind of W.A. exhibition in London; cost
.£20,000, of which Mr. Calvert will supply
half. There is to be a W.A. general infor-
mation bureau in London, which he will run
for a year gratis just for the fun of the thing,
resigning all business appointments “ in order to
have a free hand to run the agency for all it is
worth ; ” also, he will guarantee the Govt, against
loss for the first year, or longer if necessary.
Next, he will —also at his own expense—send out
an exploring party “to complete the task which
the Elder expedition was sent out for and failed
to accomplish.” He will present W.A. with the
nucleus of a national portrait-gallery and assist
in providing the requisite building if necessary.
Finally, he has named one of his racers “ Westra-
lian,” and he has a list of other local names ready
for his mokes to carry to the honor and glory and
advt. of W.A. as his racing career progresses !
Naturally, he has-been received in the West as
every visitor is received from whose every pore
sovereigns ooze and in whose wake is a trail of
champagne-bottles. Now, what is to be done
with A. F. Calvert ? So far, every man in
Westralia professes to “ see right through him.”
They scoff at his exploring feats, and when he is
dined and given costly presents they laugh in their
sleeves and dig one another in the ribs. But he
will advertise the province, say they. A river of
British capital will flow in his wake. And so he
gyrates through the province as its privileged
and hall-marked guest. And for the present, not-
withstanding that his liege subjects profess to re-
gard him as a gilded guy, he is king of W.A. It
is indeed difficult to withhold some sort of admira-
tion for a man who can become an explorer with-
out exploring, who can harness a Lord Mayor and
all manner of small fry eminent in the boom-
world to his chariot, who can manoeuvre so that
the hundreds who deride him in secret assist in
public to hoist him on high. Westralia is badly
Calvert-bitten, but as yet the symptoms are latent

UNRIVALLED.
Society
The project for making the chief civic
printer or draper of Sydney a Lord Mayor is the
sort of thing which will prevent any person with a
sense of humor from taking the mayoral billet in
future. The mediaeval lord saved himself from
becoming wholly ridiculous by wearing a suit of
armour and sticking a spear through anybody who
criticised him too much. He might be no better
than any other man, but as he was in a position to
drop heavily on the person who said so he at least
kept up a semblance of importance. The modern
lord, when he is criticised by small boys in the
street, must either walk away rapidly, or run after
the small boys with a walking-stick, or call for a
large Irish policeman. And the modem lord
mayor, who is a kind of bogus temporary lord that
sells slop clothing or wire nails, is a mixture of
nothing in particular—a sort of imitation peer
who bosses the corporation rubbish-tip and argues
over drainage contracts, and when the small
newspaper boy asks him where he got that hat he
has no ancestral pride to fall back upon. The
hardest thing in all this world to keep up is a
dignity that isn’t big enough to help to keep itself
up, and which has to be held up by main force all
the time.
Photo. by The Crown Studios.
Miss NELLY MORTYNE.
The other day, at St. Kilda, Governor
Brassey was confronted by a dusty man in broken
boots and an infamous hat. “ Gould you give a
feller a job?” asked the stranger, abruptly. “I
am afraid not, my man,” said his Excellency ; “ I
am not an employer.” “Oh, I jes’ thought I’d
ask you,” said the shabby man, wearily, “ seein’
I’d asked every other man in Melbourne.”

Lady Brassey is playing Lady Bountiful in
Melbourne, regardless of expense. You appeal to
a man’s heart through his stomach ; her Ladyship
appeals to the women’s hearts through the
stomachs of their children. She is picnicking and
feasting the State school scholars of Melbourne in
a most lavish manner, and every second youngster
in the suburbs has by this time acquired an inward
pain, induced by the liberality of “me lady.” A
special excursion and particular gorge have been
arranged for those children who sang “ God Save
the Queen ” at their Excellencies on the occasion
of the famous landing at St. Kilda. The I'rasscys
evidently recognise the fact that tin poor
“ kiddies” were not to blama for that discordant
grovel.

N.S.W. Gov. Hampden is said to have a
deadly loathinv of th~ well-meaning noodles who

Justice wasn’t leaden-footed, the other
day, in Dunedin (M.L.). On the 3rd inst. a man
and woman were “pulled” before the S.M. for
theft of a watch and chain. He was discharged,
and she was committed for trial. In another por-
tion of the courthouse the Grand J ury was sitting
on the Supreme Court list; Maggie Buckingham’s
case was included therein from the lower court;
the influential citizens found a true bill; case
came on in the afternoon, the jury found the
prisoner guilty, and the Judge sent her up for
four years—all on the 3rd of December. If it had
only been a capital case and she had been hanged
in the evening, everything w uld have been com-
plete.
The Victorians have
already decided that
Baron Brassey is a slow
and leadeh speaker and
very painful to listen
to. He clears his throat
perpetually, and says
“ hem ” and “er ” and
“ah ” more often than
anything else, aqd he
has all the fire and
velocity of a sand-punt.
He never smiles when
he is speaking, but re-
gards his audience with
a patient expression like
unto a wooden ham, and
talks in a dull, ponderous
monotone. The ancestral
piek-and-shovel and re-
clamation job have eaten
into Brassey’s soul and
made him inexpressibly
heavy. Even when he
makes a light remark it
sounds as if the observa-
tion was a large mass of
earth in a railway cut-
ting, and he was re-
moving it at so much
the cubic yard.

The cablegrammsr sends out the thrilling
news that Brassey’s brother’s eye, which was
blackened at a tennis match, has been examined
by a doctor, and proves not so bad as was sup-
posed. Hardly anyone at this end knew that
there was anything wrong with Brassey’s brother’s
eye, or that Brassey had a brother, or that the
brother had an eye, and it is truly sickening to
think that because Brassey is the vice-regal figure-
head of one extreme corner of this continent, all
Australasia is required to read about the eye of
his little brother at “ ’Ome.” This country agreed
to endure Brassey on certain fixed conditions, but
his brother’s eye wasn’t in the contract.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
At a recent Adelaide Criminal Sessions,
out of 20 cases there were 19 convictions and one
nolle prosequi —a record'performance. This is
described by the local press as “a credit to the
Crown Law Department,” and “to his Honor the
Chief Justice, owing to the remarkable clearness
with which he put the cases to the juries.” And
not a word is said about the co-operation of these
19 prisoners in being so unanimously guilty. Yet,
unless it is implied that some of them were inno-
cent, and were wrongly convicted owing to the
vigor of the Crown Law Department and the
remarkable clearness of his Honor, they had more
to do with the creditable proceeding than any-
body else.

is a muscular Ker.st an, with a penchant for box-
ing exercise, and the ladies of the choir mostly

object ot the demonstration is to prove that Christ
Church, South Yarra, is mov.ng with the times —

A story comes from Ballarat (Vic.) of the
wife of a local councillor who yawned so pro-
digously in church on a recent Sunday that she
badly dislocaled her jaw, and had to be hurried
away to the h ispital in a cab. Thus far there is
an air of simple truth about the narrative that is
pleasing ; the only remarkable circumstance is that
such accidents are so rare in church. Arrived at
the hospital-gates, the lady was being assisted to
alight when the cabman’s horse moved and she
was thrown into the road. When she was picked
up she was found to have cut her forehead, but the
dislocation had been put right by the violent con-
cussing of the jaw against the gutter.

On German farms in Southern Queens-
land the whole family may often be seen at work
in the fields. Girls of 15 and 16 mend the fences,
pull the corn, dig post-holes, and even plough.
Go on the land, young woman !

Knocking about the corridors of the
largest wooden building in the world (the Wel-
lington Government offices, Maoriland) may be
s :en many a face which was well-known in the
roaring West Coast days of the 70’s, and
digger Dick Seddon always nods to these
pathetic wrecks as he passes by.
Amongst them may be quoted a
typical case. He was worth thou-
sands per annum in his Westland
flush days. Then he came down
with the times, drifted to the
Sydney Domain, doing nightly
perishes and daily starvings.
Premier Seddon heard of his
fallen lines, sent for him, put
a suit of clothes on his back,
filled him with good victuals—
and then fixed him up in a
billet among the johnnies in
the largest wooden building in
the world. Premier Dick’s
old-time friends have an air
of the bush and the billy
about them, and they
are mostly an-
cient and rather

the old digger
and the new
johnny don’t

ringham line (Vic.)

train was stopped
and some time spent
in extinguishing tho
conflagration. In
consequence, another
train, in which Lady
Brassey and the bishop of Melbourne were
riding, was delayed for 20 minutes. Some
grovelling persons have since been bursting
with indignation because the railway officials did
not move the first train on to a siding before mak-

TWO IN A BOAT.
O’er moonlit waves we float along,
Watching the shore lights gliding by ;
Across the water comes a song,
Wild, sad, and sweet! A little sigh,
Begotten in that region strange—
The mystic marge ’twixt smiles and tears—
Flutters the soft breast ’gainst my arm ;
Encircled by your dark eyas’ charm
1 wish the moments might be years !
We drift along unseen—unheard—
O’er waters scarcely ripple-stirred ;
Would we might float out far away
Oyer the ocean, lips and lips,
Till Idealh our light of love eclipse,
Then sleep—for ever and for aye !
Sydney.
ZADIG.

UNENPLOYED: “ Well, if you can’t give us
a job, boss, d’ye think you could sneak

hour?”
The law arrived at one of its most remark-
able decisions in the Wilson (Melb.).
After a long investigation it decided that the hus-
band had been so utterly bad that, viewing his
conduct by itself, his wife was fairly entitled to a
divorce. Also, it resolved that the wife had been
so wicked that when she was considered separately
the husband had every possible right to a separa-
tion. And then, considering them jointly, it
decided that because both of them were entitled to
a divorce neither of them ought to have it. Also,
they couldn’t have it because they] both wanted it;
it only one of them had wanted it, it might have
been granted, but the fact that both wanted it was
fatal. The law apparently regards divorce, not as
relief to the person who wants it but as a punish-
ment to the one who objects to it, and however
much one of the parties may deserve it, unless the
other one kicks violently the 44 statoots ” view the
matter with grave suspicion. And when two
people have acquired such a solid and unanimous
loathing that the presence of each one incites the
other to sin and drink and to be a general blot on
society, then the law holds that they are just the
sort of people l who should be compelled to live and
wrangle together all their lives. It is_a weird and
woolly kind of logic, but the law is a curious
animal and isn’* built like anything else.
* * • *
A citizen o± Onehunga (M.L.), where the
female mayors come from, came home one night
recently and found a male stranger on the pre-
mises under the sort of circumstances that the
Social Gimlet Society is interested in. His son
was along with him, and the pair took the law in
their own hands, and severely whaled the inter-
loper. Then they tore all his clothes off him, and
the old man went away to look-for a policeman,
taking the garments with him as a solid guarantee
that the stranger would not escape, and leaving
his son on guard. It was at this stage that things
began to go wrong. The undressed stranger
attacked the son, and after a dreadful conflict, in
which £5 worth of damage was done to the draw-
ing-room furniture, he laid Jhat person out. Then
he hurriedly dressed himself in the old man’s
Sunday clothes and belltopper, and ran for parts
unknown, and when the house-father came back
with a vast constable, all he found was general
wreckage and a dazed son sitting in the fireplace.

A Belfast journal—the Northern Whig—
is responsible for the following fairy-tale: “Sir
John Madden, Chief-Justice of Victoria, built
with his own hands the carriage in which he drives
to the law-courts, and the yacht in which he
cruises about the harbor at Melbourne on Satur-
day afternoons.” Madden, as it happens, doesn’t
drive to the law-courts in his kerridge, nor keep a
yacht for cruising purposes, also one glance at his
noble presence and his waxed mo suffices to con-
vince any ordinary observer that Madden would
prefer death itself to the job of constructing a
yacht or a barouche, even if he had learned how
to do those things. The story is headed ‘ 4 A Chief -
Justice who builds his own yacht.” It will in-
evitably lead to more paragraphs of a similar
nature. We live in hopes of hearing soon that
Madden has achieved a marble bridge across the
Yarra with his own fair hands.

At Hawthorn (Melb.) there is a Chinese
Mission attached to St. Colomb’s Church. It is
supervised by several very nice young ladies, each
of whom undertakes the education of one Chow.
The curriculum comprises the English language
and the Bible. As a rule, however, the Chow,
being of the fish and vegetable variety, leaves after
the English lesson, that being all his sordid soul
sees any commercial value in. The Mission meets
every Sunday afternoon, and it is a coincidence
that although many differed Chows attend, there
are never more than sufficient to make one for
each fair teacher. Sc/ue day the fell hand of
leprosy, or somethin# even worse, will smite St.
Colomb’s, and then*——

in Menzies* hotel, Melbourne. The cause of the

soda-water curse that killed him.
Brisbane Government House is very old
and mouldy, and has been long sinking
into decay. The horse-hair is sticking
out of the furniture, and the carpets
are faded and threadbare, and
there are grease-marks left by
the heads of many Governors
on the walls of the drawing-
room, and the floor is worn
into a channel with the feet
of many heavy premiers
coining in to arrange for an
extension of the Kanaka
traffic. The prospective ar-
rival of a fresh Gov., with
the varnish scarcely dry on
his title, has suddenly aroused
the Ministry to seriously con-
sider this ramshackle state of
things, and it has been decided
to refurnish the place through-
out before Lammgton arrives,
lest the chairs give out suddenly,
and the bright young potentate
comes down with a hard wallop
on the floor. The refurnishing will make a pretty
serious hole in Nelson’s surplus, by the way.

An Adelaide father whose child died whilst
he was trying to cure it by faith to the neglect of
medical advice, has been bullied by a heterodox
coroner, and threatened with a charge of man-
slaughter. Faith may move mountains, but not
microbes. The man who prays for rain to water
his young corn runs to a doctor with his gumboil.

A Victorian, who recently stole and
pawned the bassoon of the man next door, pleaded
in defence that he did it to abate a nuisance, the
adiacent person being an execrable and persistent
player who groaned and wheezed on his instrument
far into the night. Nevertheless, he got 14 days,
and the outlaw with the dreadful instrument gets
nothing. Some day, The Bulletin hopes, this
community will recognise that the bassoonist and
the trombonist are enemies of society, and will
decree that these instruments shall be played only
in the middle of a cleared space of 640 acres, and
then only after six days’notice, and in the presence
and under the supervision of the police, and by a
person bearing on him a £5 revenue stamp which
shall entitle him to play for one day only.

The S. M. Herald, reporting the recent shark
and boy encounters at Balmain, was so anxious
to incorporate with the story the fact that on a
former occasion Lady Carington sent a_ letter of
sympathy to a similarly-afflicted Balmain parent
that its English became misty, and the reader was
left in doubt as to whether the shark or Lady
Carington’s letter caused the “ terrible disaster.”
* * * •
A Maoriland man wants a divorce on the
ground that his wife is always away touring the
country in pants on a bicycle, and he hardly ever
sees her. When he does see her she generally has
her bicycle pants on, and is such a dispiriting
spectacle that he had rather she remained away.
“ Frequent desertion on a bicycle ” will soon have
to be made a valid reason for divorce.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The balance-sheet of the E.S. and A. Bank,
which is now to hand, throws very little new light on the
troubles of that embarrassed institution. The loss on the
year s operations is over £16,400. The gross takings
(£234,978) are £11,500 less for the complete year than they
were for the previous eleven months ; of this sum interest
on £168,687, and the expenses of manage-
ment (£82,723) more than swallow up the remainder. The
new deposits have increased slightly, but the total deposits
have declined ; capital has increased by £208,000; the
note issue shows a very large falling off, and the cash on
hand has been reduced by £145,000. The troubles of the
E.S. and A. are not caused by the great amount of un-
invested money on hand, for this item (£898,000) is com-
paratively moderate ; moreover, its holdings of State and
municipal securities now stand at £55,000 against £113,000
a year ago, so that it would appear to have been realising
its old investments instead of looking for new ones.
During the year £51,000 of terminable deposits have been
redeemed “ on terms advantageous to the bank,” but what
these terms may be like, or how much larger the loss
would have beep if they hadn’t been arrived at, do not
appear.
Ihe only thing which the balance-sheet shows
very definitely is that the E.S. and A., with a larger
capital than last year, less money lying idle, and £94,000
less fixed deposits to pay interest upon, has come out a
great deal worse than it did in ’94. The low rate of in-
terest is ascribed as the reason. Whether this heavy loss
of interest involves any serious loss of principal is a
matter of conjecture. Anyhow, the board seems to con-
template a probability of things becoming a great deal
worse than they are. In the last disastrous year it
earned 3J per cent, on its A 2,009,000 odd of inscribed
deposit stock, yet it asks for power to cut down the in-'
terest, if necessary, as low as U per cent., which will make
the average rate on all its old fixed deposits £2 11s. 6d.
per cent. This certainly looks as if the institution had
no very exuberant confidence in its own future just at
present.
If the E.S. and A. Bank gets through with its
new arrangement, it is rumored that one or two other
institutions, which are now leading a somewhat precarious
existence in the struggle to pay the reconstructed 4A per-
cent., may shortly follow its example. Three banks, at
all events, want a reduction of interest rather badly, and
one of them wants an extension of time as well; while
another desires to arrive at some arrangement whereby it
can pay off part of its deposits in advance, and thereby
reduce its outlay for interest and get rid of several tons of
gold which it dare not invest for fear they shouldn’t come
back in time. Another bank is reported to be making a
good part of its profits nowadays by buying up its own
depreciated deposit receipts—a device which should be
more widely adopted. Altogether, most of the recon-
struction schemes are out of date, and want to be put on
an entirely new footing.
■ • ♦ •
Chaffey Bros., Limited, the great Mildura irri-
gation concern, is about to wind up with a possible
view to reconstruction, and the Vic. Government
is pushing through a Bill to make Mildura a self-
governing district under the control of a Water Trust.
The scheme was a luckless, ill-managed affair from the
beginning. The founders were so dead sure that all the
land would be taken up that, instead of selling the blocks
close to the river first, they allowed settlers to purchase
scattered selections anywhere and everywhere, and when
the intermediate land remained unsold the co. had to
make water-channels at great cost over long reaches of
vacant land to irrigate a few outlying settlers. The
channels were cheaply and inefficiently made, and the
water leaked away, and the irrigation was more or less
unsuccessful. Many of the orchards proved useless after
years of waiting, through the planting of worthless trees
by settlers who hardly knew one vegetable from another.
There was no railway, and water-carriage was difficult and
expensive. The wine-making industry was discouraged by
the curse of Prohibition and Cold Tea. The co. worked on
money borrowed in London at 5 per cent. The selectors
tell behind in their payments to the extent of some
£40,000. Cash became an unknown quantity at Mildura,
and the settlement degenerated into a region of barter,
discouragement and sly grog-shops. According to the
last balance-sheet, the co. had over, a,000,000 of liabilities,
no cash, and no assets except Mildura, which was fast
dropping to pieces. Finally, the dreadful Real Estate
Bank sued for two P N’s amounting to a miserable £4OO
which had been dishonored for over two years—apparently,
m all that time, Mildura, with over £1,000,000 of alleged
assets, hadn’t been able to raise £4OO cash and the luck-
less company, which has apparently ruined the Chaffeys
and most other people concerned, is going to close up.
• • • *
1 he little National Bank of Tasmania reports a
net profit for the half-year, after paying income-tax, of
£3929, against £3790 last half, and £4524 the one pre-
viously. A 5 per cent, dividend absorbs £3BOl, and the
balance, with £1596 on hand from last year, makes £1724
carried forward. The National is a concern of 10 years’
standing, with £152,040 of capital and a moderate reserve
of £22,500, which might have been larger but for an
undue anxiety to pay 8 per cent, dividends in the early
years of its existence.
* » * *
The Maoriland Midland Railway Co., whose
arbitration is now in progress, claims £1,817,900 and in-
terest from the M. L. Government. If it wins, therefore,
the account against the province will stand something
like this; Cash, £1,817,1400; interest, about £25,000;
land already granted to the co., 373,000 acres ; loss
through the locking-up for 10 years of 6,000,000 acres,
amount difficult to estimate ; legal expenses, probably a
good many thousands ; and a whole decade of worry,
anxiety, profanity, and hope deferred. And against all
this the sole assets will be 82 miles of railway in three dis-
connected scraps, which could have been built by the
State, with reasonable economy, for the value' of the land-
grant alone. As a disastrous lesson in land-grant railway-
construction, the Midland is a very large thing indeed.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The National Building, Land and Investment
Co. (Sydney) is going the way of Sydney building societies
in general. It proposes to reconstruct on the following
rather steep lines ; that all interest due on deposits at
31st December, ’95, shall accrue at present rates ; that no
interest whatever shall be payable from that date until
December, ’97, and thereafter it shall be payable at the
very humble rate of 2J per cent, per annum. The
principal is to fall due at no date in particular, but when-
ever sufficient cash is accumulated to pay 2s. in the £ the
amount is to be distributed among the patient depositors.
Also, the board wants authority to take deposit-receipts
m payment of land, which is eminently advisable ; and it
also wants power to pay off any deposit at any time “ on
terms to be mutually agreed upon ” which proviso seems
to open up such possibilities for some future board’s uncle
to get his money early that it. wants ia lot of Thinking over. 1
• . • " .*. . * "
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Go. (Sydney), oiie of the few concerns in that city
which have not taken refuge in reconstruction, reports a
profit for the year of £2651, and £2197 from last year
makes the amount available £4848. Of this £550 go to
reservo (making it £6250), £3OOO are written off the assets
to allow for depreciation and possible losses, and £1298
are carried forward. The Intercolonial hopes to resume
dividends shortly; meanwhile, it has an overdraft of
£3312, and land-values in the eastern provinces are still
falling, and the new land-tax will strike it witli a dull thud
in a few months, and its profits are mostly from the Wes-
tralian business, where the boom may get spinal paralysis
at any time, and therefore it proposes to go slow.

A British co. is to be formed to take over the
Auckland (M.L.) tramways. Sooner or later a British co.
takes over everything on this planet. Auckland had a
fine chance to establish a municipal tramway system and
fooled it away.
, " • ♦ • •
In the last 23 weeks the earnings of the
Victorian railways have fallen £56,524 below last
year’s record for the same period. And there is so
little produce to carry, owing to the drought, that the
deficit promises to get bigger each week all through the
summer.
* • » ♦
The N.S.W. railways, which were doing well
during the first half of this year, have suddenly begun to
mie up a promising' deficit. Last week’s returns, were
£16,610 below those of the corresponding week of ’94.
Melbourne soft-goods importers have been busy
of late, exporting their rotten old woollen stuffs to branch
houses in other provinces (say W.A.) for the sake of the
“drawback,” which will suffer a fall of 10 per cent, on
January 1. The 40 per cent. “ drawback ” claimed from
the Government on account of these aged, obsolete
materials exceeds, in many cases, their market value ;
also in many cases, where the invoices are shockingly un-
truthful, it exceeds the original cost, including duty. A
professional Freetrader doesn’t reveal the trade tricks
with which his importing friends rob the country, even if
he be quite alive to the swindle, and so the wholesale
rogues wax fraudulently fat.

Present price of potatoes means a gain of close
on £50,000 to Vic. Western district growers from this

• • • •
Coal trade shipping co’s reckoned they had a
soft snap, the Other day. They combined in dealing with
the new Westralian Govt, coal contracts, to spring ss. a
ton on the previous year’s price, and split the work. But
again a well-laid scheme suffered a “ knock out.” Ship-
ping broker Slee, of Adelaide, having the “ office,”
tendered 3s. under the combine’s rate, and won. Big
offers from the other crowd have failed to buy Slee off.
His backers have got several big cargo tanks and will
probably knock the bottom out of the W.A. freight ring.

,N.S. W. Judge Manning’s suggestion that the Eng-
lish liquidator of the Federal Bank should be got rid of is
an unwarrantable piece of provincial impudence. The
Federal Bank wds mainly a Victorian and British calamity,
and, considering the history of its management, the
Britishers are entitled to any sort of “ confidence ” they
can derive from a liquidator of their own. They want an
outward and visible sign that the assets are being divided
with due consideration for foreign depositors.

Inspector Slee on the Young O’Brien mine at
Grenfell, N.S.W. : “ This yield -3460 z. from 840 tons
though not up to expectation is nevertheless payable, the
reef being several feet in thickness. The water for crush-
ing purposes being very dirty, perhaps the concentrates
will contain a higher yield of gold.” A sick excuse for a
sick crushing. Hitherto this mine has averaged an ounce
to the ton. _ A .fall to less than half-an-ounce is proof
beyond any doubt or excuse that the reef is much poorer,
and it is Slee’s duty to say so. “ Perhaps the concen-
trates ! ” Why did he not try them ?
• * ♦ .
New Bushman’s, Parkes, N.S.W. (first crushing
since the mine was taken over by the co. from the
tributors)—l327oz. Tonnage not stated, but said to be
over 800 tons. It has since cleaned up 156 loads for 3350 z.
of amalgam, and the mill is shut down again for want of
water. The shareholders may reasonably expect some
divs., as it is one of the very few shows in N.S.W. suffi-
ciently open to see any divs. ahead. It is a good mine,
well managed, but it might have been wiser to have taken
one of the big offers, which only float about when John
Bull is in a bad state of boom. He will soon be in a state
of bust, and the chances are that the Bushman’s mine will
one day be in such a condition as to make its shareholders
wish they had taken their divs. from J.B. in advance.
* • * *
Dear Bulletin, —New arrivals tell me the latest
Coolgardie lie circulating in Sydney is that carpenters
here are earning £1 per day. They are really earning more
considering the infernal climate and surroundings, but
they are only getting paid £4 a week, except a very few
who receive 15s. a day. Things are very dull owing to the
holiday exemption being near. After that you will have
another lie wafted to you; the same old lie-not men
enough to man the leases. The consequence will be more
exemption, whilst men are idling in Coolgardie wearily
waiting for a job anywhere, but then you can’t go about
in this country from one place to another at distances of
25 to 125 the off-chance that they may want a
man at the Great Gordelphs mine, and be told when you
get there that they are full-handed and going to apply for
exemption. —Yours, Get Back as Soon as I Can.

Great Boulder’s fortnight’s crushing: 242 tons for

Cue wire: “On Saturday the amount of gold
placed on the coach was 43080 z. On reaching Mt. Magnet,
10930 Z. from the New Chum was added to the load.” No
time stated ; if for a month, as probable, it is nothing to
blow about. Divs., not gold, are the proof of prosperity,
and, as far as the writer is aware, Cue is without one div.-
paying mine.
• » * *
After all the boom-talk about the great richness
of the Maritana, Coolgardie, comes the cheerful news that
the mine is practically shut down. The market, too, has
shut down considerably on the Shares.
• • • •
Says a Murchison man “ Cue One sold last
week for £20,000 cash and 25,000 shares. If on the Towers
or Gympie wha.t would be spot cash value ? The Francis
Reward Otis mill is now hung up pending reconstruction
of its ‘ innards.’ This machine has hitherto sufficed for
reduction of all paddocks around Cue, the owners of
which were not afraid to face the test of a public bulk
crushing. There is a splendid chance now for Fulton’s
20-head to wire into some of the 1000-ton paddocks of 2oz.
stuff we read so much about. The Mainland Consols
struck it heavy the other day- say a cwt. of solid gold at
£6O per lb. Reduce this to pence, divide by number of
shares, and note the splendid div. ! This is a specimen
mine, and when they come to mill the lode the mullock-
tip will be the ultimate destination of most of it, and it is
not alone in this respect.”
♦ ♦ ♦ »
Kalgoorlie wire concerning a rush last Sunday ;
“ One of the party saw a 24-acre block on which was a
large quartz formation and rubble carrying gold ; another
block has a kaolin formation and should also carry nice
geld.” The writer knows of hundreds of kaolin formations
which should carry nice gold, but they don’t.
♦ ♦ * *
Poor old Coolgardie has at last had another
rush within the town boundaries. “ A number of leases
were taken up, including the warden’s residence. The
country is an ironstone formation, and assays give encour-
aging returns, It is believed that the big outcrop along
this line has shed ,the gold on Fly Flat.” Coolgardie is
getting wearied of the hundreds of ghastly, barren, buck,
and otherwise ugly and valueless reefs on which the
mines around the town are situated, and is now trying to
find gold in ironstone. But it won’t wash this time.
These ironstone croppings have been napped by hundreds,
and if any other proof of their poorness was wanted the
“ encouraging returns by assay ” supply it.
•/* * *
Bayley’s Consols—the rich water-mine of Cool-
gardie—has struck another large body of water, and the
manager says he will report the amount as soon as it is
known. In the meantime, preparations are made to sell
it. It yields a sovereign to the ton. The mine is barren
of gold, but as a water-mine it can’t be equalled in Oool-
gardie.
*,♦ * *
Westralian Nov. gold output—ls,so7oz., or less
than the average. Why not add the amount which comes
monthly from London that would more than double it.
It comes into the country—and will stay there. There
are many old Westralians who regard the gold that leaves
as a robbery, and suggest an export duty on it.
♦ ♦ * *
Great News: “The Golden Bar, Coolgardie,
has resin ned .crushing;'’ The directors were astounded at
the news winch appeared in the newspapers predicting
that the crushing would go ounces to the ton. They had
no such “ expectations, although the return was a dis-
appointment.” The papers referred to, including the
good old S.M.IL, print this against themselves. No con-
tradiction came while the shares were going up. It is the
position of many another ■Ooolgardie mine. The battery
is the only consistent mining truth-teller, and when some
of the new ones start there will be a great gnashing of
teeth amongst shareholders in England.

Billy Jones is asking for extension of time to
float Tas. G.M. in London. Time is nearly up under first
contract; but possibly the water will make the contract
grow, since the mine is the wettest in Tasmania. It never
looked better, and a competent man who has lately seen
it further reports that it rim er looked much worse.
* * * *
Golden Gate (Tas.) is said to have 10 years’
work in sight. It is a big tonnage mine with fair average
stone, and could feed twice the present number of stamp-
boxes. But perhaps the yellow stuff is considered to be
safer underground ? A very substantial offer for the mine
wns refused not long ago, and now J. Bull is going to
prospect neighboring claims “on his own.”
* * * *
Tom Bakhap, an experienced Tas. tin-miner,
writes to a local daily re Premier Braddon’s now cele-
brated Anchor mine : “ Every penny of the co’s working
capital (£50,000) will be required, but, if judiciously
expended, and too many ornamental figure-heads are not
indulged in, Ido not think failure need be feared.” Good,
safe statement!
To work the aforesaid Anchor tin-mine (Tas.)
at anything approaching a Mt. Bisohoff return (say 12 per
cent, on market-value of shares) it will be necessary to
crush 5900 tons weekly. This means that the proposed
200 head of stamps will have to be increased to about 250
as the former number could only turn out 3000 tons
weekly. Moreover, an extensive and expensive system of
water conservation will have to be undertaken the
Groom River doesn’t carry enough water to treat 5000
tons of rock every week-so the working capital of
£50,000 won’t be a penny too much. Of course, it isn’t a
moral that the co. expects a Bischoff rate of return.
Unless tin-values rise a lot it is a moral it won’t get it.
Thus, innocently, a miner, discussing in a paper
the Anchor chances of success ; “ The stock of the co has
been confessedly much watered, but nowadays this seems
to be the rule.”
» ♦ * *
Silver Queen v. Montana S.M. Co., .£15,000
damages, was to be heard on 17th inst. A strip of ground
some 3ft. wide, from which the Montana Co. won rich ore,
is in dispute. Five surveyors have been employed, and
disclosures in regard to Tas. Govt, mining surveys are
expected.

During the Sept, quarter of this.year 13 M.L.
mining and three other companies (16 in all) were regis-
tered in London, with total capital of £2,185,000. And
yet the Democratic Government is “driving British capi-
tal out of the country.” When there is a prospect of
making anything British capital can’t be driven out with
a Krupp gun.
* • • •
The scrip mania is rampant in Auckland, and
the local Change is swarming with sharks. There are 50
wild oats for every investment, and the swell families of
Remuera and Ponsonby are pawning their grand pianos to
go in bald-headed for the boodle.
• • • •
At Glen Wills, Vic., writes a Bulletin corres-
pondent, the sole licensed publican has on certain days
taken something like £lOO per day over his bar. The
mining village is located in the centre of the workings,
where most conveniently stores can be delivered by
teams in a very hilly country. The miners are from week
to week delving in gullies and hillsides, on spots reached
by steep up-grade routes - chiefly bridle-tracks at dis-
tances from half-a-mile upwards. But on Saturdays, with
wages in pocket, a swinging gait carries a man into
“town ”in no time. Between Saturday afternoon and the
weary walk back to work on Monday, it is a bad record
when the publican does not take £li)0 over the bar, and
that amount is often exceeded. Attempts have been
made to cut in with a second hotel, but the licensing
bench at Omeo doesn’t see that it is “ required in the
neighborhood.” Neither did two or three shanty-keepers
who were convicted about the middle of this year for
catering for the overflow from the pub. and heavily fined.
♦ ♦ • ♦
The latest Victorian swindle is the weigh-bridge
dodge. Merchants, householders, and others have for
years past pinned their faith on weigh-bridge receipts,
quite satisfied that those documents gave the
weight to an ounce. It now transpires that certain
lessees of weigh-bridges act in collusion with certain
vendors and carters, and cheerfully give receipts to the
effect that 19cwt., or less, make one ton. Some are even
satisfied with 17 cwt.
♦ * » *
Peroration from a recent leading article in
Jimmy Thompson’s paper, the Murchison Times “ Only
when the inevitable day of reckoning comes the Mur-
chison will have less to answer for to the British investors
than the Southern goldfield” (Coolgardie). Which cer-
tainly seems to mean that Coolgardie and the Murchison
are both going to certain smash in the adjacent future,
only the enthusiastic Thompson thinks his field won’t fall
with quite such a thud as the other one.

Die electric boom is coming. One Sydney firm,
doing only the lighting of a citv block, clears close on 20
Percent, profit. Also, George Adams, who is building a
music-hall in Pitt-street, purposes laying down a £lO,OOO
electric plant, which will supply light, not only for his
own premises, but for the Market to Park-street block.
Several big English electrical firms are making very strong
efforts to secure the lighting of Sydney, and, tailing that.
permission to erect central bulk supply depots. Almost
all the aldermen have been cautiously sounded. If
Sydney Municipal Council ever permits the electric lighr-
mg to get beyond its control, it will create a huge and
unmanageable monopoly, and throw away a big revenue,
and create a power which will practically own the citv.

There are at present upwards of 200 out-of-work
miners and navvies hanging about the Ml. Lvell (Tas.)
works. Local men hav.e been discharged to make room
for Vic. miners and navvies, who are imported on mort-
gaged. passage tickets. Naturally enough the local men
howl; hut Victorian capital means that Victorian influ-
ence will bemused whenever possible to secure any jobs
that may be going. As the Tassies have no Vic. influence,
and hardly any dn their own country, they are frozen out.

In leaving Coolgardie, Herr Schmeisser and
Herr Vogelsang, the commissioners of the German Govt.,
and the representatives of one or more London mining
investment companies, took care to deny that they had
given anyone authority to say what they thought of the
fields. They had not even written their preliminary
report, and did not intend to do so till they had visited
the goldfields of the eastern provinces. This, after the
yarn that they considered South African fields not in
it with Coolgardie, indicates how Juuch reliance can he
placed on mining news from W.A.
♦ • * *
From the latest report issued bv the London
liquidator of the English and Australian Mortgage Bank :
“The question of taking proceedings against the directors
and officers mainly responsible for the past, conduct and
management of the bank’s affairs in Australia was re-
ferred for opinion, which, when received in this country,
was fully discussed, but in view of the decision given in
the Victorian courts in an action brought against certain
of the directors of a similar institution, it was not deemed
advisable to incur the heavy costs and expenses of taking
proceedings in this case.”

Some Melb. hotel figures. Ingoing, No. 1
seven years ago £2OOO paid, now £5O. Ingoing, No. 2
seven years ago £BOOO paid, now £3OOO. Ingoing, No! 3

Visitors to'the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs, Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the raanu
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Valor is the name of a Melbourne police"
man.
On Dec. 14, Queen Victoria was 34 years a
lone widow.
The only Melb. cleric who came right-end-
up out of the boom was “ Dane ” O’Hea.
Pastor Fitchett, in the Southern Cross,
writes that Booth’s face is “ too purely Semitic”—
he himself having a pleasantly-rounded, florid,
Saxon cheek.
The late J. B. Patterson, of ennobbled
memory, cut up rather better than was expected.
He leaves .£ll,OO0 —to his female relatives mostly
—which is a pretty fair dividend in these bad,
bust days.
Everything comes to him who waits—
except the capacity to enjoy it when it comes after
too long waiting. A pauper aged 102, in Liverpool
Poor Asylum, lately inherited close on £1,000,000
sterling. The irony of Fate !
Vic. Premier George Turner’s household
arrangements have no frills on them. He lives in
a modest six-roomed cottage, where all the house-
work is done by his wife and daughters, without
the aid even of a hired girl.
Lister, the Dunedin (M.L.) editor whose
office was wrecked and self and staff much
spanked by the ladies of the Gaiety Co., has got a
new experience. He is now doing three months’
“ hard ” for libelling a solicitor.
William Porteous, aged 100, committed
suicide, in Melbourne, the other day, by cutting
his throat. Porteous was tired of life. Ap-
parently a man never gets used to it, however
much experience he may have.
Civilization is progressing amongst the
Maoris. _ A native had all the principal store-
keepers in Palmerston with valueless cheques, and
then, donning swell kilts and mounting a bicycle,
made things hum in that usually quiet village.
Griffith, C.J., has lately been favoring
prisoners, convicted or otherwise, with affecting
moral homilies. The Robb arbitration case, how-
ever, delivered such a moral homily on Griffith
that anything he can say himself falls comparatively
flat.
Gov. Buxton has refused his patronage
to two S.A. racing-clubs. The man with nerve
enough to ask him to accept the honorary presi-
dency, or something of that sort, of the local
Licensed Victuallers’ Association has yet to happen
along.
Dear Bulletin,—ln your issue of the 7th
inst. you give me credit for being the author of
“ A Dream of Fair Judges.” I only wish I were.
It is the work of Frank Gavan Duffy, one of the
most able members of our Bar. —Yours truly,
O’Hara Wood.

Saul Samuel celebrated his 75th birthday
last week (writes The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent). The very ancient Samuel looks much
younger than he did a year or two ago, and seems
as if he might hold his billet till he becomes a
centenarian.
James Service, M.L.O. (Vic.) has just
passed 75. Five years ago people still spoke of
Service as the best Treasurer who ever held office
in Victoria. Now, all they say of him is that any-
body could have been a successful Treasurer in
those fine old boom days.
“ Tattersall ” Adams is removing his con-
sultations to Tasmania. His presence there ought
to mean about .£30,000 a year more postal revenue,
and if Tasmania is wise it will let the harried con-
sultationist remain, and take the money, and
-smother its religious scruples.
Michael Davitt was offered a purse of
sovereigns by his admirers at Wellington (M.L.),
but the one-armed patriot refused the offer and
\ he P urse and the money go to the cause instead.
Let us see—who was the last Australian politician
who refused a similar cash donation?
Gov. Brassey hangs out on Melb. Govt,
House, at his own expense, a new Union Jack,
measuring 24ft. by 12ft. This is just four times
the size of any “ Jack ” previously displayed from
ttie square-top tower. It is obvious that Brassey
is bidding for that Earldom for all he is worth,
The late Duke of Hamilton was a primi-
tive kind of humorist. Once he bought 21b. of
treacle at a grocery, poured it in his belltopper,
and then P ut the bat on the shopman’s head and
laughed consumedly. But, then, no man with a
real sense of bumor could continue to be a duke,
“Jimmy” Thompson, of many exhibi-
tipns fame, and who, as manager of Melbourne
Standard, was fined £lOO and imprisoned for
seven days for commenting on a certain famous
grand jury is now proprietor of a newspaper at
Cue (W.A.) and a J.P. He is a most unprofitable
beak, his fines being almost always nominal.

A well-known N.S. W. lawyer was so badly
mauled recently by a client to whom he had
presented a bill for £403 that he had to remain in
bed for several days. And the law solemnly fined
las assailant 40s. Attorneys are evidently growing
oheap in N.S.W., and the time may yet come
w hen two of them may be assaulted for 3d., with a
further reduction on bashing a quantity.

perhaps the most influential member of the

manner.”
IN A TIGHT PLAGE.
MABEL: “ Jack told me he had an awful hard time getting you to say you
loved him.”
D OROTHY: “ Yes. He fairly squeezed it out of me.”
The opinion of Melb. sassiety on Agnes
Murphy’s poor little local-minded “ novel ” har-
monises with sassiety’s remarks on Agnes herself
now that the pushing young female is far away’
The strange thing of yore was that the women
feared Miss Murphy. They couldn’t have been
afraid of her feeble satire in the local Paunch but
they certainly dreaded her power to ignore their
tea-fights and cut off their jam supply.
Ex-Chief -J ustice Lilley, of Queensland, is
66, and he badly wants to lead a new political
party and oust the Boodlewraith Cabinet and
start his political career over again—if only the
chance will present itself. It is thirty years since
Lilley took office as Attorney-General in the first
Queensland Ministry under Responsible Govern-
ment, and twenty-seven years since his one brief
experience as Premier.
Colonel Gerard Smith’s appointment to
Westralia is not viewed with much favor by
colonists (writes The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent); not that there is anything against him,
