The son of a deceased Maoriland Premier
recently got three months at Coolgardie under the
vag.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) told a recent
Caledonian gathering *that he is a direct descen-
dant of Lochiel. The S.A. Premier’s father was
an Irishman and his mother a Portuguese, and one
of his grandmothers, it seems, was a Cameron, and
he was born in Australia, so he should be solid for
the Scotoh-Irish-Australian vote, and the cordial
support of the Dago fruit-selling interest.
Nat. Levi started as an auctioneer in
Collins-street (Melb.) 41 years ago. His “ office ”
was one room 16ft. square, for which he gave .£l4
a week rental. He defeated Ireland, then a
Minister, for Maryborough in 1860, and was the
first Jew elected to Victorian Assembly. He is
opposed to payment of members, and, one might
conjecture from recent court cases, to payment of
compositors also.
Recently, in a Northern town, a man
found guilty of assault on a female was asked
what he had to say before sentence was passed.
He replied that he could offer no explanation of
the sudden impulse which drove him to his crime,
except that he had put on an old suit just given
him by “ a good, kind old gentleman.” Possibly
the microbes of the kind old gentleman’s wicked-
ness were in the clothes.
Strange, aged 22, now lying under sentence
of death for the Cunninghame (Vic.) murder, has
an undoubted taint of insanity in his disposition.
Both his grand-parents, as well as a grand-uncle
and aunt, are stated to have died in lunatic
asylums. He himself has always borne a good
character, but has been noted for occasional out-
bursts of irresponsible fury, and it was during one
of these that his crime was committed.
Premier Turner despises the richly-uphol-
stered leather chairs on the floor of the House,
and, when sitting at the table, invariably uses a
plain Yankee cane-bottomed chair, placed there
specially for his convenience. Whenever he is
£>oing to make an important speech, he gets all
the other chairs taken away by the messengers, in
order that his train of thought will not be upset
by members buzzing about in his vicinity.
J. Nathaniel Wilson, who died in London
the other day, was a member of M.L. Legislative
Council until two years ago, when he resigned.
He joined the Grey Ministry in ’7B, but it collapsed
the following year. Wilson possessed an unusually
gruff voice, which seemed to come from his very
boots, and he affected a black slouch hat of the
most desperate brigand character, an enormous
mass of long, dank hair, and a general aspect of
harmless ferocity.
Archdeacon Piddington (writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent) is going back to
Sydney with considerably less cash than he
expected to get for his bishop —about .£2OOO less, as
a matter of fact. The Mansion House meeting
was a frost, only about .£lOO being subscribed, of
which Mr. F. H. Hangar, gave one-half. Daniel
Cooper has given ,£lOO, and his sons, Dan and
Willie, .£lOO each. The millionaire, Donald
Larnach, parted a tenner.
Herbert, who has been taken on trial as
Agent-General by the Tasmanians, has the dis-
tinction not only of being Queensland’s first
Premier, but of having filled the office for a longer
consecutive period than anyone who succeeded
him. He held office for over six years, and the
next six years saw five Ministries fall and the
sixth one go distinctly bad. Lilley is about the
only other survivor of that long-lived first
Ministry. When he returned to England, Her-
bert obtained employment under the Imperial
Government. He is a barrister by profession, but
though alleged to be full of legal knowledge he
seldom appeared in the courts.
The will of “ Jim ” Toohey, brewer, was
sworn at 20,000. It is said that the firm, which
has been established just over 20 years, cleared
.£50,000 last year. The first big leg-up it got was
at the time of the famous Danjgnong gale. The
storm swept down the gully in which Burrows
and Gleeson’s brewery at Waverley was situated,
and with it went all the ready stock and beer in
course of brewing. To keep their customers
supplied B. and G. got Toohey’s people to brew
for them, and most of the trade remained with
the latter firm permanently.
The Richardson
family have been fisher-
men at Double Bay, Syd-
ney, for 50 or 60 years.
The founder of the family
was given the right to
own as much land in the
Bay as he chose to en-
close. Like'the grasping
old land-shark that he
was, he fenced in an
allotment measuring just
60ft. by 100 ft. The, con-
sideration for this grant
was that he was to catch
fish for the military. In
their career as fishermen
the family have probably
recovered more dead
bodies than any other
shellbacks along the Syd-
ney water-front. “But
v. e don’t do it now if we
can help it,” says one of
the descendants of the
ancient mariner who
fooled away his chance
of annexing all Double
Bay ; “ there’s nothing in
going to two or three
inquests a week. I make
it a point never to sight
’em, though I always try
to put someone else on
to ’em. Not long ago I
saw one on the beach and
made tracks, of course.
Met a chap looking for
prawns for bait; he axes
me if I can lay him on,
so I pointed out a place
where I thought he might
get some. Presently he
come back looking quite
hurt. “Yes, it is a
fine prawn !” says
he; “why, it’s got
blucher boots on J”
Governor Buxton (S.A.) has had his first
instalment of “ colonial experience ” in a visit to
Bungaree station and a kangaroo bunt. People
who were there say that the spectacle of the con-
verted brewer chasing a baby kangaroo three miles
and finally breaking down in a gallant attempt
to grab him by the tail (which, by the way, nearly
ended in Buxton breaking his neck in a creek),
was a very fine sight. Buxton hunts like no other
man in this world except John Lucas.
A French pen-picture of Zim. ; “ Zimmer-
man, thin and sinewy, with nothing indicative of
the cyclist; dress most correct; sharp features,
under a straw hat with a red ribbon. See him
walk along the street, his figure sways from side
to side as if disjointed; his legs, which on the
pedals are like steel, seem now broken; his gait
that of a savage duck accustomed to devour space,
and awkward in his walk; and on his smooth-
shaven face the serene air of an emperor.”
Scene during Midland Railway Arbitration
case in Parliament Buildings, Wellington (M.L.):
An expert from the West Coast, asked by counsel
as to his occupation, replied, “ Mining agent.”
“Anything else?’’ “Auctioneer.” “Anything
else?” “ Commission agent.” Court becomes in-
terested. “ Anything else ? ” “ Practical miner. ”
Here umpire Blake laid down his pen and took up
the parable: “Does this gentleman do anything
else?” “Oh, yes,” replied the other side; “he
has also been a manager of mining companies,
director of the Govt. School of Mines, and is now
advising agent to Mr. Ziman, the S. African
mining speculator.” And then the examining
counsel proceeded to ask witness: “ Have you
any knowledge of mining ? ” The Law is a
frivolous ass ! t
Crisp, the Austral winner, rode a cool,
well-judged race—equal to a veteran’s. A young-
ster, he had to meet the best men of Australia and
a red-hot favorite in Elliott, who is a thoroughly
experienced wheeler. But it was Crisp’s day out,
and as he clicked round the course the cries and
shouts of trainers to their men—“ Watch Elliott!”
“ Look out for Elliott!” “ Keep up to Elliott!”—
didn’t upset him in the least. He rode gamely,
keeping himself all together, cutting out a hot pace
all the way. And when the last lap’s struggle came
he shot away and won. His time, 4min. 42 l-ssec.,
lowers the Austral record by 19sec., but taking
the respective starts into consideration, it is not so
much better than Middleton’s smin. 1 3-ssec.
Young Crisp is apparently a thoroughly nice boy.
His papa is an M.L. A. of Tas., and his uncle is
the Rev. J. W. Crisp, of the Wesleyan Parsonage,
St. Kilda. Young Crisp does not drink, smoke,
nor bet, and is articled to a Hobart firm of
solicitors. The boy hero was received in Hobart
with citizens, bands, ovations and processions.
He was publicly congratulated in the Town Hall
by the Mayor, and afterwards at a banquet his
health was proposed by the Chief Secretary. How
the bicycle craze is biting into the marrow of the
Australian nation!

POLITICAL POINTS
Vic.’s village' settlement experiment to
date has mopped up £48,005 12s. 6d. Out of the
original 3633 settlers, 2485 remain. And they
curse the land.
To ensure a healthy metropolitan meat
supply, the Vic. Government has appointed a clerk
out of the postal department to the position of
inspector of stock, etc.
The most melancholy and lonely member
of N.S.W. Assembly is M'Millan, who has no
political acquaintances now that Parkes, Wise,
and Bruce Smith are out in the cold.
The Woman’s Suffrage and One-Man-One*
Vote Bill was thrown out in Vic. Council by 19
votes to 17. It seems like a dream that 17 out of
the 48 Rich Landlords could be induced to vote
for manhood suffrage.
The humor of it ! Griffiths, M.L.A. :
“ When do you propose to give private members
an opportunity of doing private members’ busi-
ness?” Premier Reid: “During the recess.”
(Loud laughter, of course).
J. C. Neild, M.L.A., wound up his speech
on the “ sweating ” evil last week with the declara-
tion : “So help me God, I am a humanitarian.”
And yet this is the same Neild who once talked
the House almost to death in a nine hours’ speech.
One provision in the Vic. Railway Trust
Bill is that the fact of railway advts. being
inserted in a newspaper belonging to a trustee
shall not be a disqualification. Which newspaper
proprietor is going to be appointed to a seat on
the Trust ?
The Federal Enabling Bill has practically
passed N.S.W. Parliament, and the Victorian and
S.A. Governments have both started to shove the
same measure through with all possible despatch.
The great chance which Parkes fooled away in ’9l
seems to be coming again.
The A rous, with powerful spectacles on
each eye, sees in the Sydney of the Freetrade
future the great commercial centre of the Southern
seas. But what about all the long years before
the accursed Dibbs tariff came i*x? Why didn’t
Sydney run the universe then 1
Braddon, K.C.M.G., practically runs Tas-
mania now. He is Premier without salary, does
the olfice work of the Chief-Secretary (Moore
being a cipher in Tasmanian politics), runs the
Education Dept., and proposes to take up the
Dept, of Agriculture. He also superintends
Treasurer Fysh between times, Fysh being a per-
fectly harmless politician who can be trusted to
feed out of the hand.

M‘Millan, the Freetrade M.L.A. : “We
were supposed in N.S.W. to have got down to the
bed-rock of the depression, but we were told that
12 months ago, and there is no glimmer of hope
for the next 12 months.” M‘Mi 11 an has evidently
ceased to regard the new Foreign-trade tariff as a
glimmer.
In Sydney, the other day, the Crown
subpoenaed the defendant’s lawyer as a witness for
the prosecution. Then it requested the bench to
order all witnesses out of court, which meant
ordering out the defendant’s solicitor with the
rest and leaving him with no one to conduct his
case. The Crown is a humorous institution at
times in Botany Bay, and the pity is that the
public is required to take it seriously. The kind
of Crown that pervades N.S. Wales was never
meant to be taken seriously.
An ideal income-tax would grant the father
of a family an exemption of £3O for every child
under the age of, say, 18 years. To allow the
same margin in every ease is wholly unfair; a
£2OO exemption to a bachelor and a £2OO exemp-
tion to a man with 11 children are two very
different exemptions.
Pursy Chimside, M.L.A., is suspected of
forming himself, bai Jove ! into a new party, with
a view of upsetting the present Vic, Government.
Pursy sits somewhat alone in the House, and
frowns stealthily, and pulls his cuffs down with
care, as though airaid of disclosing his deep poli-
tical plot before it is ripe.
Shiels, the man of many phases and
phrases, who tries to smash Ministries with
epigrams that smell of the midnight kerosene, has
made another futile attempt to capsize the Turner
Government. Shiels’s worst enemies wish him
luck in these forlorn hopes, for with that long, sad,
diffuse young man nothing fails like success.
Prendergast, M.L.A. (Vic.), wanted to
add to the Factories Bill a provision against any
carrier removing furniture from which the stamp
had been removed. But he got the clause mixed
up so that it read that no unstamped carrier was
to remove furniture, and the House poked
“borak ” at Prendergast till he retired in a huff.
It is pretty well known in Vic. Parlia-
mentary circles that Attorney-General Isaacs
wanted to be allowed to give his reasons for say-
ing that the seat of that brother-Hebrew, Levien,
would not be worth a month’s purchase if the
Government said all it knew about him. But
Premier Turner, who loves peace, set his foot
down, and the one or two Jews on the Govt, side
of the House kicked up a fuss. The consequence
was that Isaacs had to back down apologetically and
spoil an interesting episode.
That notice of motion which proposes to
affirm “ That, in the opinion of this House, his
Honor Sir William Windeyer should no longer
retain his seat as a Puisne Judge of the Supreme
Court of New South Wales,” still figures on the
business paper of the Assembly. At every sitting
the Speaker solemnly goes through the farce of
mumbling out “No. 50, so-and-so, Mr. Price”;
the Government whip as solemnly mutters
“ Object,” and the thing is done with for the day.
As The Bulletin has remarked already: the
hour has come, but Price isn’t the man.
Should Coolgardie obtain separation, the
Swan River gang will be in a pitiable plight. J.
Bull has put his money on the goldfields, and his
sympathy is where his money is. And, if he can
be shown that separation will help along his in-
terests, there is plenty of influence to logroll a
new province in about the quickest time on record.
Unless the Esperance Bay Railway is got under
weigh the trouble will almost certainly begin at
an early date. And if the goldfields get separa-
tion and establish their port on the south coast,
Perth will be almost as dead as Babylon.
The gentle and peaceable Kanaka has been
breaking out in Rockhampton. About 300 wild,
unemployed islanders are camped near the town,
and one night 150 of them charged the Salvation
Army, broke up the service, wrecked the big

Governor Brassey complained, at a Melb.
Y.W.C.A. bun-worry, that Victorians expect
impossibilities from their Governor. “It is im-
possible for any man—certainly impossible for
me,” said his Ex., “ to deliver twice in every day
of the week carefully-prepared addresses on differ-
ent subjects.” Yet really the people of Victoria
wouldn’t care a cent if Brassey didn’t deliver an
address a month. The conviction that he is in
duty bound to jaw constantly and at great length,
and to go down into the very bowels of his sub-
ject every time, is going to make Brassey pall
dreadfully in the course of a few weeks.
Schey’s Bill to amalgamate the N.S.W. soli-
citor and barrister into one shark, and to make
the barristerial end of the animal do the work he
is paid for or else hand back his fee, was thrown
out by the Leg. Council chiefly through the agency
of barrister Want. That legal gentleman called
on the Councillors to “ chuck ” the measure on the
vague and weepful ground that, in some way not
explained, it would be a bitter injustice to the
poor litigant—that person whom every barrister
sympathises with so deeply that he can’t sleep at
night. Also, he pointed out that Schey wasn’t a
lawyer, and therefore couldn’t possibly be compe-
tent to deal with this legal robbery, which is just
the same as arguing that because a man isn’t a

Premier Reid stated to the managers for
the Legislative Council, during the conference
between the two Houses on the Land and Income
Tax, that he will not sit in any Government
which endeavours to raise the land-tax over the
present Id. in the £. The assurarice has been
registered for reference by the old Freetrade-
party, and is taken as marking the line of cleavage
in the Reid following. When the inevitable
demand for more land-tax comes along, Reid is
pledged to throw in his lot with the Parkes-Burns-
Davies-M‘Millan band of ancient revenue-tariffists,
and then the rest of the party must seek a new
leader.
The Reid Government’s proposal to build
railways from Cobar to Broken Hill, Condobolin
to Menindie, and Bourke to Barringun—about 6<K)
miles of line costing nearly £‘2, 000,000 —fell dead.
The latter line was killed by the alleged discovery
that there are only 75 inhabitants in 85 miles.
These far Western lines wouldn’t pay for a very long
while if built on the borrowed money principle,
and, anyhow, the bulk of Ms.L.A. represent
eastern districts, and the eastern districts can
swallow all the borrowed cash that is going. And
yet even the Cobar-Broken Hill line couldn’t, by
any fiendish ingenuity, pay much worse than
Shea’s Creek.
Three of the reasons advanced by the
Midland Railway Co. for its claim of ,£1,800,000
against the Maoriland Government are worth
recording as fine examples of the gall of J. Bull.
They are: (1) That the State delayed for an ur
reasonable time to consent to certain large devia-
tions from the original contract. The co. had no
claim for these concessions, which were granted as
a favor, and it now wants large damages be-
cause the favor wasn’t granted soon enough; (2) That
the State was unreasonably slow in consenting to
an extension of time —another favor that wasn’t
granted so quickly as the arrogant J. Bull syndi-
cate thought it should be ; (3) that the lands of
the co. were not exempted from land-tax. The
British shindykit not only wants the earth, but it
also wants damages if the earth isn’t forthcoming
as quickly as it might be.
Chief Justice Onslow, of Westralia, is at
present also Acting-Governor and President of the
Executive Council. Recently one Clinton wanted
damages for injuries received in a railway accident,
and the Council, of which Onslow was the Presi-
dent, decided to contest the claim. Then Clinton
sued the Government, of which Onslow was tem-
porarily a part, and Onslow, despite the fact that
he was a portion of the defendant, sat on the
bench as judge. He also in his double capacity as
judge and part of the defendant, summed up
against the plaintiff, and when the special jury
gave a verdict for £4OO, he remarked that “if
this was a special jury he would much like to
know what a common jury was like.” The latter
remark was probably only one of those gentle-
manly snorts which the average bad-tempered
judge fires off at people who are not allowed to
talk back, and was not indicative of any bias ; but
the mixing up of the executive and judicial office
in such a case always suggests a possibility of bias
none the less.
Howe, M.P., recently rose in S.A. Assem-
bly, at a most solemn moment, and desired to
observe : “ When the war cloud lowers over the
land let us stick to our oars like men, and not
wait till the steed is stolen before looking the
stable door.” The members woke up, and were
just beginning to dissect this statement, when
there was a loud clap of thunder,. and Howe sat
down.
Dear Bulletin, —No wonder that there
are continual difficulties about the management of
the Victorian railways. The present Minister of
Railways, Williams, is a one-eyed man ; the last
Minister, Richards, was a one - armed man.
Wheeler—who was Minister of Railways in the
Shiels Govt. —has only one tooth. What is
wanted for the Victorian lines is a whole man, not
a mere fraction,— A One-Legged Man.

In the Vic. mallee districts, according to
Longmore, M.L.A., some of the big original
leaseholders who pay the State one-twentieth of
a penny per acre per annum are selling their rights
for 10s. and 20s. per acre. The Bill now before
Parliament provides for the granting of mallee
leases at a rental of 2 per cent, on the capital
value, and unless great care is taken this will also
mean that the State tenant, who gets his land at
2 per cent., will sub-let it by-and-bye at five. Sub-
leasing of State lands is partly sweating and
partly defrauding the revenue ; it should be abso-
lutely illegal; and the minimum penalty should be
the forfeiture of the original lease and a fine of
five times the amount of the rent colleoted from
the sub-tenant.
The anaemic Vic. Freetrade Association,
which is always on the point of dying for want of
money to pay the office rent, and which consists
mainly of Premier Reid’s brother, has telegraphed
Premier Reid congratulating him on the establish-
ment of Freetrade and direct taxation in New
South Wales. Reid proposes to raise three times
as much by Customs duties as by direct taxation,
so there doesn’t seem so very much to telegraph
about after all. When is the Shoddy Government
coming along which will establish real Freetrade,
and let the party have the blessing it is always
asking for ? New South Wales has voted Free-
trade for many years, and has never got it, and
never gets much nearer to it, and never seems
really to want it. And if it did get it the Free-
trade-party would probably be the most disgusted
institution in this hemisphere.
Melb. Metrop. Board, dissatisfied with the
working of its minimum-wage arrangement, pur-
poses altering the law to the following effect■
That for each day of eight hours not less than the
following wages shall be paid to persons of the under-
mentioned ages employed under the board’s contracts,
that is to say, under the age of 16 years, 2s. 6d. ; 16 and
under 20 years, 4s. ; 20 years and under 55 years, 6s. ;
over 65 years, 4s.
The alleged purpose of this sliding .scale is to give
the eternal old man a show. The obvious result
will be that men between the ages of 20 and 55
will not be wanted by Metrop. Board contractors,
and the alleged minimum-wage law will not be
worth a straw. The laborer who wants a job
when the Fitzgibbon Board has revised its present
chuckle-headed scheme will have to be over 55 or
under 20—on the pay-sheet, at least. Some con-
tractors will come right down to the half-crown
scale at once, and insist on everybody being under
16, even if he has a wife and eight children.

“ Well-known to the police, yer Wor-
th’p.” is a notable phrase in the police-courts. At
a Sydney eoutt, the other day, an alleged cripple
Was charged with “drunk’’who had never been
in custody before. “Anything known ?”&c.
“Yes, your worship—he’s Well-known to the
police.’’ “Twenty shillings or 14 days.” Of
course he h id not 2d pence, and went to Cockatoo
for a fortnight. Writer asked the constable if the
man had been in custody betore, and was answered
“No; but ho ought to have been.” Had this con-
stable told the truth the culprit would no doubt
have got “the rising.” The magistrate took the
constable’s words to mean “repeatedly convicted.”
And this is how some S.M. s manage to get
through 100 oases an hour.

By many the beneficent healing process
of Massage is thought to be a modern discovery,
butj as a matter of fact, it was known to the
ancients long before the Christian era—when it
was practised by the recognised physicians of the
day. It is only of late years that the medical
faculty has once again recognised its efficacy. As
a thoroughly scientific and most important and
valuable accessory to the modern art of medicine,
it is essential that Massage should be practised
only by thoroughly efficient practitioners. This
treatment is not mere rubbing ; its successful ad-
ministration depends entirely upon the special
qualifications of the masseur, who, in addition to
the physical gifts necessary, must have a complete
knowledge of anatomy, physiology, and organic
chemistry. That Mr. Teepoo Hall, of Collins-street,
Melbourne, possesses ail the qualifications neces-
sary to the successful and fashionable masseur is
abundantly proved by the fact that he has received
testimonials as to his ability, kindness, and atten-
tiveness from all the leading physicians of Mel-
bourne, including Urs. T. N, Fitzgerald* J, P,
Ryan, Arthur F. Davenport, M. U. O’Sullivan,
1. Taylor Downie, W, H. Burton, Walter M‘Gib-
bon, Geo. Graham, etc., besides clergymen and
many leading merchants and ladies. The pamph-
let issued by Mr. Teepoo Hall on this interesting
subject explains the system of treatment, and can
be obtained from him free, on receipt of 3 stamps.*
WOOL-TRADE WITH JAPAN.
The National Wool Company, of Melbourne, has
taken the initiative in bringing under the notice
of the Woolbrokers’ Association the importance of
developing our present wool-trade with Japan,
The first attempt in this direction was made 20
years ago by the late Mr. Monokton Synnot, and
a second one in 1885. The movement was pro-
perly organised, and would no doubt have suc-
ceeded but for a sudden rise in wool values—a rise
of from 25 to 30 per cent.—which at once killed all
interest in the movement by making producers
content with existing gains and indifferent or
lethargic in their attitude towards a movement
which then only seemed to aim at providing for a
vague necessity which might arise in the dim and
distant future. However, past investigation has
proved : (1) That woollen-manufacturing in China
and Japan is in its infancy. (2) That China, while
possessing sheep, produces no wool which will
compare favorably with our lowest qualities, and
that sheep are there propagated for food chiefly.
Also, that what wool is made use of, is used in the
method in vogue in Scriptural times, 2000 years
ago and upwards, with the skin attached. (3)
That Japan cannot breed sheep without growing
food to keep them alive in winter. It, therefore,
seems highly desirable that the efforts of the
National Wool Company to develope trade with
Japan should be supported vigorously.*
“The 440 village settlers in South Aus-
tralia,” writes a Bulletin correspondent, “ while
owing- the State .£B5 per head, spent .£l2OO a year
in tobacco—ls. per week per head (at wholesale
rates) if all were smokers. The tobacco-bill repre-
sents over 10 per cent, of the annual cost of keep-
ing the 1770 men, women, and children on the
settlement.” But man does not live by bread
alone. He, to a great extent, lives by tobacco,
especially as there is no grog on S.A. village-
settlements.

take offence j

THE POET ON THE CRITIC.
Alas! tlie poet soon grows old—too soon his song
is sung ;
But—glorious compensating law !—the Critic’s
always young.
P. LUFTIC.
Hear them tus-ging at the bell,
Lust.v youth ami pretty miss,
Urged on by your bright example.
Have resolved at least to sample
All the joys of wedded bliss,
Play your part, Nathaniel!
Cease awhile you two caressing !
List to gabbled troth and blessing I
Now you re married, lucky man !
Now caress her—while you can—
King that, cracked old dinner-bell I
’Tis the golden-wedding bell
Provided by Nathaniel!
Fling the rice—what! rice as well?
Yes, gratis by Nathaniel!
Who lives to fling the bridal grain
(A .d sweeps it up for use again?)
To usher them to Paradise
And all the joys of wedded clover.
Then he kindly tells them where’s
The cheapest shop for prams, and chairs,
’(Jute old dog, Nathaniel! Gets his coin, Nathaniel!
Hope-, lit * his, th ir joy will last
Till the golden-wedding's past,
So e ich ll murmur, looking hack
O’er that long double harness track,

Among the very numerous publications
commemorative of Christmas this year, W, H.
Glen and Co.’s (Melb.) “Christmas Federal”
occupies a very noteworthy place. This annual is
quite an edition de luxe, being beautifully printed,
and containing an admirable selection of dance
music and a number of choice songs. A contem-
porary publication, by the same firm, is “Merry
Little Tunes for the Children,” edited by the
well-known English expert musician, Miss Flor-
ence Wickins. Both publications should present
special attractions for families living in the bush.*
Any mean, stingy, undesirable Edwin who
reads these line is hereby cautioned not to take
Angelina within cooee of Messrs. Stewart
Dawson and Co.’s magnificent jewellery emporium
in the Strand Arcade and George-street, Sydney,
for of a certainty the wealth of artistic ornaments
there collected will give the dear girl a fever only
to be Cured by a hair of the dog that bit her. But
any manly, generous, intelligent Edwin who
desires to hear himself called “You duck!” in
the sweetest of all feminine keys is informed that
nowhere in Australasia can he purchase that
luxury more cheaply than at Stewart Dawson’s—
a name which carries with it the certainty of
good taste and good value in all that pertains to
the trade in watches, silver-ware, rings, brooches,
bracelets and a hundred precious things. Country
residents should write for Messrs. Stewart
Dawson and Co.’s catalogue.*

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The interprovincial cricket-match, N.S.W. v.
8., or rather N.S.W. v. Queens!., nd Graziers’ Meat Export
o. for nine of the oo’s employes played—aroused a
dead-and-alive sort of interest in lydney. On Saturday—
the Governor’s day, and naturally I le best the attendance
was scarce 2000. The veteran Boyll* stood out in obedience
to the order of the boss of the Queensland Graziers’ Co.
That functionary also ordained that Hoare was to play.
Percy M'Donnell captained Q. ; Syd. Gregory N.S.W. If
Syd. didn’t work his bowling for all it was worth, lie never-
theless gave every bowler a good chance to distinguish
himself.
Coningham’s big scores of 151 in th fi ;-st innings,
and 51 in the second were made by garn i, - Ing cricket,
and were the feature of the match. The > ... > •er of the
Queensland Graziers’ Meat Export Co. i ' ■ > to
Coningham : “ Congratulations ; will show m apprecia-
tion substantially.” Coningham’s batting was p.. .omenal
after his five weeks of enforced rest. The way he and
M'Donnell, who are both quite out of practice, walloped
the N.S.W. bowling, shows that the province has no
champion trundler to take Turner’s place.
Lewis, of the Queensland Meat Export Go’s
team, is the co’s cooper, and is one of the biggest hitters
in Australia ; but he has a wretched defence, and did
nothing this trip.
Walters’ big score of 150 was knocked up by
hard graft and good luck in second-class style. One good
chance at 32 was missed by the wicket-keeeper, and a
better one was missed at 81 by “ Dr. Bobby” Macdonald,
the worst field in the world, perhaps. Little “ Dr. Bobby ”
Mac. dropped the ball as if it were a red-hot cannon-shot.
The only N.S.W. batsmen who didn’t give a chance in a
good score were Donnan (40) and Gregory (43).
The brilliant innings of the match was P.
M'Donnell’s 65 ; the last 40 of which were made in 10 hits
in this order, 4,4, 4,4, 4,5, 5,4, 2, 4. M'Donnell’s cutting
and driving were a treat; his fivers were off one over of
M'Kibbin’s ; one fell among the crowd, the other among
the ladies in their reserve.
P. Mac’s batting last Saturday recalls how,
when he captained the Australians against North of Eng-
land in ’BB, he enjoined upon everyone just before his
going to the wickets with A. Bannerman that the only
course the Australians could adopt was block, block,
block ; the wicket was bad and the match must not be
lost. P. Mao. took strike and, strictly following his own
advice, blocked the first ball, blocked it safely, just safely
enough to lift it clean out of the ground. Then he kept
on blocking steadily so as to allow that slogger, A. Banner-
man, to open his shoulders and demoralise the field. A. B.
swiped viciously, and drove up a tremendous three while
P. Mac. blocked up 84.
The Bananalanders fielded so well, especially
the ex-N.S.W. and ex-Vic. men, that dropping several
good catches at a great cost was all the more unpardon-
able. The pick of the visitors’ fieldsmen was Atkins, ex-
South Sydney man. N.S.W.’s score of 485 was no more
than should have been ; for day and wicket were perfect,
luck was quite favorable, and the visitors’ bowlers, know-
ing little, bowled mostly mediocre stuff, and often rub-
bish. Coningham was quite out of form with the ball.
• • ♦ »
The interprovincial match, N.S.W. v. Queens-
land Graziers’ Meat Export Co. terminated in a long-
drawn-out agony in the presence of a handful of members
and a large dust-cart. O’Brien, the last of the Butchers
representatives, made 48 runs; Dr. Bobby Macdonald
carried out his bat for 77, and the slow, painful innings
closed for 273. N.S.W. easily made the necessary 51,
winning by nine wickets. Donnan made 33, not out;
M'Kenzie 21, stumped.
Dr. Bobby Macdonald is a Brisbane dentist, and,
according to a legend from a reliable source, has a prac-
tice worth £3OOO a year.
* * ♦ *
Although The Bulletin’s attempt to place the
three prize-winners for the Austral Wheel Race was a
failure, its selection of seven men to fight out the finish
proved good enough. Of those seven men, two (Hall and
Anderson) fell. They were the only riders who came to
grief. The other five finished Ist, 3rd, 4th, 6th and 9th
respectively. Tub Bulletin ventured a suggestion that
the four top-weights—Pither, Parsons, Walker, and M'Lean
—had no chance, and they were all beaten off.
Hill’s ride in the Austral final (he finished sixth)
was an object lesson re the necessity for being provided
with a cool head to carry out a settled policy when
the pinch comes. On the previous Saturday the Victorian
won his heat in masterly style, after allowing Crisp to
make all the running. He spurted clean away from the
Tasmanian within the last furlong of the journey and
finished the fresher of the two. Crisp says now “ that he
had plenty of go left in him when he finished third for his
heat, in front of Peart.” Also, Peart thinks he could
have beaten Hill and Hall if he had chosen to go a little
faster in the heat, yet it stands to reason that the third
and fourth men in a test race are doing all they know
to make sure of the place which qualifies them for a
chance in the final. In proof of which assumption it will
be found, on looking into the figures, that Crisp and Peart
were the only competitors who, after finishing third and
fourth for their trial heat, managed to turn the tables on
the winner and the runner-up in the final.
The fourth of the Austral heats was the one to
pick the winner from, judging by the fast time and the
further fact that Payne, a fine performer, couldn’t over-
take the placed men. Well, The Bulletin picked three
out of its seven selections from that particular test-race,
and reckoned the winner and runner-up better wbrth
backing than Crisp, who finished third. So they were—
until the question came to be settled. Hall, the runner-
up, had the misfortune to get knocked over, and Hill was
found wanting in nerve. He would go to the front and
drop back again into third position ; then run very wide
and take the lead once more. In the Stand critics were
saying that Hill dodged about as though he had 100 yards
up his sleeve. But he couldn’t raise a spurt in the final
lap. His strength had to some extent evaporated under
the warm excitement of a £2OO prize. The statement that
Hill was beaten by the time (4.42 l-sth) is ridiculous, for
he did the distance in 4.45} on the previous Saturday—a
terribly sultry day. Besides, there is considerable doubt
as to 4.42 l-sth being the right time for the race. It was
originally registered on the official board as 4.45.
Zimmerman’s fancy for the Austral was young
mu
Payne, long ago tipped by The Bulletin as a
promising rider, was the only N.S. VV. representative who
showed to advantage in the M. B.C. recent meeting.
Winning the M.B.C. £4O plate was a fine performance.
• ♦ * *
Carpenter’s public crying over Parsons’ taking
the triplet from him is, all things considered, an inco-
herent display of feeling. It’s an accepted sporting con-
dition that no man is beaten on his merits, but it’s work-
ing that axiom to death to raise a whoop and wail after
being beaten by a lap. Fact is, Carpenter, Martin and Co.
couldn’t ride fast enough. In the five-mile race at the
last Sydney carnival, Lewis snatched the triplet cleverly
from Parsons and hung on just behind it for four miles
and three quarters. Then, in the last lap, Parsons
started on his own and beat Lewis all the way. Now
Parsons’ trainer issues a challenge to concede a lap in 10
miles to Carpenter, and give C. his own pacer all the way.
Which is a most generous offer to Carpenter after the way
that misguided youth has taken on.
* • • *
Crisp is one of the pluckiest young cyclists that
ever trod a treadle—“ trod a treadle” being the bikey
equivalent for “looked through a bridle.” The Tasmanian
boy came over to. Melbourne to win the Austral, a stranger
in a strange land, and didn’t trouble about any other
event at the meeting.
• • • *
Elliott’s most sorrowful backer was Elliott. He
spent much time and money in training for the Austral,
and he put his shirt on the final. When dead-bird meets
dead-bird then comes the bookmakers’ tug.
• • * *
A man who has never been in good condition,
but who will shine when he does get there, is Lewis.
♦ * ♦ ♦
Barden, hearing of Parsons’ five-mile victory
over Zimmerman, instantly challenged Parsons for > 200
a-side over five miles. Barden couldn’t be kidded into
racing the beaten Zim. five miles, although he has
improved much since Zim. ran him off his legs in their
famous five-mile contest. Yet now is the time for the
Zimmerman-downed cyclist to hurry to Australia, chal-
lenge Zim. and make boodle. For although Z. has come
back from his mountain trip in much better health, cir-
cumstances over which he has no control will stop him
reaching champion form this trip. Should Johnson slip
over here from ’Murka he ought to capture Zim.’s scalp
sure. What’s left of it, that is.
* • • «
Exit Megson, enter Payne, say many N.S.W.
cyclists.
* * • •
Crisp, winner of the Austral wheel race, was the
lightest competitor (9st.) and rode the heaviest machine
(241 b.). No less than 13 cameras were focussed on Crisp
within three minutes after he achieved his victory.
• • • *
This year’s winner of the Austral Wheel Race
neither drinks, smokes, nor bets, but he’s very young at
present and may acquire those habits anon. The previous
nine winners were ordinary mortals who smoked if they
didn’t drink, or betted if they didn’t smoke, and some of
them did as Maud Hobson used to say in “In Town ”
everything.
• * • «
When Crisp and the other “ Austral meeting ”
prize-winners came to take their money they didn’t un-
pack their souls with many words. Ail their speeches
put together contained scarcely enough wind to blow the
froth off a pint of beer.
* * ♦ ♦
Paul Mell: “ Once more has the running of
the Austral Wheel-race shown up the utter defective-
ness of the handicapping in cycling competitions. Just
as Megson and Co. last year did not get a chance to race
in the final, but gave up on seeing the hopelessness of
their position, so Parsons, Walker and Pither found
themselves clean out of it last Saturday week. The pace
of the front group, handicapped half a lap ahead, was so
terrible from the jump that the chances of the back men
pulling up became nil. This was obvious before the race
was half over, and the finish was left to the front crowd, of
whom every rider had more than 200 yards start.
“ This circumstance has to be taken into account
in comparing the time of the Austral wheel-races up to
date. Crisp’s time is the shortest, but then his start was
the longest. His 4min. 41 jsecs. was not the time taken to
travel two miles, Jaut only that distance less 255 yards.
“ In considering the differences of condition in
the preliminary and final heats, it is necessary to compare
the pace of the lot. They are as follows ; -
min. secs.
Ist heat ' 4. 57 3-5
2nd „ 4. 53 1-5
3rd ~ 4. 54
4th „ (in which Crisp ran third) 4. 45 3-5
sth „ 5. 53
Final 4. 41i
The fact that Crisp’s heat was run faster than any other
except the final only proves that the placed men in it,
who started from 275yds, 210yds., 255yds, and 230yds.,
were too generously handicapped.”
* * * *
Melb. Herald gravely summed up the chances
of the various Austral candidates in exactly the same
style as the Melb. Cup review. Blank was considered to
have “a very likely looking chance,” while if Dash were
about in the last lap “ his fine sprint would make him
very hard to beat.” Also, Juggins was “doing good
work and seemed likely to effect a surprise,” and so on to
the end of the list. Not one man was put down as not
having Buckley’s chance, consequently the paper will now
proudly point out that it mentioned the winner. “Men-
tioned,” forsooth.
* * ♦ »
The 27th Jan., ’96, sees the biggest cash prizes
yet offered in Australasian cycling- the £250 first prize
and £4O second prize in the Australian Natives’ Associa-
tion two-mile wheel-race in Melbourne.
* • • •
The bike reporter for Melb. Age blows a
gigantic trumpet to the glory of local champion Parsons,
and in all directions there is a disposition to overrate
him—in the present writer’s opinion. Parsons seems
always to be racing the last ounce out of himself ; he cer-
tainly looks very sick on it, whilst old-bird Martin sweats
wisely but not too much. And whereas the enthusiastic
young Vic. won £25 in stakes at the M.B.C. meeting, the
Irish-American plugger picked up £35. In the five-mile
race he let Parsons overlap him and walk in for the first
prize of £l5, after which he started to go hard for the
£lO second money and won it. Harris, the Englishman,
hasn’t distressed himself at all thus far, and will probably
not show his best Australian form whatever that may be
—before New Year’s Day.
Zimmerman makes his first Victorian track
appearance on Melbourne Exhibition oval, 21st Dec.
Seven races are billed, and Z. will ride in four. Two of
Z.’s races are y. time, paced—quarter and half miles. The
biggest prize is £4O- Exhibition Handicap.
• • » «
The Fisher-Valentine match for Amateur Cham-
pionship of Australia is built on points and eight rounds.
And the men box with full-sized gloves. Fisher is son of
an M.L. Mayor and M.P., and used to be a bank-clerk in
M.L. He is about .'iin. taller and 101 b. heavier than Valen-
tine. Fisher boxed Flacke, the Dutch giant, four rounds,
and says he had the best of it. He is amateur champion
of M.L.
* » » *
Good judges aver that Waterfall may prove
quite capable of upsetting Auraria and Wallace in the
March pots. Being an undoubted flrst-classer he can’t
be laid aside, and his connections have more than a
glimmer of hope. There’s not very much difference
between the three, anyhow.
♦ * ♦ «
“Punter” writes “ Judging from the satis-
factory list of entries, the A. J.O. Summer Meeting should
prove quite as successful as heretofore, despite the cry of
‘hard times.’ The Villiers Stakes on Boxing Day pro-
mises to be a miniature Carrington Stakes, and Belmont,
Beau Brummel, and the best of Mr. S. Hordern’s may
furnish the winner among them. Although only nine
youngsters have accepted for the rich December Stakes,
yet they are all of the first water, and an interesting race
should be seen. Le Var will probably pull off the prize
for the Clarke syndicate, whilst Huret, Mosaic, and
Reward should prove his most dangerous opponents. In
the A.J.C. Handicap, Nobleman, if he runs, should win.
Response, Rewi, and Trier may also be seen to advantage.
“ Woodford and Quiver are at present favorites
for the Summer Cup, but the “ in and out ” running mare
will bloom later on at the Australian or Sydney Cup.
Maybe, Alcmena, Erewhon, and Kelso’s pair, Rewi and
Te Whiti, have all been given chances by the handioapper,
and, moreover, are in first-rate condition, and of the lot
Alcmena or Erewhon may perhaps prove victorious. The
ever-popular Carrington Stakes is sure to bring Out a
large field, several horses having already been backed for
it. Nobleman, Malachite, Moorefield, Coolalta, and Her
Ladyship seem very fairly treated, and the winner should
spring from this quintet. The top weight has improved
out of all knowledge, and if he incurs no penalty pre-
viously he should be capable of winning. Previous run-
ing will influence the decision of Tatt.’s Cup, but if
Mahee, Alcmena, Te Whiti, and Gozoreina are found at
the post unpenalised they should provide the winner
among them.”
• ♦ ♦ *
The managership of the Australian Eleven will
be decided this month. Meanwhile “ Jack ” Blaokham is
in with an application for the post, and is bringing all
sorts of influence to bear. Portus (N.S.W.) is said to
have a very good show, but unless the N.S.W. Civil Ser-
vice Commission comes round soon to definitely fix his
status, he won’t risk his billet by taking extended leave
of absence.
• ♦ ♦ *
The fixtures for the next Australian Eleven in
England show that it has been hospitably treated, so far
as opportunity for making “gate” is concerned: the
team plays three matches with “Yorkshire,” and appears
prominently at Manchester, Lords, and Kennington Oval—
all places of big gate. Its hardest time, in the playing
sense, comes in between June 10 and July 5, wherein the
Australians meet Marylebone, Yorkshire, England,
Notts, Yorkshire, and North of England in succession.
Judging from present evidence, the team will lose all
three test-matches unless it can happily join forces with
the elements at the right time on each occasion. Should
Turner rejuvenate and Eady, the big Tasmanian crack
field and fast bowler, be good enough for inclusion, the
team will be pretty right in bowling. Otherwise, it will
be considerably wrong. M'Kibbin is a bowler whose
physique will not allow of him maintaining first-class form
throughout a long and arduous season; moreover, he
wants to do just a little more to be sure of going.
* * » *
Placke, his friends allege, is whole again and is
out looking for a man to swim five miles for £5O a side.
Before Placke’s coming several blowful local swimmers
made the welkin ring with the blow they put into their
own trumpet. Now these swimmers might be dead ; they
are so still.
*** ~ «
In reply to “J. Kop” : He certain turf-tipster
advts. that have appeared in this paper The Bulletin
can only say that it takes them for what they are worth.
A man who supplies racing tips to confiding punters for a
monetary consideration probably does his best to find
winners, and induce further application for his “dead
birds.” It is to the advertiser’s interest, seemingly, to
give his clients some cause for thinking him an intelligent
prophet. The “discretionary investment” business
is quite another matter. The question of honesty in any
sort of dealer with other people’s money concerns them
who find the cash. Some trustees are honest, whilst
others are otherwise. A discretionary investment, in Tub
Bulletin’s opinion, subjects the conscience of the trustee
to a severe strain.
* • » *
Last week Petitt instead of MTherson was
announced as winner of the champion sawing match
(Tas.). Petitt won the handicap, not the champion.
♦ ♦ * *
Zimmerman and the M.B.C. are not snorting at
one another as alleged. Zimmerman drew 30,000 people
in Sydney, and the M.B.C.’s Austral final drew 30,000
people in Melbourne without Zim.’s assistance. Misun.
derstandings between such natural allies don’t last long.

Messrs. Berg l & Brabbin will be most happy to take charge of their shipments on arrival in London,

will give satisfaction. For full paiticulars apply

known Bacteriologist, lias established the fact that very minute proportions of “Sanitas ”
Fluid, “Sanitas” Oil and “ Sanitas ” Emulsion suffice to quickly destroy the microbes of
Cholera, Diphtheria, Typhoid Fever, Scarlet Fever, Pneumonia, Measles, Influenza,
Pueiperal Fever, Glanders, Yellow Fever, &c.
It has also been shown that the vapour of “Sanitas” Oil, as generated from the
“Sanitas” Disinfecting Fumigator, has a most destructive action on the germs of
disease, and that, consequently, its ii I alatio i must be most beneficial in the treatment
of Diphtheria, Phthisis, and all Diseases of the bungs and Throat.
When used k>r fumigating sick rooms, Dr. G 'i fiths’ experiments show that a short time
serves to destroy all the germs that are present in the air.
llis experiments also demonstrate that when “Sanitas” Fluid is sprayed about dwelling
rooms, the microbes of disease are entirely and quickly destroyed, and that small
quantities of “Sanitas” Emulsion equally well destroy tin same gernis when present
in water. Of great importance is the further fact which is brought out by Dr. Griffiths’
investigations, namely, that not only do “Sanitas” Disinfectants kill all disease germs,

Sheridan, now lying under sentence of
death in Darlinghurst Gaol, is to be hanged on
January 7. That Sheridan will be hanged on his
“ record ” goes without saying. On March 19,
1885, Justice Innes sentenced him to 10 years’
penal servitude; and on March 25, same year,
Chief Justice Martin gave him an additional five
years. these sentences were passed upon ver-
dicts of manslaughter found by two distinct juries.
In his trial for the Jessie Nichols mishap the
Crown took no chances ; there was only one
count, that of murder, so that another man-
slaughter verdict was out of the question.

BYGONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I see two faces in my dreams
That come of a time alar ;
One is lit with the shine of streams
And one is pale as a star.
Fair faces they that played their part
In the days when passion woke,
For one fair woman broke my heart
And the other’s heart I broke.
Nov. 23, ’95.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
Before the boom there was in Australia a
most brilliant financier with a truly Hebrew
capacity for mental arithmetic. But he met his
Waterloo over some very smart transactions, and
got into—great trouble. Now, if you could pene-
trate into the culinary portion of a certain estab-
lishment you would see a wan man in a striped
cotton shirt peeling potatoes or quartering cab-
bages. That’s the brilliant financier ! His brain
is not much worse than it used to be, but he can
get no remunerative work for it. If a man were
valued according to his capacity instead of accord-
ing to his luck in not being bowled out, the
Government would have rushed him with an offer
of a billet in the Treasury, and given him all the
most intricate work to do at a large salary. But
he had the ill-luck to be found out, and so men of
far less capacity and perhaps of less honesty hold
the good billets, and the brilliant financier wears
a striped cotton shirt and peels potatoes for a
living. Young man, don’t be found out!
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Mu :alypte Special Oil.*
Dr. Garth Wilkinson, the Age’s crimino-
logical authority, who The Bulletin lately in-
sinuated was probably mythical, is, it would
seem, a real personage, after all. He is
M.R.C.S.E., L.S.A., fellow of the Icelandic
Miciety of Copenhagen and F.R.G.S., and a
writer on such weird subjects as “The Human
ilody and its Connection with Man,” “ The
Forcible Introspection of Women for the Army
and Navy by the Oligarchy, considered Physi-
c 3 illy,” “ Human Science, Good and Evil, and its
“ Divine Revelation and its Works and
.Sciences,” “The Greater Origins and Issues of
L fe and Death,” “The Soul is Form, and doth
the body Make ; ” likewise translator and editor
of Swedenborg’s works, and editor of Anti-
Vaccination Tracts. From all of which it is evi-
dent that the Age might as well quote Mrs.
Mellon as an authority on criminology. Dr. Garth
Wilkinson’s extraordinary titles certainly justify
a suspicion that he is somewhat loony.

One of ex-Governor Carington’s methods
of achieving popularity was to board ships
on their arrival and paternally lecture his
new subjects with stock platitudes. On one of
these occasions, a brace of new-chum “Celes-
tials,” who apparently failed to appreciate the
gubernatorial oration, had descended the accom-
modation-ladder with a view of getting aboard a
shore boat which lay outside the smart new Govt,
launch. The coxswain of the latter, who objected
to the transfer of “ filth ” to his snowy deck, was
strenuously opposing the yellow invasion with his
best “pigeon English” and variegated “colonial”
when H.E., who had just got the last of his
benevolent injunctions off his chest, and doubtless
felt in a high condition of moral elevation, came
to the gangway, and, ascertaining the casus belli,
saw and seized the opportunity of scoring a point.
“ Johnson,” he exclaimed, in a tone of mild re-
proach, “ are not those men your fellow-creatures
and just as good as you?” “Certainly, your
Excellency ; if they are as good as you,” promptly
replied the son of Neptune. Here the gallery
laughed in the wrong place, and the star actor
yawned loudly.

PROCLAMATION.
IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TLIIRLEIB CASTLES ” and “ CAPSLAUT 1
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Agents for W, D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

With an ordinary tape measure determine th° width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet, of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a

Recently there was a slight difference of
opinion on one of the S.A. village settlements,
and, as a result, the chairman of trustees had his
nose-broken, and a fellow-trustee his jaw dis-
located and one of his ears allegedly chewed.
When the Parliamentary party of inspection
visited the settlement, they asked a prominent
settler—the proud author of the broken nose, dis-
located Jaw, and allegedly-chewed ear aforesaid—
whether there was any want of harmony amongst
the settlers, and he said he really hadn’t noticed
any trouble so far.
PRAYER UP TO DATE.
I’ve faith in the praying,
The praying for rain.
It’s time vve were laying
It on once again.
We’ve no time to go through it,
But still there’s a way :
Let the unemployed do it
At six bob a day.
Sydney.

The Best Christmas Gift.
Webster’s International Dictionary.
Originating in a modest Spelling Hook, published when Webster was a mere student, the work has developed by
different stages, during the century or more which has since passed, into a tome of over 2000 close pages of three
columns each; replete with every addition and advantage which scholarship, literary skill, and a keen appreciation
of the requirements Of modern life can conceive,— Extract from review by Bruce Smith , Esq., Barrister-at-Law, Sydney.
from His Excellency Sir Frederick M. Barley, Kt., Lieutenant-Oovernor and Chief Justice of
New South Wales ;
Government House, Sydney, 10th July, 1895. —I purchased a copy of Webster’s Dictionary some
three years back, since which time I have had it in constant use. It is, in my opinion, a very
valuable work, and should find a place in every library.

This Dictionary is one of very great value to all engaged in scholastic wor k.
Prom J. C. Marnard, Esq., Undersecretary for Public Instruction, Sydney

From P, Bridges, Esq., Chief Inspector of Schools:—
WEBSTER’S INTERNATIONAL DICTIONARY.
Chief Inspector’s Office, Department of Public Instruction, Sydney. The term “Dictionary
gives but a very imperfect idea of the complete and comprehensive character of this work Whi
meeting fuHy all the requirements of a perfect dictionary, telling with clearness and accuracy all tin
should be known about words, it is also a cyclopcedia of the latest information upon all subjects <
general interest. The pictorial illustrations that abound through the body of the dictionary are <
invaluable assistance to the student, and the “Classified Selection of Pictorial Illustrations” given as a
appendix is the best of the kind that I have seen. I have had the work in constant use for seven
months, and willingly bear testimony to its excellence. It has proved more useful than I expectei
Ihe mechanical devices to facilitate reference are so good that the time and labor of looking u
information are reduced to a minimum. No one who buys this work will be dissatisfied with h
investment.
From the Hon. Sir Jorph P. Abbott, K.C.M.G.,
M.P., Speaker Legislative Assembly, Sydney :—
Speaker’s Room, Sydney,
29th November, 1895.
Webster’s Dictionary is like “good wine, which
needs no bush,” it needs no praise from myself or
anyone else. For 30 years it has weekly conferred
upon me obligations: each edition has been an
improvement upon the last, until now an edition
has been produced which, in my opinion, it is not
.possible to improve. If Samuel Johnson had
lived now and had the use of Webster’s Dictionary
he would not have written the following words :
Dictionaries are like watches; the worst is
better than none; and the best cannot be expected
to go quite true.” Webster has proved that a
dictionary can go quite true.
From the Hon. James N. Brunker, Chief
Secretary, N.S. W. :
Parliament House, Sydney,
30th May, 1895.
It is a source of gratification to me to hail the in-
troduction of Webster’s International Dictionary
to the colonies. It cannot be too highly estimated
as an important factor in the cause of education,
and 1 have every confidence in expressing the
opinion that the more widely such a valuable work
is used and circulated the more fully it will be
appreciated.

From Sir Gsorge R. Bibbs, K.C.M Sydney:-
TiWT k he mOSt per { e . ct and complete of
the kind that I have seen, and it is a perfect mine
public m h an and informatlon of & rea ‘ value to any
Prom Sir Henry Parkes, G.0.M.G.. Sydney:
It is the renewal of an old acquaintance which I
habitually consulted many years ago, but it comes
with a new and more comely face, fuller signs of
vigorous health, and far greater fitness to be of
service to the English-speaking race. It seems to
me that for accuracy, comprehensiveness, port-
ability, and convenience of reference, it is a noble
dictionary. It is within its own covers a great
store of philological interest and learning for the
student.
Prom George Colquhoun, Esq,, Crown Solicitor,
Ne w South Wales :
It is almost impossible to write in words of
sufficient praise touching your latest edition of
that magnificent dictionary the “ Webster Inter-
national.” From the time of Johnson up to the
present day many dictionaries have been published,
all valuable in themselves ; but, to meet the grow-
ing requirements of the times, to keep pace with
the marvellous progress in science, literature, and
art of this nineteenth century, I know of no better
dictionary than the “ Webster International.” It
is the dictionary of the day. It requires no testi-
monial, for it is a testimonial to itself. It is terse,
accurate, comprehensive, and practical. Infor-
mation sparkles in every page. It is a luminous
and most valuable production, and should find a
place in every school, college, and library as a
standard authority.
Prom M. W. MacCallnm, M.A., Professor
Modem Literature, University of Sydney :
The new edition of Webster’s International
Dictionary is in my opinion one of the best and
most useful books of its kind. It covers a wide
ground, and in all the departments in regard to
which I am competent to speak shows evidence
of sound and thorough work.
Prom Professor Archibald Liversidge, Sydney
University :
I have examined Webster’s International Dic-
tionary carefully. It appears to be very accurate
and complete, even in purely scientific terms ; and
I have no doubt that those relating to other
departments of learning are equally well edited.
Like all the larger modem dictionaries, it is some- -
what encyclopaedic in character, and it contains a
very large amount of useful information for every-
day purposes.

SECRETS OF NATURE
REVEALED.
Letter from a N.S.W. backblocks black-
smith’s shop:—
A dozen chaps are ready, waiting to jump my (digging)
job. As jobs go, it’s not a bad one. Hours, from jackass
to jackass ; and on Sunday (while the boss and bossess are
gone to church) I burn and cart enough charcoal for the
week. I get paid every Monday morning regularly, and
the boss tells me that he prays for me every Sunday. It
is all in the 8 bib a week.
At Malonga, a scrubby western station,
there is a well with a history. The late owner,
who lived at Lake George, got the news : “ 100 ft.
and no water.” He said “.Sink on.” 200 ft.
“Sink on.” At 800 ft. he got vexed with the
question. “ Tell that infernal well-sinker to sink
on until he hears the cocks crowing in hell or
until he gets water.” The man was poisoned by
“fowl” air in the shaft, and perhaps fulfilled his
instructions.
In slow-going Westralia ibis reckoned that
the fastest and most reliable messenger is the
bicycle-rider. The railway comes next, then the
steamboat, then the swagman, and last of all the
telegraph. One tale ooncerns a man who started
out for a place 100 miles distant with a mob of
sheep, and sent a telegram ahead to announce his
departure. He arrived two days before the
message. Westralia is probably the only place on
earth where a sheep can beat a telegram badly on
a 100-mile race over bad country. In that cob-
webbed land even the electric fluid goes
crutches.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
33 of this issue.
When The Bulletin quoted Bishop Julius
(M.L.) it, of course, had to leave out the naughty
little twinkle in his eye (remarks a correspon-
dent) —
I never saw so much bad coin in all my life. To offer to
the Church—the cause of God—money that the baker or
the butcher would not accept—shocks me. The man who
would do that sort Of thing wants the grace of God—or
three months.
The twinkle came with those last three words.
Julius is the opposite of a humorless person, and
the unco guid of Ballarat, therefore, held him in
much disrepute while he was there as Archdeacon.
He shocked a lot of Christians when he said they
were afraid the evolution of religion would lose
them their dear old Devil. Julius tricycled,
yachted, &0., and was a great smoker. He was
giving a magic-lantern entertainment at the dole-
ful Sebastopol one night, but had to beg his audi-
ence for a match —“I smoked all mine on the
road here.” The usual pious grocer passed up a
match, with a short sermon on the evils of tobacco.
“There’s smoke enough in hell, Archdeacon,
without having it here on earth.” At the which
Julius laughed consumedly. He once, excused a
man who was snoring through the sermon by
telling how his little son one night couldn’t get to
sleep. “Preach me a sermon, father—it will put
me to sleep like it does in church.” The Bishop
is no anaemic snnffler—he is a healthy, active,
sane man, with a sense of humor.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Ex-jockey “Johnny” Riley, of the mar-
vellous memory, assistant N.S. W. Parliamentary
Librarian, has made many a big Parliamentary
reputation. Here is a scene, not fictitious :
Enter distinguished politician. “I say, Riley, I
want to speak on the So-and-So question. Have
you got any books on it?” Riley (without hesita-
tion) : “Oh, yes. There was a debate on it, let
me see, yes, in the Commons, in ’52. We’ll find
that in volume so-and-so of so-and-so, page so-
and-so. Then there’s so-and-so and so-and-so.”
Goes out, and an hour later the distinguished
politician has a whole desk full of authorities,
debates, references, precedents, &c., to work from,
all marked at the proper page. A quarter of an
hour after, the D.P. will be holding forth to an
admiring House, an admiring press and ladies’
gallery, and an admiring country in an erudite
speech, backed up with references betraying the
most elaborate research.


The Melb. Benevolent Asylum is a seminary
for boys over 60 and girls over 50. Nevertheless,
its newspaper-boxes at the railway stations are
carefu ly sifted, to see there are no demoralising
newspapers, especially illustrated.
The most ridiculous figure in Melb. is a
well-known lanky broker, who cycles in a very long
Beaufort coat, the tails of which droop gracefully
over the hind wheel of the jigger and act as an
effective brake and mud-guard.

Australia .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dark rose her shore in seas of amethyst
By tropic breezes kissed,
A summer land in watery wastes forlorn,
Her ranges floating in the snow-white mist
And gold of early morn.
The tides of Empire ebbed and flowed afar ;
The thrones of nations in the dust were hurled,
Silent she slept beneath the morning star,
A virgin world.
Love, Birth, and Death, the stress of Age and
Race,
Changed not her maiden face—
Unstocked her pastures and untilled her soil—
She who for Labor builds a throne apace
Saw not her children toil;
Down the low valleys, up the stormy steeps,
Careless, they roamed at will: the land was free
From desert stark to where the mangrove sleeps
Upon the sea.
There dropped no anchor at her river bars
Beneath the quiet stars ;
No wandering sail her silent waters swept,
By waste and scrub, o’er plain and rocky soars
No alien footstep crept;
In feathery billows of her grassy seas
Some lonely mountain stretched its capes of blue ;
Only the heavens above her and the breeze
Her secrets knew.
The ploughshare and the axe’s ringing stroke
That giant slumber broke.
She learnt the serfdom of a stronger sway,
She felt the Dawn stir in her veins and woke
To greet the larger day.
Her mighty solitudes and mountain chains
Flung wide their portals to the stranger’s tread ;
His flocks in thousands roamed her sun-lit plains—
The Past was dead.
Whei’e the wild grass grew rank on slopes forlorn
Rise fields of yellow corn
And purple lucerne-bloom makes sweet the air ;
The sullen mountain, lost in mists of morn,
Its golden heart lays bare.
Spoils of her pastures crowd full many a mart;
Her glittering treasure calls to many a land ;
She has no secrets for the daring heart,
The strong brown hand.
The smoke and thunder of her cities rise
To the same careless skies ;
Her arteries pierce the same wide sun-lit leas,
Her fleets stretch forth their wings of enterprise
O’er the same summer seas.
She to the Nations cries : “No Past, no Fame,
No Memories quicken round my flag unfurled ;
The mightier, therefore, shall I carve my name
Upon the World.”
Q.
GEO. ESSEX EVANS.
At Market wharf, Sydney, is a pet sheep
formerly used in decoying sheep on board
steamers. This animal has been taught some bad
habits by the sailors. He chews a quid with the
worst of them, and, when his coppers are hot,
presses to the opposite pub. and quenches his
thirst from the drippings of the beer taps. After
a good drink he often finds the road not wide
enough, and frequently has to be carried to his
den.
Vic. Conservator of Forests Perrin has
doubts about the old records of tall trees in Aus-
tralia. Boyle, for example, 35 years ago found,
and W. J. Caire photo’d, a eucalypt asserted to
be 470 ft. high, and named it “The Baron,” after
Botanist Von Mueller. Four years ago Perrin
had it measured exactly by a surveyor with a
theodolite, and found it only 210 ft. high, or less
than half what was alleged. Another common
yarn tells of a fallen gum on Mount Baw Baw
(hymned by Sladen) 480 ft. long as it lay, and 2ft.
through where the top had been broken off. Von
Mueller guessed the original height of this asserted
find to be over 520 ft. Perrin classes this story as
first cousin to fish yarns. He knows of trees up
to 300 ft. in several parts of Victoria and Tas-
mania, and taped a fallen blue-gum on the Huon
River (Tas.) as 330 ft. That’s the biggest un-
doubted record he knows.
Dear Bulletin, —Re alleged discovery of
aboriginal cave-paintings—or imprints—of human
hands at Woolloomooloo Bay. I see that local
authorities attribute them to an earlier race than
the present Australian blackfellows. There does
not appear to be much reason for this theory.
When i was living on a branch of the Hawkes-
bury, some years ago, I was shown a cave the
walls of which were stamped all over with dull,
red imprints of human hands, evidently similar to
those found in the Woolloomooloo cave, A very
old resident informed me that his father had told
him that the blacks, who came periodically to the
place to eat shell-fish, used to light a fire out-
side the cave, and, after killing an animal of
some sort, dip their hands in its blood, and then
in the ashes of the fire, and leave the marks of
them on the walls of the cave. He could not find
out, however, whether this curious ceremony had
any tribal or religious significance.—Crbkve Rob.
The late strike at Bindirnine reminds me,
writes a Bulletin correspondent, that a few years
ago, when strikes were looked upon as a sort of
last fearful resource calculated to bring famine
and rapine on the land, there was trouble at the
same shed. The tact and general popularity of
the manager, Mr. Jtaterson (father of “Banjo,”
by the way), smoothed over the difficulty to the
immense relief of the neighborhood. As showing
the manner of man he was, two young fellows up
that way got into trouble over the same girl—
“unbeknownst” to one another, of course. So
one went to Mr. Paterson and explained. His
governor, he remarked, was one of the old stamp
of moralists, and he was in a fix for money to
square it. “How much does she want?” “Fifty
pounds.” “Well, it’s worth that to keep your
name out of it. Here you are —pay it back when
you can.” A few days afterwards the other fel-
low confided. His trouble was valued at .£3O.
Somehow it came out that the girl was identical.
Mr. Paterson interviewed her on behalf of one
and impressed a friend to represent the other.
Later they waited on her at the same time and
compared notes. A few legal threats and she
caved in. “ .Now, my girl, hc-re’s .£2O to get away
with, but, look here—tell us who it was ? ” “Oh
I can’t tell you ! ” “ Why ?” “ Because I don’t
know ! ”
From Hergott Springs to Oodnadatta, the
present terminus of S.A.’s unfinished transcon-
tinental railway, the train —and a lamentable
“mixed” train at that—now runs once a fort-
night. It used to run once a week, but this un-
justifiable extravagance had to be knocked off.
The journey is one of 300 miles across a lonesome
waste, and at the northern end of it there is a
township of 136 people and 130 dogs. At the
southern end of this famous extension is a town of
160 people and 165 dogs. There are 15 stopping
places in the 300 miles of line, and most of them
are named Something-or-other Creek, because
there is nothing in sight except a creek; the popu-
lation of the largest settlement of the lot amounts
to 10 adults and 16 dogs. The line stops at
Oodnadatta because there is very little behind
and nothing at all in front. The only way yet
suggested to make it pay is to sentence all Adelaide
offenders, in addition to the usual fine, to make
the journey to Oodnadatta and back, and thus
establish a forced traffic. When Federation
arrives, however, that lonesome town will doubt-
less be made the Federal capital, on the ground
that it wants something done for it and its 130
dogs worse than any other town in Australia.
As a place to be not less than 600 miles away from,
Oodnadatta has no equal.
Dear Bulletin, —“ F.,” who writes re
barter in Australian bush townships, has evi-
dently “ been there.” Some years ago I was on
the staff of a Q. country rag. We compositors
seldom saw any money. Saturday nights the
boss (who wore the same paget-coat all the time
I was with him—four years) would come up to us
after “ dis.” was in, and in the same old apologetic
style break the news (?) of his impecuniosity by
remarking that he was very sorry, &c., &c., but
if we cared he would give us an order for a few
tilings “to see us over Sunday ” on Smith, the
storekeeper, who ran a quarterly advt. with him.
He would square-up on Monday. That Monday
never came. Whenever a theatrical or circus-co.
arrived in town a consultation took place among
us three “inside” as to who should collect the
account. We generally tossed up, though Bill,
who was married, got the benefit of the doubt, as
a rule. We rarely troubled the boss in such
matters. I once got measured for a pair of boots,
for which, when finished, I gave, by arrangement,
an order for the amount on my employer in pay-
ment, This was held, perforce, by the tradesman
until the end of the quarter, when accounts were
squared “ per contra.” On the departure of a
liquidating subscriber the stampede from the
composing-room to the editor’s sanctum was of an
exciting character, and even after an apportion-
ment of the cash on hand doubts were oft-times ill
concealed as to the veracity of the “boss” in
connection with his statement of the amount re-
ceived. In such Micawber-like fashion matters
proceeded until old ’s inability to part for a
bale of paper eventually suspended the publica-
tion of this typical up-country organ of light.—
Lex.
A popular Tasmanian priest entered a
local emporium to make purchases. A group o#
lady customers stood chatting to the proprietor,
and a polite shopman ran to attend the Church.
The priest winked slyly at the shopman and
nodded with a show of humorous modesty towards
the ladies, as he surreptitiously pulled up the leg
of his trousers and showed the alert shopman a
white sock. Counter-jumper seized the situation
cocksuredly, and, after a hurried word with the
proprietor which somehow caused a solemn silence
among the ladies, he proceeded to ransack the
shelves. Eventually from an obscure shelf he
dragged a cardboard-box and proudly displayed
to the blushing and indignant priest an assort-
ment of “best ladies’ white.” As soon as his
reverence recovered his breath he hurriedly
shoved the lid on the box and pushed it towards
the man with—“ Tut, man, tut, take it away
quick ; is it shame ye’d be puttin’ on me ?”
Brumby's Run.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[The aboriginal term tor a wild horse is “ Brumby.” At
a recent trial in Sydney a Supreme Court Judge,
hearing of “ Brumby horses,” asked, “ Who i»
Brumby, and where is his run ?”]
It lies beyond the Western Pines
Towards the sinking sun,
And not a survey mark defines
The bounds of “ Brumby’s run.”
On odds and ends of mountain land
On tracks of range and rock,
Where no one else can make a stand,
Old Brumby rears his stock—
A wild, unhandled lot they are
Of every shape and breed,
They venture out ’neath moon and star
Along the flats to feed.
But when the dawn makes pink the sky
And steals along the plain,
The Brumby horses turn and fly
Towards the hills again.
The traveller by the mountain-track
May hear their hoof-beats pass,
And catch a glimpse of brown and black,
Dim shadows on the grass.
The eager stock horse pricks his ears
And lifts his head on high
In wild excitement when he hears
The Brumby mob go by.
Old Brumby asks no price or fee
O’er all his wide domains :
The man who yards his stock is free
To keep them for his pains.
So, off to scour the mountain-side
With eager eyes aglow,
To strongholds where the wild mobs hide
The gully-rakers go.
A rush of horses through the trees,
A red shirt making play ;
A sound of stockwhips on the breeze,
They vanish far away !
Ah, me ! before our day is done
We long with bitter pain
To ride once more on Brumby’s run
And yard his mob again.
THE BANJO.
Vie. Parliament is getting on with a bill
to provide for the establishment of crematoriums
at cemeteries, so that the dead may be combusted
decently and in order. There are 230,000 dead in
the three Melbourne cemeteries, and as many as
22 coffins in one grave, which is about the strongest
argument in favor of cremation that could be
advanced. Think of it : a city of 450,000 living
and 250,000 dead, and only a few of them dead
long enough to be past.the putrefactive stage !
