In Flood On A NORTHERN RIVER FOR THE BULLETIN.
roar 1 A swirling,, thundering, tumultuous
churning of rushing water. And rain ! How it
rained, not in drops, but walls of water, slanting
slopes of liquid, a vast cataract of sky-oceans rush-
ing down with hisses, splutters, murmurs and loud
groans, and beating on the iron roof of Captain
Saunders’ house like endless peals of musketry.
And, above the noise of the storm, the mighty
roar of the river, the howling, bellowing, menacing
rumble of the flood as it swept down the channel
and rose, inch by inch, then foot by foot, over the
flats.
It was night, and to the Captain, peering through
the window, nothing was descernible in the rain-
blackness, but the glisten of white foam when the
torrent formed a circling eddy.
“ Lucky, ” he said to him-
self, “ that I drove the stock
to the hills this morning.”
Though he spoke aloud
the batter on the roof
smothered his voice. Even
as he walked the floor he
knew the children were
awake and crying in the
next room, for he had
looked in and seen them,
but he heard them not. His
wife came to his side for a
moment and screamed in his
ear:
“Do you think the house
is safe, Alec ?”
“The house is all right.
The flood never came this
high, but the luoern’s ruined
—no crop this year, and
God knows what that means
to us.”
The wife nodded, half-
hearing, half - guessing his
meaning, and returned to
lie down by the children.
Hourspassed. The wearied
children slept, the man dozed
on a lounge, the woman
only was awake.
It seemed to her, turning
her eyes from her babe,
that the floor was sliming.
She put down her feet from
th e bed they touched water!
With a cry of terror, she
rushed to her husband,
splashing as she went, and
shook him.
“Alec! Alec! We must
leave the house. The flood
is rising ! ”
The captain rose quickly
and, for a few minutes,
waded frantically up and
down the room; but he
shook his head at the pro-
posal to escape.
“ Remember, the house is
on piles. There must be four
feet of water all round us.”
Even as be spoke the water
had risen to their knees.
Then it went over the chairs,
the tables, so that they has-
tened to remove the ch Finn
and themselves to the loft
above.
And still the w.wiir rose. Cooped there, the
sloping iron overhead, the rising water beneath,
they were trapped. A sheet of iron must be
raised, but how do it without tools ? The captain
looked about in despair. At last he remembered
the bed-head, and, stooping down, unscrewed the
top bar. With this as a lever he broke the bat-
tens, broke the bar itself, and at last, half-tearing,
half-bending, the corrugated sheets, a hole was
made in the roof.
It was time. Already the water was ankle-deep
in the loft. He climbed out on to the ridge, and
one by one the children are dragged up beside
him. Last came the wife.
Then the house rocked.
“My God !” cried the captain, “she has lifted
off the blocks. ”
Feeling, but not seeing, each other, huddled,
clinging together on the cold ridge-capping, they
felt the house moving and turning round and
round.
They could see the shimmer of shining water all
around and dark masses looming up and floating
by. Perhaps other dwellings, sheds, stacks, trees
or what not. Fortunately, the house floated and
kept upright, but it was drifting rapidly and more
rapidly.
The man groaned.
It might have been an hour they clung there,
helpless, numb, despairing, when a huge manogany,
rolling and tossing in the stream, struck the house
on one angle. The building healed over broad-
side, shook, trembled, then rising again like a boat
after a squall, continued its course.
The captain, holding on to the stove-pipe, had
gone right under water ; now in the darkness he
felt about for the wife and children that had been
clinging to him. Slowly he crept along, groping
in the darkness like a blind man. The house
swayed and rocked in the curftnt, the deluge of
rain beat on him, but he heeded it not. Foot by
foot he progressed, calling out “ Mother! mother !
are you there ? ” The sound of his voice was lost
in the storm. More slowly he crept, fearing to
know the truth, fearing to come to the end of the
house and the end of the hopes.
“ Mother 1 mother 1 are you there ? ”
He was on the extreme edge of the gable. No
one else was there, he was alone. But he would
not believe it—would not be convinced, but crawled
aimlessly to and fro on the rocking house-top,
clinging with one hand and stretching out the
other into darkness, space and rain, as though
those he sought had dodged him like players at
blind man’s bluff.
In the grey of dawn, 10 miles down the river,
the rescue-boat, pilot Gibson steering, pulled out
into the back water.
A boiling, churning, yellow current, whirling
round in eddies and thick with slime and silt, and
floating on its surface a show-ground of farm pro-
ducts, houses, sheds, ricks, dead cattle, horses,
pigs, and a vast litter of pumpkins, maize-stalks,
and melons.
The boat pulled through this silently ; even the
dead face of a woman, looking at them for a
moment as an eddy brought her to the surface,
did not arrest their steady pull. They came not
to save the dead, but the living.
In the distance, bearing down on them, they
could see a floating house-top, a man astride at
one end moving his hands with strange motions.
They called to him. There was no reply. They
shouted. He did not even turn, but with his eyes
set straight in front he bent from time to time his
head over a projecting stove-pipe, moving his
hands up and down, up and down like a steers-
man turning an invisible wheel. What was he
doing ? They pulled towards him.
“ Catch the line, Saunders !” cried Pilot Gibson,
as he stood up in the boat and threw the coil on
his arm, up and over the roof.
“ What do you take me for?” replied Saunders,
not turning his head. “Do you think I want a
pilot—l that have been on this river man and boy
for 10 years !” Then bending his mouth to the
stove-pipe and moving his hands vigorously—
“ Easy a-head !—stop her !”
“ Here, none of your shiannikin, skipper. Get
down in the boat. You are going out to sea,
man.”
“ I tell you, Gibson, I know the river as well as
you —as well as any man on the coast. I’ve got
the tide, and I mean to be home to-night; the
wife’s waiting for me.”
Again the speaker moved the invisible wheel
with his hands; again he bent to the stove-pipe.
“ Full speed ahead there ! Let her rip 1 ”
“ The skipper’s looney ; ho thinks he is on his
old steamer again,” said Gibson to the rowers.
“We must pul! him off if he won’t come.”
With difficulty they made fast to the house, and,
aided by a boat-hook, the pilot climbed the sloping
roof. But the captain dodged him.
“ I tell you I don’t want a pilot. I’ll navigate
this river with my eyes shut. I tell ”
Gibson made a grab, the captain a step back, a
slide, a splash and he was in the water. Saunders’
head rose to the surface as he swam.
“ I tell you, the wife is waiting for me. The
wife is waiting ”
They had not time to cry out, not time to warn
him, when a great snag, whirled about like a
straw in the current, bore down on the swimmer,
buried him in a rush of surge and foam, pressing
him down into yellow slime and undertow. And
as they looked, the man, the snag, all had vanished
in the mighty rush of waters onward to the sea.
HENRY FLETCHER.
Ed. Bulletin.— The era of Scotch Gover-
nors in Australia has not quite gone : Lord
Lamington, who comes to Bananaland, claims
connection with William Wallace—and with at
least as much right as Brand does with John
Hampden, Brassey with the Sieur de Bressi, and
Buxton with (say) Beer-Sheba. Lamington is in
a bleak part of Lanarkshire—the parish church
was described by Burns in these lines :
As oauld a wind as ever blew ;
A caulder kirk, and in’t but few ;
As cauld a minister ne’er spak;
Ye’se w’ be bet ere I come back.
So the Cochrane-Baillies have a private Episcopal
chapel of their own, with chaplain and all com-
plete.— A. B.
One breezy afternoon Gov. Loftus, while
cruising about Sydney harbor in a launch, saw a
canvas-dingey turn turtle, after the manner of its
kind, and told his coxswain to proceed to the
rescue. On arriving within hail, the crew (crews
at that time in these craft were limited to youths
under 16) greeted the rapidly-approaching launch
with objurgatory injunctions to “sheer off,” fear-
ing damage to their slender spars and dainty
gear. They were as much at home (and very little
wetter) outside as inside their cockleshell, and
preferred to await the services of a passing pulling
skiff. However, they graciously permitted their
would-be rescuers to collect the bottom boards,
etc., which were floating around. While taking
delivery of the salvaged “flotsam” one of the
young amphibians inquired of the coxswain of the
launch in a very audible sotto voce “ Who that
—— old was sitting in the 4 starn ?’ ” On
being informed as to the identity of the august
personage thus libellously indicated, the irreve-
rent youth expressed his incredulity in scarcely
less complimentary terms. It is characteristic of
the old diplomat that his only comment on the
occurrence, delivered when the capsized craft had
been left far astern, was “ O’ Keefe ! how very
abrupt these Australian lads are ! ” But the
Governor never attempted to rescue any more
canvas-dingey boys.

have not got, write down to Hordern Bros. (Pitt-
street, Sydney) for it. We serve people in every
town in N.S.W.

Ihe public eye rejects as uninteresting* more
than half of what appears in the newspapers o
the day. It is only the remainder that is fount
in The Bulletin.
Ihe Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti
cally-condensed columns all matter that is nql
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains
ami has become the representative Australia!
journal, because it possesses a vitality lackec
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spenl
arge fortunes.
Ihe Bulletin's conductors claim that every
prog tussive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to protit by ir. s example
and follow its lines. The Jesuit of its powerful
innuenee and its unprecedented bteniry and com-
meici.il siu'pi'ss c.xu <it ohog In* seen hy cjoinprirliip
the mel hods pm sued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the (late of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name, they are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the om* completely non-
local and distinctly uni Australasi.in news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class orbiting
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical, it points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
Ihe Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth
power, and privilege—the direct cause of hair the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the churns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, avainst thedark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the. ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared hy its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quot* d in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Ear North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than and
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
